Redefining 'Need' as Inseparability, Not Causation
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes two meanings of 'need' in the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between causal need (iftiqar sababi) and necessary inseparability (talazum) and apply this distinction to the God-attributes relationship.

Core Idea
The 'need' between God and His attributes is mutual inseparability, not causal dependence.

Ibn Taymiyya accepts that if 'need' means causal dependence on an efficient cause, then a composite would indeed be contingent. But he denies that the relationship between God's essence and attributes is causal. Instead, he redefines 'need' as mere inseparability (talazum). In this lesson we will trace his analysis of the premise 'Every composite is in need of its part' and see how he tests two interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Extract the logicians' premise
The logicians say 'Every composite is in need of its part.' The part here could be the attribute or the essence. Ibn Taymiyya tests two meanings of 'need'.
2
First interpretation: causal need
If 'need' means the need of an effect for its efficient, final, material, or formal cause, then the premise is false. A part (like one of ten or a part of a whole) cannot be an efficient cause or a final cause of the whole.
Ibn Taymiyya gives examples: the one among ten, the part of the whole.
3
Second interpretation: inseparability (talazum)
If 'need' means that the whole entails its part and cannot exist without it, then the premise is true. But this is merely logical entailment, not causal dependence.
This is the interpretation Ibn Taymiyya accepts for the God-attributes relationship.
4
Apply to divine attributes
God's essence entails His attributes and vice versa; they are mutually inseparable. This does not make God contingent. The 'need' is just talazum, not causally dependent on an external agent.
Thus the logicians' conclusion that God would not be necessary by Himself does not follow.

المقام الثالث - دليل نفاة الصفات والرد عليه
إلى أجزائه، وجزؤه غيره . فـ «المركب مفتقر إلى غيره، والمفتقر إلى غيره ليس واجباً بنفسه .
فيقال : أجزاء هذا الدليل وألفاظه التي تسمونها حدوداً» كلها ألفاظ مجملة تحتمل حقاً وباطلاً واستعمال الألفاظ المجملة في الحدود» و «القياس» من باب السفسطة .
فيقال لكم: قد عُرف أن لفظ «المركب مجمل، وأن المراد به هنا «ذات تقوم بها الافتقار هو صفات». وحينئذ فالمراد بـ «الافتقار» تلازم الذات والصفات» بمعنى أنه لا تلازم الذات توجد الذات إلا مع وجود صفتها المُلازمة لها، ولا توجد الصفة إلا مع وجود الذات الملازمة لها. ولو قدر أنه أريد بـ «التركيب التركيب من الأجزاء الحسية أو العقلية مع تلازم الأجزاء، فهذا معناه .
فإذا قيل : كل مركب مفتقر إلى جزئه، إن عُني به أنه مستلزم لجزئه، وأنه لا وجود يوجد إلا بوجود جزئه، فهذا صحيح. فإن وجود المجموع بدون كلّ من آحاده ممتنع . وإن أريد أنه مفتقر إليه افتقار المفعول إلى فاعله والمعلول إلى علّته الفاعلة، أو إلى القابلة، أو الغائية أو الصُّورية فهذا باطل فإن الواحد من العشرة، والجزء من الجملة : لا يجوز أن يكون فاعلاً، ولا غاية، ولا محلاً لغيره، ولا هو الصورة .
ثم قولكم وجزؤه غيره» ، يقال : لفظ «الغير» يراد به ما كان مبايناً للشيء، وما يطلق عليه ما يجوز مُفارقته له وما ليس إيَّاه. فإن أردتم أنَّ جزء المجموع ما هو مباين له. باطل، فإنه يمتنع أن يكون مبايناً له مع كونه جزءاً منه فيمتنع أن يكون «غيراً» له بهذا الاعتبار. وإن قلتم : يجوز أن يفارقه، فهذا ليس بمسلَّم على الإطلاق، بل يجوز في بعض الأفراد أن يفارق غيره من الأجزاء، ويفارق المجموع الذي هو الهيئة الاجتماعية . ولا يلزم ذلك في كل مجموع ، لا سيما على أصلهم . فإن الفلك» عندهم «مركّب» من أجزائه وصفاته، ولا يجوز عندهم على أجزائه التفرقُ .
والمسلمون وجمهور العقلاء أن الله حي، عليم، قدير؛ ولا يجوز أن يكون صفات الله مفارقه كونه حيّاً عالماً قادراً؛ بل لم يزل ولا يزال كذلك. وكونه حياً، عالماً، قادراً، لوازم ذاته من لوازم ذاته، وهي ملازمة لذاته، لا يجوز عليها الافتراقُ بوجه من الوجوه. فامتنع أن تكون صفاته هذه «أغياراً» بهذا الاعتبار.
وإن فُسّر الغَيْرانِ بما ليس أحدهما هو الآخر، أو بما يجوز العلم بأحدهما مع عدم العلم بالآخر ؛ فلا ريب أن صفة الموصوف التي يمكن معرفتها «غير» بالموصوف له بهذا الاعتبار. لكن إذا كانت تلك الصفة لازمة له وهو لازم لها، لم يكن في ذلك ما يكون أحدهما مفتقراً إلى الآخر ، مفعولاً للآخر، ولا علة فاعلة، ولا غائية، ولا صُورية. أكثر ما في ذلك أن تكون الصفة مفتقرة إلى الذات افتقار الحال إلى محله القابل له . وهم يسمون القابل علة قابلة لكن فيما يحدث لها من المقبولات، لا فيما يكون لازماً لها أزلاً وأبداً. وإن قدر أنهم يسمون جميع ذلك «علة» و «معلولاً»، فتكون الذات علة قابلة للصفة بهذا الاعتبار. وكون الصفة «معلولة) هو معنى كونها صفة قائمة بالموصوف .
هذا فليس مجموع الذات والصفة مفتقراً إلى شيء من أنواع العلل الأربع، لا افتقار الحال إلى الفاعل ولا إلى الغاية ولا إلى القابل، ولا إلى الصورة فبطل أن يقال : المجموع مفتقر إلى جزئه افتقار المعلول إلى علته بوجه من الوجوه، لكن غايته أن فيه افتقار الصفة إلى الموصوف افتقار الحال اللازم لمحله إلى محله المستلزم له. فيقال لهم : وأي شيء في كونه موجوداً بنفسه لا فاعِل له ما يوجب نفي هذا التلازم الذي سموه «افتقاراً» نحو ذاته وصفاته؟
وقولهم : : «ما افتقر إلى غيره لم يكن واجباً بنفسه، يقال لهم : قد علم أن المراد بـ «الافتقار ) : التلازم ؛ والمراد بـ «الغير»: ما هو داخل في المجموع، إما الذات وإما الصفات، ليس المراد به ما هو مباين له، وما يجوز مُفارقته له وغايته أن يراد أن الصفة لا بد لها من الموصوف . فليس المراد افتقارُ المعلول إلى علته الفاعلة. وحينئذ فليس في هذا التلازم الذي سميتموه «افتقاراً» ولا في هذه الصفات التي سميتموها «أغياراً» ما يوجب أن يكون شيء من ذلك مفعولاً لفاعل ولا لعلة فاعلة
Third Station - The proof of the negators of attributes and the refutation of it.
... to its parts, and its part is other than it. So 'The composite is in need of something else, and that which is in need of something else is not necessary by itself.'
So it is said: The parts of this proof and its expressions which you call 'definitions' are all ambiguous expressions that bear truth and falsehood, and using ambiguous expressions in definitions and syllogism is a kind of sophistry.
So it is said to you: It is known that the term 'composite' is ambiguous, and what is meant by it here is 'an essence to which need pertains, which is attributes.' And thus what is meant by 'need' is the inseparability of essence and attributes, meaning that the essence does not exist except with the existence of its inseparable attribute, and the attribute does not exist except with the existence of its inseparable essence. And if it were assumed that by 'composition' is meant composition from sensory or intellectual parts with the inseparability of parts, then this is its meaning.
So if it is said: Every composite is in need of its part, if what is meant is that it entails its part, and that it has no existence except with the existence of its part, then this is correct. For the existence of the whole without each of its individuals is impossible. But if what is meant is that it is in need of it with the need of the effect for its agent, and the effect for its efficient cause, or for the receptive [cause], or the final [cause], or the formal [cause], then this is false, for the one among ten and the part of the whole cannot be an agent, nor a final cause, nor a locus for something else, nor is it the form.
Then your statement 'and its part is other than it' – it is said: The term 'other' refers to what is separate from the thing, and what can be separated from it, and what is not identical to it. If you mean that the part of the whole is separate from it, that is false, for it is impossible for it to be separate from it while being a part of it; thus it is impossible for it to be 'other' to it in this sense. And if you say: It can be separated from it, then this is not granted absolutely, but it is permissible in some individuals that it separates from other parts, and separates from the whole which is the social form. And that does not necessarily apply to every composite, especially according to their own principle. For the celestial sphere according to them is 'composite' from its parts and attributes, and it is not permissible according to them for its parts to disperse.
And the Muslims and the majority of rational people [hold] that God is living, knowing, powerful; and it is not permissible that God's attributes separate from His being living, knowing, powerful; rather He has never ceased and will never cease to be so. And His being living, knowing, powerful are necessary concomitants of His essence, and they are inseparable from His essence, and separation is not permissible upon them in any way. Thus it is impossible for these attributes to be 'others' in this sense.
And if 'the two others' is interpreted as that one of them is not the other, or as that it is permissible to know one without knowledge of the other, then there is no doubt that the attribute of the described that can be known is 'other' than the described in this sense. But when that attribute is necessary for him and he is necessary for it, there is not in that what makes one of them in need of the other, an effect of the other, nor an efficient cause, nor a final cause, nor a formal cause. The most there is in that is that the attribute is in need of the essence with the need of a state for its receptive locus. And they call the receptive cause a material cause, but in what occurs to it of received things, not in what is necessary for it eternally and perpetually. And if it is assumed that they call all that 'cause' and 'effect', then the essence would be a receptive cause for the attribute in this sense. And the attribute being an 'effect' is the meaning of its being an attribute subsisting in the described.
So this: the whole of essence and attribute is not in need of any of the four types of causes, neither the need of the state for the agent, nor for the final cause, nor for the receptive cause, nor for the formal cause. So it is invalid to say: The whole is in need of its part with the need of the effect for its cause in any way, but its utmost is that there is in it the need of the attribute for the described, the need of the necessary state for its locus that entails it. So it is said to them: What is there in His being existent by Himself with no agent for Him that necessitates negating this inseparability which they called 'need' regarding His essence and attributes?
And their statement: 'What is in need of something else is not necessary by itself' – it is said to them: It is known that what is meant by 'need' is inseparability; and what is meant by 'other' is what is included in the whole, either the essence or the attributes, not what is separate from it and what can be separated from it. And its utmost is that what is intended is that the attribute cannot do without the described. So what is meant is not the need of the effect for its efficient cause. And then there is not in this inseparability which you have called 'need' nor in these attributes which you have called 'others' what necessitates that any of that be an effect of an agent nor of an efficient cause.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of a standard logical proof used by those who deny that God has real attributes (the 'negators of attributes'). The proof states: 'Every composite is in need of something else, and that which is in need of something else is not necessary by itself. Therefore, if God had attributes, He would be composite and thus not necessary by Himself.' Ibn Taymiyya argues that this proof rests on equivocal terms, and thus is sophistical.

He begins by identifying the key terms in the argument: 'composite' (murakkab), 'need' (iftiqar), and 'other' (ghayr). He claims each is ambiguous (mujmal), capable of bearing both true and false meanings. The logicians' use of such terms in definitions and syllogisms, he says, is sophistry (safsata).

He then clarifies what the proof should mean if it is to be valid. The intended meaning of 'composite' here is not the usual sense of parts externally assembled, but rather 'an essence to which need pertains, which is attributes'. In other words, the 'composition' is between the essence (dhat) and its attributes (sifat). Correspondingly, 'need' here does not mean dependence on a separate external cause; it means the inseparability (talazum) of essence and attribute: the essence cannot exist without its attribute, and the attribute cannot exist without its essence. This is a mutual entailment, not a causal dependence.

Ibn Taymiyya then systematically examines the premises. Considering the statement 'Every composite is in need of its part', he distinguishes two possible interpretations. If it means the composite entails its part and cannot exist without it, then it is true—the whole indeed cannot exist without its parts. But if it means the composite depends on its part as an effect depends on its efficient, final, formal, or material cause, then it is false. A part of a whole cannot be an efficient cause, nor a final cause, nor a locus, nor a form. Thus the crucial sense of 'need' in the refuted proof is invalid.

He then turns to 'its part is other than it'. The term 'other' can mean 'separate' (mubayin), i.e., distinct and capable of separation. He argues that a part cannot be separate from the whole it constitutes, so it is not 'other' in that sense. Alternatively, if they mean it can be separated, that is not universally true; in some composites separation is impossible, such as the celestial spheres (according to the philosophers themselves) and especially in God's attributes, which are eternal and inseparable. Muslims hold that God is eternally living, knowing, powerful, and these attributes are necessary concomitants (lawazim) of His essence; they cannot separate. Thus they are not 'others' in the sense of being separable.

There is another valid interpretation of 'other': two things are 'other' if one is not identical to the other, or if knowledge of one does not entail knowledge of the other. In this sense, an attribute is indeed 'other' than the essence. But this kind of 'otherness' does not imply dependence of the efficient-cause type. The attribute's only 'need' is that it subsists in the essence as a state (hal) in its locus (mahall), but this is a necessary inseparability, not a causal relation. Even if one calls this 'cause and effect' (the essence being a receptive cause), that does not entail that the composite (essence+attributes) is in need of an external agent.

Finally, Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the composite of essence and attributes is not in need of any of the four causes (efficient, final, material, formal). The utmost relationship is the attribute's need for the essence as a necessary state for its locus. This does not conflict with God being self-sufficient (wajib al-wujud bi-dhatihi). The logicians' argument fails because they equivocate on 'need' and 'other'. The true meaning of 'need' here is simply inseparability, and 'other' means a part of the whole, not something external. Thus nothing in this entails that God is not necessary by Himself.

Key Terms
الافتقار (al-iftiqar): In this context, 'need' means mutual inseparability (talazum) between essence and attribute, not causal dependence on an external agent. [Note: Usually iftiqar means dependence on a cause; here it is redefined as mere entailment.]
التلازم (al-talazum): Mutual inseparability: the essence cannot exist without its attributes and vice versa. [Note: Talazum is a common logical term for co-implication; here it describes the relation between God and His attributes.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, which interpretation of 'need' (iftiqar) makes the premise 'Every composite is in need of its part' true?
A) Need as causal dependence on an efficient cause
B) Need as entailment (the whole cannot exist without the part)
C) Need as the effect's need for its final cause
D) Need as the attribute's need for the essence

Key Takeaway
The term 'need' must be disambiguated: only causal need implies contingency; entailment does not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'The Quran is in need of Allah for its existence.' Does 'need' here mean causal dependence or entailment? Explain using Ibn Taymiyya's distinction.
?
Construct an argument where the word 'need' is used equivocally, then show how Ibn Taymiyya would analyze it.

The Concept of 'Other' and Inseparable Attributes
Ibn Taymiyya deconstructs the term 'other' in the logicians' proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the term 'other' (ghayr) in arguments about divine attributes and determine whether it implies separability.

Core Idea
Attributes are not 'other' in the sense of being separable from God's essence.

The logicians argue that if God had attributes, His essence and attributes would be 'other' to each other, implying separability and need. Ibn Taymiyya examines the term 'other' (ghayr) and shows it has multiple meanings. He demonstrates that the attributes of God are not 'other' in the sense that would imply they could exist apart from His essence. In this lesson we will dissect his treatment of 'other' and see how he defends the inseparability of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim about 'other'
The logicians say: 'its part is other than it' (جزؤه غيره). They argue that if God had attributes, the attributes would be 'other' than His essence, and thus God would be composite and in need.
2
List the meanings of 'other' given by Ibn Taymiyya
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes three meanings: (1) something separate (mubayin) and capable of separation, (2) something not identical (ghayr in the sense of distinct), (3) something that can be known independently.
3
Test each meaning against divine attributes
For (1): attributes cannot be separate from God because they are inseparable. For (2): attributes are indeed distinct from the essence in the sense that they are not identical, but this does not imply separability. For (3): we can know God's attributes without fully knowing His essence, but that is a cognitive distinction, not a real separation.
Ibn Taymiyya focuses on meaning (1) as the crucial one.
4
Conclude that the logicians' use is equivocal
The logicians intend meaning (1) to prove need, but divine attributes are not 'other' in that sense. Thus their argument fails.
Ibn Taymiyya also mentions that even according to philosophers, parts of the celestial sphere are inseparable, so their own principles contradict the claim.

والمسلمون وجمهور العقلاء أن الله حي، عليم، قدير؛ ولا يجوز أن يكون صفات الله مفارقه كونه حيّاً عالماً قادراً؛ بل لم يزل ولا يزال كذلك. وكونه حياً، عالماً، قادراً، لوازم ذاته من لوازم ذاته، وهي ملازمة لذاته، لا يجوز عليها الافتراقُ بوجه من الوجوه. فامتنع أن تكون صفاته هذه «أغياراً» بهذا الاعتبار.
وإن فُسّر الغَيْرانِ بما ليس أحدهما هو الآخر، أو بما يجوز العلم بأحدهما مع عدم العلم بالآخر ؛ فلا ريب أن صفة الموصوف التي يمكن معرفتها «غير» بالموصوف له بهذا الاعتبار. لكن إذا كانت تلك الصفة لازمة له وهو لازم لها، لم يكن في ذلك ما يكون أحدهما مفتقراً إلى الآخر ، مفعولاً للآخر، ولا علة فاعلة، ولا غائية، ولا صُورية. أكثر ما في ذلك أن تكون الصفة مفتقرة إلى الذات افتقار الحال إلى محله القابل له . وهم يسمون القابل علة قابلة لكن فيما يحدث لها من المقبولات، لا فيما يكون لازماً لها أزلاً وأبداً. وإن قدر أنهم يسمون جميع ذلك «علة» و «معلولاً»، فتكون الذات علة قابلة للصفة بهذا الاعتبار. وكون الصفة «معلولة) هو معنى كونها صفة قائمة بالموصوف .
هذا فليس مجموع الذات والصفة مفتقراً إلى شيء من أنواع العلل الأربع، لا افتقار الحال إلى الفاعل ولا إلى الغاية ولا إلى القابل، ولا إلى الصورة فبطل أن يقال : المجموع مفتقر إلى جزئه افتقار المعلول إلى علته بوجه من الوجوه، لكن غايته أن فيه افتقار الصفة إلى الموصوف افتقار الحال اللازم لمحله إلى محله المستلزم له. فيقال لهم : وأي شيء في كونه موجوداً بنفسه لا فاعِل له ما يوجب نفي هذا التلازم الذي سموه «افتقاراً» نحو ذاته وصفاته؟
وقولهم : : «ما افتقر إلى غيره لم يكن واجباً بنفسه، يقال لهم : قد علم أن المراد بـ «الافتقار ) : التلازم ؛ والمراد بـ «الغير»: ما هو داخل في المجموع، إما الذات وإما الصفات، ليس المراد به ما هو مباين له، وما يجوز مُفارقته له وغايته أن يراد أن الصفة لا بد لها من الموصوف . فليس المراد افتقارُ المعلول إلى علته الفاعلة. وحينئذ فليس في هذا التلازم الذي سميتموه «افتقاراً» ولا في هذه الصفات التي سميتموها «أغياراً» ما يوجب أن يكون شيء من ذلك مفعولاً لفاعل ولا لعلة فاعلة
Third Station - The proof of the negators of attributes and the refutation of it.
... to its parts, and its part is other than it. So 'The composite is in need of something else, and that which is in need of something else is not necessary by itself.'
So it is said: The parts of this proof and its expressions which you call 'definitions' are all ambiguous expressions that bear truth and falsehood, and using ambiguous expressions in definitions and syllogism is a kind of sophistry.
So it is said to you: It is known that the term 'composite' is ambiguous, and what is meant by it here is 'an essence to which need pertains, which is attributes.' And thus what is meant by 'need' is the inseparability of essence and attributes, meaning that the essence does not exist except with the existence of its inseparable attribute, and the attribute does not exist except with the existence of its inseparable essence. And if it were assumed that by 'composition' is meant composition from sensory or intellectual parts with the inseparability of parts, then this is its meaning.
So if it is said: Every composite is in need of its part, if what is meant is that it entails its part, and that it has no existence except with the existence of its part, then this is correct. For the existence of the whole without each of its individuals is impossible. But if what is meant is that it is in need of it with the need of the effect for its agent, and the effect for its efficient cause, or for the receptive [cause], or the final [cause], or the formal [cause], then this is false, for the one among ten and the part of the whole cannot be an agent, nor a final cause, nor a locus for something else, nor is it the form.
Then your statement 'and its part is other than it' – it is said: The term 'other' refers to what is separate from the thing, and what can be separated from it, and what is not identical to it. If you mean that the part of the whole is separate from it, that is false, for it is impossible for it to be separate from it while being a part of it; thus it is impossible for it to be 'other' to it in this sense. And if you say: It can be separated from it, then this is not granted absolutely, but it is permissible in some individuals that it separates from other parts, and separates from the whole which is the social form. And that does not necessarily apply to every composite, especially according to their own principle. For the celestial sphere according to them is 'composite' from its parts and attributes, and it is not permissible according to them for its parts to disperse.
And the Muslims and the majority of rational people [hold] that God is living, knowing, powerful; and it is not permissible that God's attributes separate from His being living, knowing, powerful; rather He has never ceased and will never cease to be so. And His being living, knowing, powerful are necessary concomitants of His essence, and they are inseparable from His essence, and separation is not permissible upon them in any way. Thus it is impossible for these attributes to be 'others' in this sense.
And if 'the two others' is interpreted as that one of them is not the other, or as that it is permissible to know one without knowledge of the other, then there is no doubt that the attribute of the described that can be known is 'other' than the described in this sense. But when that attribute is necessary for him and he is necessary for it, there is not in that what makes one of them in need of the other, an effect of the other, nor an efficient cause, nor a final cause, nor a formal cause. The most there is in that is that the attribute is in need of the essence with the need of a state for its receptive locus. And they call the receptive cause a material cause, but in what occurs to it of received things, not in what is necessary for it eternally and perpetually. And if it is assumed that they call all that 'cause' and 'effect', then the essence would be a receptive cause for the attribute in this sense. And the attribute being an 'effect' is the meaning of its being an attribute subsisting in the described.
So this: the whole of essence and attribute is not in need of any of the four types of causes, neither the need of the state for the agent, nor for the final cause, nor for the receptive cause, nor for the formal cause. So it is invalid to say: The whole is in need of its part with the need of the effect for its cause in any way, but its utmost is that there is in it the need of the attribute for the described, the need of the necessary state for its locus that entails it. So it is said to them: What is there in His being existent by Himself with no agent for Him that necessitates negating this inseparability which they called 'need' regarding His essence and attributes?
And their statement: 'What is in need of something else is not necessary by itself' – it is said to them: It is known that what is meant by 'need' is inseparability; and what is meant by 'other' is what is included in the whole, either the essence or the attributes, not what is separate from it and what can be separated from it. And its utmost is that what is intended is that the attribute cannot do without the described. So what is meant is not the need of the effect for its efficient cause. And then there is not in this inseparability which you have called 'need' nor in these attributes which you have called 'others' what necessitates that any of that be an effect of an agent nor of an efficient cause.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of a standard logical proof used by those who deny that God has real attributes (the 'negators of attributes'). The proof states: 'Every composite is in need of something else, and that which is in need of something else is not necessary by itself. Therefore, if God had attributes, He would be composite and thus not necessary by Himself.' Ibn Taymiyya argues that this proof rests on equivocal terms, and thus is sophistical.

He begins by identifying the key terms in the argument: 'composite' (murakkab), 'need' (iftiqar), and 'other' (ghayr). He claims each is ambiguous (mujmal), capable of bearing both true and false meanings. The logicians' use of such terms in definitions and syllogisms, he says, is sophistry (safsata).

He then clarifies what the proof should mean if it is to be valid. The intended meaning of 'composite' here is not the usual sense of parts externally assembled, but rather 'an essence to which need pertains, which is attributes'. In other words, the 'composition' is between the essence (dhat) and its attributes (sifat). Correspondingly, 'need' here does not mean dependence on a separate external cause; it means the inseparability (talazum) of essence and attribute: the essence cannot exist without its attribute, and the attribute cannot exist without its essence. This is a mutual entailment, not a causal dependence.

Ibn Taymiyya then systematically examines the premises. Considering the statement 'Every composite is in need of its part', he distinguishes two possible interpretations. If it means the composite entails its part and cannot exist without it, then it is true—the whole indeed cannot exist without its parts. But if it means the composite depends on its part as an effect depends on its efficient, final, formal, or material cause, then it is false. A part of a whole cannot be an efficient cause, nor a final cause, nor a locus, nor a form. Thus the crucial sense of 'need' in the refuted proof is invalid.

He then turns to 'its part is other than it'. The term 'other' can mean 'separate' (mubayin), i.e., distinct and capable of separation. He argues that a part cannot be separate from the whole it constitutes, so it is not 'other' in that sense. Alternatively, if they mean it can be separated, that is not universally true; in some composites separation is impossible, such as the celestial spheres (according to the philosophers themselves) and especially in God's attributes, which are eternal and inseparable. Muslims hold that God is eternally living, knowing, powerful, and these attributes are necessary concomitants (lawazim) of His essence; they cannot separate. Thus they are not 'others' in the sense of being separable.

There is another valid interpretation of 'other': two things are 'other' if one is not identical to the other, or if knowledge of one does not entail knowledge of the other. In this sense, an attribute is indeed 'other' than the essence. But this kind of 'otherness' does not imply dependence of the efficient-cause type. The attribute's only 'need' is that it subsists in the essence as a state (hal) in its locus (mahall), but this is a necessary inseparability, not a causal relation. Even if one calls this 'cause and effect' (the essence being a receptive cause), that does not entail that the composite (essence+attributes) is in need of an external agent.

Finally, Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the composite of essence and attributes is not in need of any of the four causes (efficient, final, material, formal). The utmost relationship is the attribute's need for the essence as a necessary state for its locus. This does not conflict with God being self-sufficient (wajib al-wujud bi-dhatihi). The logicians' argument fails because they equivocate on 'need' and 'other'. The true meaning of 'need' here is simply inseparability, and 'other' means a part of the whole, not something external. Thus nothing in this entails that God is not necessary by Himself.

Key Terms
الغير (al-ghayr): The logicians use 'other' to mean separable or distinct; Ibn Taymiyya argues that attributes are not 'other' in the sense of being separate from the essence. [Note: In standard usage, ghayr can mean any distinct entity; here it is narrowed to exclude inseparable attributes.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, which meaning of 'other' (ghayr) would make the logicians' premise 'its part is other than it' false when applied to God's attributes?
A) Other in the sense of not identical
B) Other in the sense of separable and separate
C) Other in the sense of known independently
D) Other in the sense of being a different concept

Key Takeaway
The term 'other' must be disambiguated: separable otherness would threaten tawhid, but non-identical otherness does not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Find a contemporary theological statement about God's attributes that uses the term 'other' (or 'distinct') in an ambiguous way. Explain which meaning would be valid and which would be problematic according to Ibn Taymiyya.
?

Refuting the Infinite Regress Objection and Defending the Confined Cause
Refutation of infinite regress objection and defense of confined cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the infinite regress objection and distinguish between confined and transitive causes in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Infinite regress only follows if every ruling depends on an external cause.

The objection that a ruling must be for the essence of the original case, otherwise infinite regress occurs, is a common challenge to identifying shared attributes as causes. Ibn Taymiyya responds by clarifying the conditions under which regress actually follows. This leads him to explain the fiqhi concept of the 'confined cause' (al-illa al-qasira), which is permissible by consensus when textually indicated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's objection
Find the claim that if the ruling is not for the essence (dhat) of the original case, then an infinite regress (tasalsul) would be necessary. This is the standard objection against using sabr wa taqsim to identify a cause.
The opponent assumes that every ruling must ultimately be grounded in something intrinsic.
2
Locate Ibn Taymiyya's conditional response
He states that infinite regress only occurs if the ruling is always established for an external cause (kharj). If it is sometimes for the essence and sometimes for an external, no regress follows.
This is a logical conditional: if (always external) then regress; if (sometimes essence) then no regress.
3
Understand the conclusion about the cause
Since regress is not inevitable, it is possible to know that the ruling in the original case is due to a shared description (wasf mushtarak) and not the essence. This legitimizes the search for a common cause.
4
Apply the distinction to usul al-fiqh
Ibn Taymiyya connects this to the fiqhi categories: a cause can be transitive (mutaddiya) or confined (qasira). A confined cause, when textually indicated, is accepted by consensus. The majority also allow derived confined causes.
This shows that even if a cause does not extend to other cases, analogical reasoning based on it is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله يجوز أن يكون الحكم ثابتاً في الأصل لذات الأصل، وإلا لزم أن يكون التسلسل، فنقول : لا يلزم التسلسل إلا إذا كان لا يثبت إلا لخارج (۱) . أما إذا كان تارة الأصل يثبت لخارج (۱) وتارة لغير خارج (۱) لم يلزم التسلسل . وحينئذ فلا يمتنع أن تعلم أن الحكم في هذا الأصل المعيَّن لم يثبت لما يختص به بل لوصف مشترك بينه وبين غيره. فائدتان في تعليل الحكم بـ «علة قاصرة وهذا كما يقول الفقهاء: إن الحكم يعلَّل تارة بـ علة متعدّية» وتارة بـ «علة قاصرة». والتعليل بـ «القاصرة» إذا كانت منصوصةٌ جائز باتفاق الفقهاء. وإنما تنازعوا فيما إذا كانت مستنبطة»، والأكثرون على جواز ذلك، وهو الصحيح، وهو مذهب مالك والشافعي وأحمد بن حنبل في المنصوص عنه، فإنه دائماً يعلل بـ «العلل القاصرة». وهو اختيار أبي الخَطَّاب وغيره من أصحابه.
As for his statement: 'It is possible that the ruling is established in the original case for the essence of the original, otherwise an infinite regress would occur,' we say: infinite regress is only necessary if it is not established except for an external [cause]. If it is sometimes established for an external and sometimes for a non-external, then infinite regress does not occur. In that case, it is not impossible to know that the ruling in this specific original case was not established for what is specific to it but for a description common between it and others. Two benefits in explaining the ruling with a 'confined cause'. This is as the jurists say: the ruling is sometimes explained by a 'transitive cause' and sometimes by a 'confined cause'. Explaining by a confined cause, if it is textually indicated, is permissible by consensus of the jurists. They only differed when it is derived; the majority permit that, and it is correct. It is the school of Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad ibn Hanbal in the transmitted opinion, for he always explains rulings with confined causes. And it is the choice of Abu al-Khattab and others among his companions.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical objection and its rebuttal, followed by an expansion on the fiqhi concept of 'confined cause' ('illa qasira). The objection claims that if the ruling in the original case were not for its own essence, an infinite regress would ensue. Ibn Taymiyya responds by distinguishing two scenarios: if the ruling is always established for something external (i.e., an attribute), then indeed we would need an infinite chain of causes. But if sometimes the ruling is for the essence itself and sometimes for an external attribute, no regress follows. Therefore, we can know that in a particular original case, the ruling is due to a shared attribute (the cause) rather than the essence. He then relates this to the usul al-fiqh concept: jurists differentiate between 'transitive cause' (mutaddiya) that extends to other cases and 'confined cause' (qasira) that is limited to the original. Using a confined cause is permissible by consensus when textually ordained; when the cause is derived by ijtihad, the majority (including Malik, Shafi'i, and Ahmad) permit it. Ibn Taymiyya notes that Ahmad always used confined causes, citing Abu al-Khattab. This supports the legitimacy of analogical reasoning based on a common attribute even if the cause does not appear in other cases, as long as the derivation is sound.

Key Terms
علة قاصرة
علة متعدية
سبر وتقسيم
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya shows that the infinite regress objection only applies if all rulings depend on external causes, and he defends the use of confined causes in fiqh as a legitimate basis for analogy.

Ibn Taymiyya's Dilemma on Exhaustive Division
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' claim that exhaustive division does not yield certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma against the logicians' denial of certainty in exhaustive division and evaluate its logical strength.

Core Idea
Exhaustive division yields certainty; denying it undermines the logicians' own practice.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians who claim that exhaustive division (al-sabr wa al-taqsīm) does not produce certainty. He presents a powerful dilemma: either division yields certainty or it does not. Both options refute the logicians' position. He then illustrates with an example from metaphysics—the division of existence—which the logicians themselves accept. This argument is crucial for showing that the method of division, used in both logic and uṣūl al-fiqh, can indeed produce certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
The logicians argued that even if a cause is established for something external, it might be for something else, so exhaustive division does not yield certainty.
This claim is the target of Ibn Taymiyya's refutation.
2
Extract the dilemma
Ibn Taymiyya poses a disjunctive conditional: either division in rational matters yields certainty, or it does not. He then draws consequences from each option.
The dilemma is a form of constructive dilemma: (A or B), if A then C, if B then D; therefore C or D.
3
Analyze the example of existence
He gives an example of dividing existence into necessary/possible, eternal/originated, etc. This is an exhaustive division of a universal concept.
If the intellect can enumerate the divisions of the most universal concept (existence), it can certainly do so for less universal ones.
4
Understand the refutation
If division yields certainty, then the logicians' claim that the disjunctive conditional is not a form of demonstration is false. If not, then their own statement (which relies on a division) is false. Either way, they are refuted.
Thus, the logicians' position is internally inconsistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - إمكان العقل حصر «العقليات» جواب قولهم: وإن ثبت لخارج فمن الجائز أن يكون لغير ما أبدى، وإن لم يُطلع عليه مع البحث عنه، فيقال: إما أن يكون التقسيم في «العقليات» قد يفيد اليقين، وإما أن لا يفيده بحال. فإن كان الأول بطل جَعْلُهم «الشرطي المنفصل» من صور القياس البرهاني. وإن كان الثاني بطل كلامهم هذا. ومعلوم أن هذا أحق بالبطلان من ذاك، فإن القائل إذا قال: الوجود إما أن يكون واجباً، وإما أن يكون ممكناً؛ وإما أن يكون قديماً، وإما أن يكون حادثاً؛ وإما أن يكون قائماً بنفسه، وإما أن يكون قائماً بغيره؛ وإما أن يكون مخلوقاً، وإما أن يكون خالقاً؛ ونحو ذلك من التقسيم الحاصر لجنس الوجود، كان هذا حصراً لكلي عقلي بل الوجود أعم الكليات. وإذا أمكن العقل أن يحصر أقسامه فحصر أقسام بعض أنواعه أولى. وهم يسلمون هذا كله وهذا هو العلم الأعلى عندهم. فكيف يقولون: إن السبر والتقسيم لا يفيد اليقين؟
The Third Station - The possibility of the intellect encompassing the rational matters. Answer to their statement: 'Even if it is established for something external, it is possible that it is for something other than what was shown, even if it is not disclosed despite investigation...' So it is said: Either the division in rational matters yields certainty, or it does not yield it at all. If the first, then their making of the disjunctive conditional a form of demonstrative syllogism is invalid. If the second, then their statement here is invalid. It is known that this is more deserving of invalidity than that. For if someone says: Existence is either necessarily existent or possible; either eternal or originated; either self-subsistent or subsistent in another; either created or creator; and similar exhaustive divisions of the genus of existence, this would be an enumeration of a rational universal, rather existence is the most general of universals. If the intellect can enumerate its divisions, then enumerating the divisions of some of its species is more appropriate. And they concede all of this, and this is the highest science according to them. So how can they say that examination and division does not yield certainty?
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against the logicians who claimed that exhaustive division (السبر والتقسيم) does not produce certainty. Ibn Taymiyya begins by quoting their statement, then presents a dilemma: either such division yields certainty or it does not. If it does, then the logicians' own claim that the disjunctive conditional is not a form of demonstrative syllogism is false. If it does not, then their own statement is false (since they rely on divisions themselves). He then gives an example: the division of existence into necessary/possible, eternal/originated, etc., is an exhaustive division of a universal concept. If the intellect can do this for the most universal concept, it can do it for less universal ones. He concludes by asking rhetorically how they can deny that division yields certainty, given that they themselves accept such divisions in their highest science (metaphysics).

Key Terms
التقسيم
السبر والتقسيم
اليقين
الشرطي المنفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The disjunctive conditional is a valid demonstrative syllogism.
B) The logicians' claim that division does not yield certainty is false.
C) The intellect cannot enumerate all rational matters.
D) The example of dividing existence is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that exhaustive division of a universal like existence yields certainty, contradicting the logicians' denial and revealing their inconsistency.

The Principle of Vision: Existence and Deservingness
Ibn Taymiyya establishes the principle of vision and God's superior deservingness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that God is more deserving of being seen than any created thing, based on the nature of vision and existence.

Core Idea
Vision requires an existent; God is the most perfect existent, thus the most deserving of being seen.

Ibn Taymiyya begins by establishing a foundational principle: vision (ru'ya) is a relationship that pertains only to what exists. Since God is the most perfect existent, indeed the Necessary Existent, He is more entitled to be seen than anything that is subject to non-existence. In this lesson, you will analyze this principle and see how he uses the example of light and the sun to illustrate the point that our inability to see God is due to our weakness, not to any impossibility in God being seen.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key premise about vision
Find the sentence that states what vision relates to. Ibn Taymiyya says vision is pure existence and only relates to an existent, not a non-existent.
This is the foundation of the argument.
2
Extract the comparative principle
Locate the comparison between what is more perfect in existence and what is accompanied by non-existence. He says whatever is more perfect in existence is more deserving of vision than what is accompanied by non-existence.
This is an awlawiyya (priority) argument.
3
Understand the example of light
How does the example of light and the sun support the principle? Light is more existent than darkness; strong light like the sun is not invisible in itself but because of weak eyesight.
Similarly, God's essence is not invisible, but our eyes are too weak.
4
Identify the conclusion about ru'ya
What does Ibn Taymiyya conclude about the possibility of seeing God? It is not impossible; God can strengthen our eyesight to see Him.
This counters the claim that ru'ya is impossible.

Source Text

لا تنفى مسميات الألفاظ ... فإن الرؤية وجود محض، وهي إنما تتعلق بموجود لا بمعدوم. فما كان أكمل وجوداً، بل كان وجوده واجباً، فهو أحق بها مما يلازمه العدم. ولهذا يشترط فيها النور الذي هو بالوجود أولى الظلمة، والنورُ الأشدُّ كالشمس لم يمتنع رؤيته لذاته، بل لضعف الأبصار. فهذا أنا نعجز عن رؤية الله مع ضعف أبصارنا، ولهذا لم يطق موسى رؤية الله في الدنيا . لكن لا يمتنع أن تكون رؤيته ممكنة والله قادر على تقوية أبصارنا لنراه . وإذا قيل : هي مشروطة بـ «اللون» و «الجهة ونحو ذلك مما يمتنع على الله، قيل له : كل ما لا بد منه في الرؤية لا يمتنع في حق الله. فإذا قال القائل : لو رُؤي للزم كذا، المجملة بالشرع واللازم منتف كانت إحدى مقدمتيه كاذبة وهكذا كل ما أخبر به الصادق الذي أخبر بأن المؤمنين يرون ربَّهم كما يرون الشمس والقمر ، كلُّ ما أخبر به وظن الظان أن في العقل ما يناقضه لا بد أن تكون إحدى مقدماته باطلة . فإذا قال: «لو رُؤى لكان متحيزاً، [۲۳۰] أو جسماً، أو كان في جهة، أو كان ذا لون، وذلك منتف عن الله، قيل له : جميع هذه الألفاظ مجملة لم يأت شرع بنفي مسمياتها حتى تُنْفَى بالشرع، وإنما ينفيها من ينفيها بالعقل فيُسْتَفْسَرُ عن مراده، إذ البحث في المعاني المعقولة، لا في مجرد هذه الألفاظ . ما يطلق عليه فيقال: ما تريد بأن المرئي لا بد أن يكون متحيّزاً ؟ فإن «المتحيز» في لغة العرب التي نزل بها القرآن يُعنى به «ما يحوزه غيره، كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿ أَوْ مُتَحَرًا إِلَى فِئَةٍ ﴾ [سورة الأنفال : ١٦]. فهذا تحيز موجود يحيط به موجود غيره إلى موجودات تحيط به، وسُمي «متحيزاً» لأنه تحيز من هؤلاء إلى هؤلاء. والمتكلمون يريدون بـ «التحيز» «ما شغَلَ الحيّز»، و «الحيّز» عندهم تقديرُ مكان»، ليس أمراً موجوداً . فالعالم عندهم متحيّز» ، وليس في حيز وجودي، و «المكان» عند عند أكثر هم وجودي . فإذا أريد بـ «المتحيز ما يكون في حيز وجودي مُنعت المقدمة الأولى، وهو قوله: كل مرئي متحيز»، فإن سطح العالم يمكن أن يُرى، وليس في عالم آخر . وإن قال : بل أريدُ به لا بد أن يكون في حيز، وإن كان عَدَمياً»، قيل له : العدم ليس بشيء، فمن جعله في الحيّز العدمي لم يجعله في شيء موجود ومعلوم أنه ما ثُمَّ موجود إلا الخالق والمخلوق . فإذا كان الخالق بائناً عن المخلوقات كلها لم يكن في شيء موجود. وإذا قيل : هو في حيز معدوم كان حقيقته أنه ليس في شيء. فلِمَ قلت : إن هذا محال (۱)؟ وكذلك إذا قال : يلزم أن يكون جسماً»، ففيه إجمال تقدم التنبيه [٢٣١] عليه (۲) . وكذلك إذا قال: «في جهة، فإن الجهة) يراد بها شيء موجود وشيء معدوم. ما يراد بـ «الجهة» فإن شُرِط في المرئي أن يكون في جهة موجودة كان هذا باطلاً برؤية سطح العالم. وإن جعل العدمُ جهةً قيل له : إذا كان بائناً عن العالم ليس معه هناك غيره فليس في جهة وجودية. وإذا سميت ما هنالك «جهة) وقلت: هو في جهة على هذا التقدير، منعت انتفاء اللازم وقيل لك : العقل والسمع يدلان على ثبوت هذا اللازم، لا على انتفائه . وحينئذ، فإذا احتج عليها بـ قياس التمثيل، فقيل : المخلوقات الموجودة يمكن رؤيتها، فالخالق أحق بإمكان الرؤية، لأن المصحح للرؤية في المخلوقات أمر مشترك بين الخالق والمخلوق لا يختص بالمخلوق، وإذا كان المشترك مستلزماً لصحة الرؤية ثبتت صحة الرؤية .
The meanings of words are not negated ... for vision is pure existence, and it only relates to an existent, not a non-existent. So whatever is more perfect in existence, indeed whose existence is necessary, is more deserving of it than that which is accompanied by non-existence. For this reason, light is required for it, which is more existent than darkness, and stronger light like the sun is not prevented from being seen due to its essence, but due to the weakness of eyesight. Thus, we are incapable of seeing God with the weakness of our eyesight, and for this reason Moses could not bear seeing God in this world. But it is not impossible that His vision be possible and that God is able to strengthen our eyesight so that we see Him. And if it is said: Vision is conditioned by 'color' and 'direction' and the like, which are impossible for God, it is said to him: Everything necessary for vision is not impossible in God's case. So if someone says: 'If He were seen, such-and-such would be entailed,' [and it is] ambiguous according to the Law and the consequent is negated, then one of his premises is false. And thus every report of the truthful one who reported that the believers will see their Lord as they see the sun and the moon — everything he reported, if a thinker thinks that reason contradicts it, then one of his premises must be false. So if he says: 'If He were seen, He would be spatial (muttahayyiz), or a body, or in a direction, or possessing color,' and that is negated of God, it is said to him: All these terms are ambiguous; the Law has not come to negate their meanings so that they are negated by the Law. Rather, whoever negates them does so by reason, so he must be asked to explain his intention, for the discussion is about intelligible meanings, not about mere words. It is said to him: What do you mean by 'the visible must be spatial'? For 'spatial' (al-mutahayyiz) in the language of the Arabs in which the Qur'an was revealed means 'that which is encompassed by something else,' as in His statement: 'or turning to a group' (Quran 8:16). This is a 'spatializing' (tahayyuz) that exists, surrounded by another existent to other existents, and it is called 'spatial' because it moves from these to those. The mutakallimun mean by 'spatiality' (al-tahayyuz) 'what occupies a space' (hayyiz), and 'space' for them is 'an estimated place,' not something existent. So the world for them is 'spatial,' but not in an existent space, and 'place' for most of them is existent. So if by 'spatial' is meant 'what is in an existent space,' the first premise is denied, namely his statement 'every visible is spatial'; for the surface of the world can be seen and it is not in another world. And if he says: 'Rather I mean that it must be in a space, even if it is non-existent,' it is said to him: Non-existence is not a thing; so whoever places it in a non-existent space does not place it in anything existent. And it is known that there is no existent except the Creator and creation. So if the Creator is separate from all creation, He is not in any existent thing. And if it is said: He is in a non-existent space, the reality is that He is not in anything. So why do you say that this is impossible? And likewise if he says: 'It entails that He be a body,' there is ambiguity in that, as was previously noted. And likewise if he says: 'in a direction,' for 'direction' (al-jiha) can mean something existent or something non-existent. If the visible is conditioned to be in an existent direction, this is invalid by the vision of the surface of the world. And if non-existence is made a direction, it is said to him: If He is separate from the world and there is no other with Him there, then He is not in an existent direction. And if you call what is there a 'direction' and say He is in a direction on this assumption, the negation of the consequent is denied, and it is said to you: Reason and revelation indicate the affirmation of this consequent, not its negation. Then, if one argues against it by analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil), it is said: The created existents can be seen, so the Creator is more deserving of the possibility of vision, because that which makes vision correct in created things is a common factor between the Creator and the creature, not specific to the creature. And if the common factor entails the correctness of vision, then the correctness of vision is established.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' argument that seeing God is impossible because it would require God to possess attributes like being spatial, a body, in a direction, or having color. He begins by establishing a principle: vision relates to existent things, and things with more perfect existence (like the Necessary Existent) are more deserving of being seen. He uses the example of light: the sun is not invisible because of itself but because of weak eyesight; similarly, we cannot see God due to our weakness, but God can strengthen us. Then he addresses specific objections: if someone says vision requires color or direction, Ibn Taymiyya responds that whatever is necessarily required for vision is not impossible for God. He then analyzes the claim that 'if God were seen, He would be spatial (muttahayyiz)'. He shows that the term 'muttahayyiz' is ambiguous (mujmal). In Arabic language, it means 'that which is encompassed by something else' (as in Quran 8:16). The mutakallimun use it to mean 'occupying a space', and they consider space as an estimated non-existent thing. So if 'spatial' means being in an existent space, then the premise 'every visible is spatial' is false because the surface of the world is seen but not in another world. If it means being in a non-existent space, then that is not a real attribute, and being in non-existence is simply not being in anything, which is not impossible. Similarly for 'body' and 'direction' (jiha): if direction means an existent direction, then the surface of the world disproves it; if it means a non-existent direction, then it is not a real attribute, and revelation and reason affirm that God is in a direction (i.e., above the heavens) in a way that does not entail creation. After refuting the objections, Ibn Taymiyya presents his positive argument by analogy (qiyas al-tamthil): created existents can be seen; the Creator is more deserving of the possibility of vision because the factor that makes vision correct (al-musahhih li-l-ru'ya) is a common factor between Creator and creature, not specific to the creature. Since the common factor entails the correctness of vision, the vision of God is established. This argument uses the Islamic principle that the Creator shares attributes of perfection with creation in a superior way (as in the famous argument 'what is due to the more perfect is more fitting for the less perfect' but here reversed: the more perfect is more deserving).

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya in this passage, what is the reason we cannot see God in this life?
A) God's essence is invisible by nature.
B) Our eyesight is too weak and God has not granted us the strength to see Him.
C) Vision of God is impossible for any being.
D) God is not an existent.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's principle that vision pertains to existents and that greater existence warrants greater visibility is the foundation for defending ru'ya against claims of impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Write out Ibn Taymiyya's argument from this passage in a simple syllogistic form: Premise 1: Vision only relates to existents. Premise 2: God is the most perfect existent. Conclusion: God is more deserving of being seen. Then check whether the conclusion follows logically.
?
Read Ibn Taymiyya's statement: 'the stronger light like the sun is not prevented from being seen due to its essence, but due to the weakness of eyesight.' Explain in your own words how this analogy supports his case.

Refuting Objections to Ru'ya: The Ambiguity of 'Spatial' and 'Direction'
Ibn Taymiyya refutes objections by analyzing the ambiguity of terms like muttahayyiz and jiha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's method of refuting the logicians' objection that vision of God requires spatiality or direction, by distinguishing linguistic and technical meanings.

Core Idea
The terms 'spatial' and 'direction' are ambiguous; once clarified, the objection's premises are exposed as false or meaningless.

Logicians argue that if God were seen, He would have to be in a place or direction, which they consider impossible for God. Ibn Taymiyya does not simply deny the objection; he dissects the very terms used. By showing that 'muttahayyiz' and 'jiha' have multiple meanings, he demonstrates that either the premise is false or the objection relies on a technical definition that does not apply to God. In this lesson, you will learn his analytical technique of term analysis (tabayun al-mujmal).

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the statement: 'If He were seen, He would be muttahayyiz (spatial), or a body, or in a direction, or possessing color.' This is the objection that Ibn Taymiyya aims to refute.
The objection uses terms that are considered impossible for God.
2
Notice how Ibn Taymiyya characterizes these terms
He says they are 'mujmal' (ambiguous) and that the discussion must be about intelligible meanings, not mere words. He insists on asking what the objector means by these terms.
This is a key move in critical refutation.
3
Analyze the term 'muttahayyiz'
Ibn Taymiyya gives two definitions: (a) in Arabic language, it means 'that which is encompassed by something else' (from Quran 8:16); (b) in kalam, it means 'what occupies a space', where space is an estimated non-existent place.
Show that the first meaning does not apply to God, but the second meaning uses a non-existent space, which is not a real attribute.
4
Test the premise 'every visible is muttahayyiz' against each meaning
If spatial means 'in an existent space', then the surface of the world is a visible thing not in an existent space, so the premise is false. If spatial means 'in a non-existent space', then it is a meaningless condition.
Ibn Taymiyya uses a counterexample (the world's surface) to falsify the first interpretation, and argues the second is not a real requirement.
5
Analyze the term 'jiha' (direction) similarly
Direction can mean an existent direction (like above) or a non-existent concept. If the condition is an existent direction, the surface of the world disproves it. If non-existent, then it is not a real condition, and revelation and reason affirm that God is in a direction (above) without embodiment.
This shows the objection's weakness.

The meanings of words are not negated ... for vision is pure existence, and it only relates to an existent, not a non-existent. So whatever is more perfect in existence, indeed whose existence is necessary, is more deserving of it than that which is accompanied by non-existence. For this reason, light is required for it, which is more existent than darkness, and stronger light like the sun is not prevented from being seen due to its essence, but due to the weakness of eyesight. Thus, we are incapable of seeing God with the weakness of our eyesight, and for this reason Moses could not bear seeing God in this world. But it is not impossible that His vision be possible and that God is able to strengthen our eyesight so that we see Him. And if it is said: Vision is conditioned by 'color' and 'direction' and the like, which are impossible for God, it is said to him: Everything necessary for vision is not impossible in God's case. So if someone says: 'If He were seen, such-and-such would be entailed,' [and it is] ambiguous according to the Law and the consequent is negated, then one of his premises is false. And thus every report of the truthful one who reported that the believers will see their Lord as they see the sun and the moon — everything he reported, if a thinker thinks that reason contradicts it, then one of his premises must be false. So if he says: 'If He were seen, He would be spatial (muttahayyiz), or a body, or in a direction, or possessing color,' and that is negated of God, it is said to him: All these terms are ambiguous; the Law has not come to negate their meanings so that they are negated by the Law. Rather, whoever negates them does so by reason, so he must be asked to explain his intention, for the discussion is about intelligible meanings, not about mere words. It is said to him: What do you mean by 'the visible must be spatial'? This is a 'spatializing' (tahayyuz) that exists, surrounded by another existent to other existents, and it is called 'spatial' because it moves from these to those. The mutakallimun mean by 'spatiality' (al-tahayyuz) 'what occupies a space' (hayyiz), and 'space' for them is 'an estimated place,' not something existent. So the world for them is 'spatial,' but not in an existent space, and 'place' for most of them is existent. So if by 'spatial' is meant 'what is in an existent space,' the first premise is denied, namely his statement 'every visible is spatial'; for the surface of the world can be seen and it is not in another world. And if he says: 'Rather I mean that it must be in a space, even if it is non-existent,' it is said to him: Non-existence is not a thing; so whoever places it in a non-existent space does not place it in anything existent. And it is known that there is no existent except the Creator and creation. So if the Creator is separate from all creation, He is not in any existent thing. And if it is said: He is in a non-existent space, the reality is that He is not in anything. So why do you say that this is impossible? And likewise if he says: 'It entails that He be a body,' there is ambiguity in that, as was previously noted. And likewise if he says: 'in a direction,' for 'direction' (al-jiha) can mean something existent or something non-existent. If the visible is conditioned to be in an existent direction, this is invalid by the vision of the surface of the world. And if non-existence is made a direction, it is said to him: If He is separate from the world and there is no other with Him there, then He is not in an existent direction. And if you call what is there a 'direction' and say He is in a direction on this assumption, the negation of the consequent is denied, and it is said to you: Reason and revelation indicate the affirmation of this consequent, not its negation. Then, if one argues against it by analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil), it is said: The created existents can be seen, so the Creator is more deserving of the possibility of vision, because that which makes vision correct in created things is a common factor between the Creator and the creature, not specific to the creature. And if the common factor entails the correctness of vision, then the correctness of vision is established.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' argument that seeing God is impossible because it would require God to possess attributes like being spatial, a body, in a direction, or having color. He begins by establishing a principle: vision relates to existent things, and things with more perfect existence (like the Necessary Existent) are more deserving of being seen. He uses the example of light: the sun is not invisible because of itself but because of weak eyesight; similarly, we cannot see God due to our weakness, but God can strengthen us. Then he addresses specific objections: if someone says vision requires color or direction, Ibn Taymiyya responds that whatever is necessarily required for vision is not impossible for God. He then analyzes the claim that 'if God were seen, He would be spatial (muttahayyiz)'. He shows that the term 'muttahayyiz' is ambiguous (mujmal). In Arabic language, it means 'that which is encompassed by something else' (as in Quran 8:16). The mutakallimun use it to mean 'occupying a space', and they consider space as an estimated non-existent thing. So if 'spatial' means being in an existent space, then the premise 'every visible is spatial' is false because the surface of the world is seen but not in another world. If it means being in a non-existent space, then that is not a real attribute, and being in non-existence is simply not being in anything, which is not impossible. Similarly for 'body' and 'direction' (jiha): if direction means an existent direction, then the surface of the world disproves it; if it means a non-existent direction, then it is not a real attribute, and revelation and reason affirm that God is in a direction (i.e., above the heavens) in a way that does not entail creation. After refuting the objections, Ibn Taymiyya presents his positive argument by analogy (qiyas al-tamthil): created existents can be seen; the Creator is more deserving of the possibility of vision because the factor that makes vision correct (al-musahhih li-l-ru'ya) is a common factor between Creator and creature, not specific to the creature. Since the common factor entails the correctness of vision, the vision of God is established. This argument uses the Islamic principle that the Creator shares attributes of perfection with creation in a superior way (as in the famous argument 'what is due to the more perfect is more fitting for the less perfect' but here reversed: the more perfect is more deserving).

Key Terms
متحيز
جهة
جسم
لون
حيز
عدمي
إجمال

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Taymiyya say about the term 'muttahayyiz' as used by the mutakallimun?
A) It means being in an existent space.
B) It means being encompassed by something else.
C) It means occupying an estimated, non-existent space.
D) It means being a body.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's refutation works not by denying the objection outright, but by showing that its key terms are ambiguous; once clarified, the argument collapses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take the objection: 'If God were seen, He would have to be a body (jism).' Using Ibn Taymiyya's method, analyze the term 'jism' as it is used by the objector and by the linguists. State which meaning would make the premise false or irrelevant.

In a debate, your opponent says: 'God cannot be seen because vision requires color.' How would you respond by first asking what is meant by 'color' and then using a counterexample (e.g., seeing the surface of the world has no color)?

The Analogical Argument (Qiyas al-Tamthil) for the Possibility of Seeing God
Ibn Taymiyya presents an analogical argument for the possibility of ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's analogical argument that God is more deserving of being seen than created things, and evaluate its logical structure.

Core Idea
If created existents can be seen, then the Creator, who shares the factor that makes vision possible, is more deserving of being seen.

After refuting objections, Ibn Taymiyya provides a positive argument by analogy (qiyas al-tamthil). He argues that since created existents can be seen, and the factor that allows vision (al-musahhih li-l-ru'ya) is a common attribute between Creator and creature, then the Creator is even more deserving of being seen. This argument is a form of analogical reasoning that is central to Islamic theology and jurisprudence. In this lesson, you will examine its structure and assess its validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogical argument
Locate the sentence: 'If one argues against it by analogical reasoning, it is said: The created existents can be seen, so the Creator is more deserving of the possibility of vision.' This is the core analogy using the principle of awlawiyya (priority).
The analogy goes from the less deserving (creature) to the more deserving (Creator).
2
Find the common factor (al-musahhih)
Look for the phrase 'that which makes vision correct'. Ibn Taymiyya states that the factor is a common attribute between Creator and creature, not specific to the creature.
This is the middle term in the analogy.
3
Understand the direction of the analogy
The analogy is from the lower (creature) to the higher (Creator) – min al-adna ila al-a'la. This is a valid form of analogical reasoning in Islamic logic, based on the principle that if the lower shares a property, the higher shares it more.
It relies on the superiority of the Creator.
4
Check the validity of the inference
Does the common factor genuinely entail the possibility of vision? Ibn Taymiyya asserts that since the common factor entails the correctness of vision in creatures, it should also entail it in the Creator.
One might object that the factor might not be sufficient in the Creator's case, but Ibn Taymiyya assumes the factor is the same and that the Creator's superiority strengthens the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا تنفى مسميات الألفاظ ... فإن الرؤية وجود محض، وهي إنما تتعلق بموجود لا بمعدوم. فما كان أكمل وجوداً، بل كان وجوده واجباً، فهو أحق بها مما يلازمه العدم. ولهذا يشترط فيها النور الذي هو بالوجود أولى الظلمة، والنورُ الأشدُّ كالشمس لم يمتنع رؤيته لذاته، بل لضعف الأبصار. فهذا أنا نعجز عن رؤية الله مع ضعف أبصارنا، ولهذا لم يطق موسى رؤية الله في الدنيا . لكن لا يمتنع أن تكون رؤيته ممكنة والله قادر على تقوية أبصارنا لنراه . وإذا قيل : هي مشروطة بـ «اللون» و «الجهة ونحو ذلك مما يمتنع على الله، قيل له : كل ما لا بد منه في الرؤية لا يمتنع في حق الله. فإذا قال القائل : لو رُؤي للزم كذا، المجملة بالشرع واللازم منتف كانت إحدى مقدمتيه كاذبة وهكذا كل ما أخبر به الصادق الذي أخبر بأن المؤمنين يرون ربَّهم كما يرون الشمس والقمر ، كلُّ ما أخبر به وظن الظان أن في العقل ما يناقضه لا بد أن تكون إحدى مقدماته باطلة . فإذا قال: «لو رُؤى لكان متحيزاً، [۲۳۰] أو جسماً، أو كان في جهة، أو كان ذا لون، وذلك منتف عن الله، قيل له : جميع هذه الألفاظ مجملة لم يأت شرع بنفي مسمياتها حتى تُنْفَى بالشرع، وإنما ينفيها من ينفيها بالعقل فيُسْتَفْسَرُ عن مراده، إذ البحث في المعاني المعقولة، لا في مجرد هذه الألفاظ . ما يطلق عليه فيقال: ما تريد بأن المرئي لا بد أن يكون متحيّزاً ؟ فإن «المتحيز» في لغة العرب التي نزل بها القرآن يُعنى به «ما يحوزه غيره، كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿ أَوْ مُتَحَرًا إِلَى فِئَةٍ ﴾ [سورة الأنفال : ١٦]. فهذا تحيز موجود يحيط به موجود غيره إلى موجودات تحيط به، وسُمي «متحيزاً» لأنه تحيز من هؤلاء إلى هؤلاء. والمتكلمون يريدون بـ «التحيز» «ما شغَلَ الحيّز»، و «الحيّز» عندهم تقديرُ مكان»، ليس أمراً موجوداً . فالعالم عندهم متحيّز» ، وليس في حيز وجودي، و «المكان» عند عند أكثر هم وجودي . فإذا أريد بـ «المتحيز ما يكون في حيز وجودي مُنعت المقدمة الأولى، وهو قوله: كل مرئي متحيز»، فإن سطح العالم يمكن أن يُرى، وليس في عالم آخر . وإن قال : بل أريدُ به لا بد أن يكون في حيز، وإن كان عَدَمياً»، قيل له : العدم ليس بشيء، فمن جعله في الحيّز العدمي لم يجعله في شيء موجود ومعلوم أنه ما ثُمَّ موجود إلا الخالق والمخلوق . فإذا كان الخالق بائناً عن المخلوقات كلها لم يكن في شيء موجود. وإذا قيل : هو في حيز معدوم كان حقيقته أنه ليس في شيء. فلِمَ قلت : إن هذا محال (۱)؟ وكذلك إذا قال : يلزم أن يكون جسماً»، ففيه إجمال تقدم التنبيه [٢٣١] عليه (۲) . وكذلك إذا قال: «في جهة، فإن الجهة) يراد بها شيء موجود وشيء معدوم. ما يراد بـ «الجهة» فإن شُرِط في المرئي أن يكون في جهة موجودة كان هذا باطلاً برؤية سطح العالم. وإن جعل العدمُ جهةً قيل له : إذا كان بائناً عن العالم ليس معه هناك غيره فليس في جهة وجودية. وإذا سميت ما هنالك «جهة) وقلت: هو في جهة على هذا التقدير، منعت انتفاء اللازم وقيل لك : العقل والسمع يدلان على ثبوت هذا اللازم، لا على انتفائه . وحينئذ، فإذا احتج عليها بـ قياس التمثيل، فقيل : المخلوقات الموجودة يمكن رؤيتها، فالخالق أحق بإمكان الرؤية، لأن المصحح للرؤية في المخلوقات أمر مشترك بين الخالق والمخلوق لا يختص بالمخلوق، وإذا كان المشترك مستلزماً لصحة الرؤية ثبتت صحة الرؤية .
The meanings of words are not negated ... for vision is pure existence, and it only relates to an existent, not a non-existent. So whatever is more perfect in existence, indeed whose existence is necessary, is more deserving of it than that which is accompanied by non-existence. For this reason, light is required for it, which is more existent than darkness, and stronger light like the sun is not prevented from being seen due to its essence, but due to the weakness of eyesight. Thus, we are incapable of seeing God with the weakness of our eyesight, and for this reason Moses could not bear seeing God in this world. But it is not impossible that His vision be possible and that God is able to strengthen our eyesight so that we see Him. And if it is said: Vision is conditioned by 'color' and 'direction' and the like, which are impossible for God, it is said to him: Everything necessary for vision is not impossible in God's case. So if someone says: 'If He were seen, such-and-such would be entailed,' [and it is] ambiguous according to the Law and the consequent is negated, then one of his premises is false. And thus every report of the truthful one who reported that the believers will see their Lord as they see the sun and the moon — everything he reported, if a thinker thinks that reason contradicts it, then one of his premises must be false. So if he says: 'If He were seen, He would be spatial (muttahayyiz), or a body, or in a direction, or possessing color,' and that is negated of God, it is said to him: All these terms are ambiguous; the Law has not come to negate their meanings so that they are negated by the Law. Rather, whoever negates them does so by reason, so he must be asked to explain his intention, for the discussion is about intelligible meanings, not about mere words. It is said to him: What do you mean by 'the visible must be spatial'? For 'spatial' (al-mutahayyiz) in the language of the Arabs in which the Qur'an was revealed means 'that which is encompassed by something else,' as in His statement: 'or turning to a group' (Quran 8:16). This is a 'spatializing' (tahayyuz) that exists, surrounded by another existent to other existents, and it is called 'spatial' because it moves from these to those. The mutakallimun mean by 'spatiality' (al-tahayyuz) 'what occupies a space' (hayyiz), and 'space' for them is 'an estimated place,' not something existent. So the world for them is 'spatial,' but not in an existent space, and 'place' for most of them is existent. So if by 'spatial' is meant 'what is in an existent space,' the first premise is denied, namely his statement 'every visible is spatial'; for the surface of the world can be seen and it is not in another world. And if he says: 'Rather I mean that it must be in a space, even if it is non-existent,' it is said to him: Non-existence is not a thing; so whoever places it in a non-existent space does not place it in anything existent. And it is known that there is no existent except the Creator and creation. So if the Creator is separate from all creation, He is not in any existent thing. And if it is said: He is in a non-existent space, the reality is that He is not in anything. So why do you say that this is impossible? And likewise if he says: 'It entails that He be a body,' there is ambiguity in that, as was previously noted. And likewise if he says: 'in a direction,' for 'direction' (al-jiha) can mean something existent or something non-existent. If the visible is conditioned to be in an existent direction, this is invalid by the vision of the surface of the world. And if non-existence is made a direction, it is said to him: If He is separate from the world and there is no other with Him there, then He is not in an existent direction. And if you call what is there a 'direction' and say He is in a direction on this assumption, the negation of the consequent is denied, and it is said to you: Reason and revelation indicate the affirmation of this consequent, not its negation. Then, if one argues against it by analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil), it is said: The created existents can be seen, so the Creator is more deserving of the possibility of vision, because that which makes vision correct in created things is a common factor between the Creator and the creature, not specific to the creature. And if the common factor entails the correctness of vision, then the correctness of vision is established.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' argument that seeing God is impossible because it would require God to possess attributes like being spatial, a body, in a direction, or having color. He begins by establishing a principle: vision relates to existent things, and things with more perfect existence (like the Necessary Existent) are more deserving of being seen. He uses the example of light: the sun is not invisible because of itself but because of weak eyesight; similarly, we cannot see God due to our weakness, but God can strengthen us. Then he addresses specific objections: if someone says vision requires color or direction, Ibn Taymiyya responds that whatever is necessarily required for vision is not impossible for God. He then analyzes the claim that 'if God were seen, He would be spatial (muttahayyiz)'. He shows that the term 'muttahayyiz' is ambiguous (mujmal). In Arabic language, it means 'that which is encompassed by something else' (as in Quran 8:16). The mutakallimun use it to mean 'occupying a space', and they consider space as an estimated non-existent thing. So if 'spatial' means being in an existent space, then the premise 'every visible is spatial' is false because the surface of the world is seen but not in another world. If it means being in a non-existent space, then that is not a real attribute, and being in non-existence is simply not being in anything, which is not impossible. Similarly for 'body' and 'direction' (jiha): if direction means an existent direction, then the surface of the world disproves it; if it means a non-existent direction, then it is not a real attribute, and revelation and reason affirm that God is in a direction (i.e., above the heavens) in a way that does not entail creation. After refuting the objections, Ibn Taymiyya presents his positive argument by analogy (qiyas al-tamthil): created existents can be seen; the Creator is more deserving of the possibility of vision because the factor that makes vision correct (al-musahhih li-l-ru'ya) is a common factor between Creator and creature, not specific to the creature. Since the common factor entails the correctness of vision, the vision of God is established. This argument uses the Islamic principle that the Creator shares attributes of perfection with creation in a superior way (as in the famous argument 'what is due to the more perfect is more fitting for the less perfect' but here reversed: the more perfect is more deserving).
