The Eternal Cannot Cease: A Disjunctive Proof from Ibn Taymiyya
Ibn Taymiyya proves that the eternal is either necessary by itself or a necessary concomitant, both impossible to cease.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and validate Ibn Taymiyya's disjunctive syllogism proving the impossibility of non-existence for the eternal.

Core Idea
The eternal's impossibility of ceasing follows from either necessity by itself or necessary concomitance.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya presents a tight disjunctive argument to demonstrate that anything eternal—whether necessary by itself or a necessary concomitant—cannot possibly cease to exist. This argument is central to Islamic theology (kalām) for establishing God's permanence and the impossibility of non-existence for any eternal entity. Let's walk through the logical structure step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The conclusion Ibn Taymiyya aims to prove is: 'The eternal cannot cease to exist.' He states this at the beginning: 'al-qadīm lā yajūzu ʿadamuhu'.
2
Extract the disjunction
Ibn Taymiyya provides two possible ways something can be eternal: either it is eternal by itself (qadīm bi-nafsihi) or it is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the necessary by itself. Find the phrase 'immā wājib bi-nafsihi, wa immā lāzim'.
The disjunction is exclusive and exhaustive: every eternal must fall into one of these two categories.
3
Analyze the first option
If something is eternal by itself, it necessarily exists by itself (mawjūd bi-nafsihi), and whatever exists by itself is necessary by itself (wājib bi-nafsihi). A necessary being cannot cease, because ceasing would imply it was receptive to non-existence, contradicting its necessity. Trace this chain in the Arabic.
The reasoning uses the principle that 'eternity is more specific than existence' (al-qidam akhaṣṣ li-l-wājib min al-wujūd).
4
Analyze the second option and conclude
If the eternal is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the necessary by itself, then its non-existence would entail the non-existence of the necessary by itself, which is impossible. Thus both cases lead to the same conclusion: non-existence is impossible. This is a valid constructive dilemma.
Ibn Taymiyya ends by noting that rational people agree on this (ittafaqa al-ʿuqalāʿ ʿalā hādhā).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك إذا قيل: القديم لا يجوز عدمه، لأن قدمه إما بنفسه، أو بموجب واجب بنفسه أو لازم يجب قدمه بنفسه. وما كان قديماً بنفسه كان موجوداً بنفسه بالضرورة، فإن القدم أخص للواجب بنفسه من الوجود فيلزم من ثبوته ثبوت الوجود، فإذا كان قديماً بنفسه فهو موجود بنفسه بالضرورة. وما كان موجوداً بنفسه فهو واجب بنفسه، وإلا لافتقر إلى فاعل. فالقديم إما واجب بنفسه، وإما لازم للواجب بنفسه. وكلاهما ممتنع العدم، لأنه يستلزم عدم الواجب بنفسه، ولو عُدم لكان قابلاً للعدم، فلا يكون واجباً بنفسه. ولهذا اتفق العقلاء على هذا، وهو أن القديم إما موجود واجب بنفسه، وإما لازم لما هو كذلك.
And likewise if it is said: The eternal is not permissible to cease, because its eternity is either by itself, or by something necessitating a necessary by itself or a necessary concomitant that must be eternal by itself. And whatever is eternal by itself exists by itself necessarily, for eternity is more specific to the necessary by itself than existence, so from its affirmation the affirmation of existence follows. So if it is eternal by itself, then it exists by itself necessarily. And whatever exists by itself is necessary by itself, otherwise it would depend on an agent. So the eternal is either necessary by itself or a necessary concomitant of the necessary by itself. And both are impossible to cease, because that entails the non-existence of the necessary by itself, and if it ceased, it would be receptive to non-existence, so it would not be necessary by itself. And for this reason the rational people agreed upon this, which is that the eternal is either an existent necessary by itself or a necessary concomitant of that which is so.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage presents a disjunctive syllogism to prove that anything eternal cannot cease to exist. Ibn Taymiyya argues that eternity has two possible sources: either the thing is eternal by itself (bi-nafsihi) or it is eternal through something else that is necessary by itself (wājib bi-nafsihi) or a necessary concomitant (lāzim) that must be eternal. If something is eternal by itself, it necessarily exists by itself, because eternity is a more specific attribute than existence; thus, if it is eternal, it exists. And whatever exists by itself is necessary by itself (otherwise it would need an external agent). Therefore, the eternal is either necessary by itself or a necessary concomitant of the necessary. In both cases, non-existence (ʿadam) is impossible. If the necessary by itself could cease, it would be receptive to non-existence, contradicting its necessity. The necessary concomitant also cannot cease because that would entail the non-existence of the necessary by itself. This argument is foundational in Islamic theology (kalām) for establishing the impossibility of non-existence of the eternal (e.g., God).

Key Terms
قديم
واجب بنفسه
لازم
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why is non-existence (ʿadam) of the eternal impossible in both cases—whether it is necessary by itself or a necessary concomitant?
A) Because the eternal is defined as that which cannot cease.
B) Because if it is necessary by itself, it cannot cease; and if it is a concomitant, its cessation would entail the cessation of the necessary by itself.
C) Because all eternal things are equally impossible to cease.
D) Because the necessary by itself would become contingent if it ceased.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's disjunctive proof shows that the impossibility of ceasing follows from either self-necessity or necessary concomitance, covering all cases of eternality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the disjunctive syllogism from a kalām text (e.g., al-Ghazali's Iqtisād) that argues 'The world is either temporally originated or eternal. If originated, it requires a creator. If eternal, it also requires a cause. Therefore, the world has a creator.' Identify the disjuncts and conclude.
?
Validate the following argument: 'The Qur'an is either created or uncreated. If created, it is temporal. If uncreated, it is eternal. Therefore, the Qur'an is either temporal or eternal.' Is this a valid disjunctive syllogism? Why or why not?

Demonstration -- Burhan
advanced
15 min
The Impossibility of a Contingent Eternal
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the notion of a contingent eternal being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that an eternal being cannot be contingent and identify the logical structure and critique of the philosophical position.

Core Idea
Eternal and contingent are contradictory; if something is eternal, it must be impossible to cease.

Ibn Taymiyya attacks the philosophical claim that the heavens are eternal yet contingent. He begins with a legal analogy from the story of Barira to illustrate how a shared attribute can be the true cause, then applies this logic to the problem of eternity. This lesson will walk through his reasoning and reveal the inner contradiction of the philosophers' position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the fiqh analogy
The Barira case: a freed slave woman has the option (khiyar) to remain with her slave husband or leave. The ruling is either due to her being under a deficient husband or to her self-ownership. Ibn Taymiyya notes that the specific attribute of the original case is not necessarily the cause; a shared attribute (self-ownership) may be the cause.
This analogy grounds the logical principle that a specific attribute cannot be the cause if the ruling also applies elsewhere without it.
2
Apply the analogy to the eternal-contingent issue
Similarly, the claim that the eternal (qadim) is impossible of non-existence is not due to a specific attribute (e.g., being eternal by itself) but due to the shared attribute of being eternal, whether by itself or by another.
This sets up the argument that any eternal being, regardless of how it acquires eternity, cannot be contingent.
3
Trace the logical reasoning
Ibn Taymiyya argues: The eternal is either necessary by itself (wajib bi-dhatihi) or a necessary concomitant of the necessary. Both are impossible of non-existence: the first because it exists necessarily, the second because its non-existence would entail the non-existence of its cause, the necessary by itself, which is absurd.
This establishes that eternal necessarily implies impossibility of non-existence.
4
Identify the target and its contradictions
The target is the philosophers (like Ibn Sina) who claim the spheres are eternal yet contingent. Ibn Taymiyya shows they contradict themselves and even Aristotle, who only considered originated things as possible. Their attempted reconciliation fails logically and opposes the consensus of rational thinkers.
This step situates the argument within the broader Islamic critique of Greek-influenced philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا قيل : خِيارُ الأمة المعتقة تحت العبد، كقصة بَرِيرَة، إما أن يكون يثبت لكونها كانت تحت ناقص، وإما أن يكون لكونها ملكت نفسها، أمكن أن يقال : وإما أن يكون لمجموع الأمرين. لكن تعليله بما يختص الأصل - سواءً كان هو المجموع، أو بعض منه ـ يمكن التعليل بما العلم بنفيه كما يمكن العلم بغيره من المنفيات. كما إذا قيل : الإنسان إنما كان يختص الأصل حسّاساً متحركاً بالإرادة، لحيوانيته، لا لإنسانيته ؛ والحَيَوانية مشتركة بينه وبين الفرس وسائر الحيوان فيكون حساساً متحركاً بالإرادة ، فلا يمكن أن يدعي في مثل هذه أن المختص بالإنسان هو علة كونه حساساً متحركاً بالإرادة، بل العلة ليست إلا المشترك بينه وبين الحيوان . وكذلك إذا قيل : القديم لا يجوز عدمه، لأن قدمه إما بنفسه، أو بموجب القديم واجب بنفسه أو لازم يجب قدمه بنفسه. وما كان قديماً بنفسه كان موجوداً بنفسه بالضرورة، فإن القدم أخص للواجب بنفسه من الوجود فيلزم من ثبوته ثبوت الوجود، فإذا كان قديماً بنفسه فهو موجود بنفسه بالضرورة. وما كان موجوداً بنفسه فهو واجب بنفسه، وإلا لافتقر إلى فاعل. فالقديم إما واجب بنفسه، وإما لازم للواجب بنفسه. وكلاهما ممتنع العدم، لأنه يستلزم عدم الواجب بنفسه، ولو عُدم لكان قابلاً للعدم ، فلا يكون واجباً بنفسه. ولهذا اتفق العقلاء على هذا، وهو أن القديم إما موجود واجب بنفسه، وإما لازم لما هو كذلك . تناقض من ادعى قديماً ممكن العدم فإذا قال القائل : لو كانت الأفلاك قديمة لامتنع عدمها ، لكن عدمها ممكن بالأدلة الدالة عليه، فلا تكون قديمة، لم يجز أن يقال : بل القديم لا يجوز عدمه لعلة مختصة بالقديم بنفسه، دون ما كان معلولاً لغيره، فالأفلاك، وإن قيل هي قديمة، فهي ممكنة العدم. فإن هذا باطل لما ذكرناه من أن المشترك بين الواجب بنفسه والواجب بغيره هو مستلزم لِقِدَم المشترك بين القديم الموجود بنفسه والقديم الموجود بغيره. فمن ادعى قديماً موجوداً بغيره، وقال إنه مع هذا يمكن عدمه، فقوله متناقض، كما بسط في موضعه. ولهذا لم يقل أحد من العقلاء : إنها قديمة يمكن عدمها الإمكان المعروف . وإنما أدعوا أن لها ماهية باعتبار نفسها، فقيل : الوجود والعدم، ولكن وجب لها الوجود من غيرها بقدم الأفلاك مع إمكان العدم وقد تبين بطلان هذا في غير هذا الموضع وبين أن هذا قول مخالف بطلان القول لجميع العقلاء، حتى أرسطو وأتباعه عندهم لا يكون ممكناً إلا المحدث الذي يمكن وجوده وعدمه وحتى هؤلاء الذين قالوا بأنها قديمة يمكن وجودها وعدمها»، كابن سينا وأتباعه تناقضوا ووافقوا سلفهم وسائر العقلاء فذكروا في عدة مواضع أن الممكن الذي يقبل الوجود والعدم لا يكون إلا محدثاً، وأن كل ما كان دائماً لا يكون ممكناً . وأما القديم الذي لا يمكن عدمه فليس عندهم ممكناً. وإن كان وجوبه بغيره . وإنما خالف في هذا طائفة من الفلاسفة كابن سينا وأمثاله الذين أرادوا أن يجمعوا بين قول سلفهم وبين ما جاءت به الرسل مع دلالة العقول عليه، فلم يمكنهم ذلك إلا بما خالفوا به الرسول مع مخالفة العقول، مع مخالفة سَلَفهم فيما أصابوا فيه، وموافقتهم فيما أخطئوا فيه وكان كُفْراً في الملل، ومع تناقضهم ومخالفة جميع العقلاء.
If it is said: The option (khiyar) of the slave woman who is freed while under a slave husband, like the story of Barira, is either established because she was under a deficient one, or because she became owner of herself, it is possible to say: 'Or it is due to both matters.' But its being caused by something specific to the original (case) — whether it is the whole or part of it — it is possible to cause by what is known to be negated just as it is possible to know other negated things. As if it is said: 'Man, who is specific to the original, is sentient and moving by will only because of his animality, not because of his humanity; and animality is shared between him and the horse and other animals, so he is sentient and moving by will. So it is not possible to claim in such a case that what is specific to man is the cause of his being sentient and moving by will; rather, the cause is only what is shared between him and the animal.' Similarly, if it is said: The eternal (qadim) does not admit of non-existence, because its eternity (qidam) is either by itself, or by something necessitating the eternal which is necessary by itself or a necessary concomitant whose eternity is necessary by itself. And whatever is eternal by itself exists by itself necessarily, for qidam is more specific to the necessary by itself than existence, so from its affirmation the affirmation of existence follows. So if it is eternal by itself, it exists by itself necessarily. And whatever exists by itself is necessary by itself, otherwise it would need a maker. So the eternal is either necessary by itself or a concomitant of the necessary by itself. Both are impossible of non-existence, because it would entail non-existence of the necessary by itself, and if it were non-existent it would be receptive of non-existence, so it would not be necessary by itself. Hence the rational ones agree on this: that the eternal is either an existent necessary by itself, or a concomitant of what is such. Contradiction of one who claims an eternal possible of non-existence. So if someone says: 'If the spheres (aflak) were eternal, their non-existence would be impossible; but their non-existence is possible according to the proofs indicating it, so they are not eternal,' it is not permissible to say: 'Rather, the eternal does not admit of non-existence due to a cause specific to the eternal by itself, not to what is an effect of something else; so the spheres, even if said to be eternal, are possible of non-existence.' For this is false because of what we mentioned: that what is shared between the necessary by itself and the necessary by another necessitates the eternity of what is shared between the eternal existent by itself and the eternal existent by another. So whoever claims an eternal existent by another and says that with this it can be non-existent, his statement is contradictory, as elaborated in its place. Hence no rational person has said that they are eternal, possible of non-existence in the well-known sense. Rather, they claimed that they have a quiddity (mahiyya) in themselves, so existence and non-existence are (possible) for it, but existence became necessary for it from another (the claim of) eternity of spheres with possibility of non-existence. And the falsity of this has been shown elsewhere, and it is shown that this statement contradicts all rational thinkers, even Aristotle and his followers among them do not consider possible except the originated (muhdath) which can exist and cease. And even those who said that they are eternal, can exist and cease, like Ibn Sina and his followers, contradicted themselves and agreed with their predecessors and all rational thinkers, for they mentioned in many places that the possible which accepts existence and non-existence is only originated, and that whatever is perpetual is not possible. As for the eternal which cannot be non-existent, according to them it is not possible, even if its necessity is from another. Only a faction of philosophers like Ibn Sina and his ilk opposed in this, those who wanted to combine the statement of their predecessors with what the messengers brought along with the indication of intellects. They could not do so except by opposing the messenger and opposing intellects, while opposing their predecessors in what they were correct in, and agreeing with them in what they erred in and was disbelief in the religions, and with their contradictions and opposition to all rational thinkers.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the philosophical claim that the heavens (aflak) are eternal (qadim) yet contingent (mumkin al-‘adam). He begins with an illustration from fiqh: the case of Barira, a slave woman freed while married to a slave. The point is that if a ruling (khiyar) is found in a case where a certain attribute (e.g., having a deficient husband) is present, but the same ruling also occurs in another case where that attribute is absent but a shared attribute (e.g., self-ownership) is present, then the specific attribute of the original case cannot be the sole cause; the shared attribute must be at least part of the cause. He then analogizes to the case of man and animal: humanity is specific to man, but sentience and voluntary motion are due to animality, which is shared with other animals. So the specific attribute (humanity) is not the cause. Similarly, in proving that the eternal cannot be contingent, he argues: the eternal (qadim) is either necessary by itself (wajib bi-dhatihi) or a necessary concomitant of such. Both are impossible of non-existence because they entail the non-existence of the necessary by itself, which is self-contradictory. Therefore, any claim that something is both eternal and possible of non-existence is contradictory. He refutes the counterargument that the eternal can be contingent if its eternity is from another (e.g., the spheres being caused). He insists that the shared property of being eternal (whether by itself or by another) necessitates impossibility of non-existence. He then attacks the philosophers like Ibn Sina who tried to reconcile the Aristotelian position of eternal spheres with the religious and rational requirement that only originated things are truly possible. Ibn Taymiyya points out their internal contradictions and their departure from all rational thinkers, including Aristotle himself.

Key Terms
قديم
واجب بنفسه
ممكن
علة
مشترك

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why can't the heavens be both eternal and contingent?
A) Because the heavens are made of a different substance.
B) Because something eternal is either necessary by itself or a necessary concomitant of the necessary; both cannot cease, so contingency is impossible.
C) Because contingency only applies to originated things, and the heavens are originated.
D) Because Aristotle said that the heavens are necessary.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya establishes that eternity and contingency are mutually exclusive, refuting the philosophical claim of eternal contingent spheres.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument in your own words as a chain of three logical steps. Then, using the same method, evaluate the claim: 'The Quran is uncreated (qadim) yet contingent.' What contradiction arises?
?
Task 2: Identify the shared attribute in Ibn Taymiyya's analogy (man vs. animal) and explain how it corresponds to the shared attribute in the eternity argument. Then apply the same principle to a different theological debate (e.g., the attributes of God).

Critique of Logicians' Epistemic Classification of Demonstration
Author critiques logicians who exclude revelation-based premises from demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' exclusion of revealed premises from demonstration and evaluate the grounds of his objection.

Core Idea
For Ibn Taymiyya, revelation is the highest certainty, not a lower form of knowledge.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya takes direct aim at the logicians who define demonstration (burhan) as necessarily composed of universal premises known without reliance on an infallible source. By classifying syllogisms that use revealed premises as merely rhetorical or dialectical, they implicitly deny certainty to divine revelation. Ibn Taymiyya's objection is not merely terminological; it strikes at the epistemological foundations of Greek logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
What exactly are the logicians claiming? They claim that demonstration must be based on premises known without the saying of the infallible (i.e., purely rational or empirical).
2
Find the consequence they draw
For them, if a premise comes from revelation, the syllogism becomes rhetorical or dialectical, not demonstrative.
3
Recognize the basis for their classification
They consider revealed premises as 'musallamat' (accepted) or 'maqbulat' (received), which they deem less than certain.
4
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's objection
He objects because from an Islamic perspective, revelation is the highest certainty. The logicians' hierarchy is therefore flawed.
5
Assess the critique's validity
Does Ibn Taymiyya's objection rely on a different epistemological standard? How might a logician respond?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكلام مع من يجعل موادَّ «البرهان» القضايا الكلية المعلومة بدون قول المعصوم بل قد يسمون القياس المستدل على إحدى مقدماته بقول المعصوم من «الخطابي» و «الجدلي»، لأنهم يجعلون تلك القضايا من «المسلمات» و «المقبولات»، لا من البرهانيات اليقينيات.
And the discussion is with those who make the materials of 'demonstration' (burhan) the universal propositions known without the saying of the infallible. Rather, they call the syllogism that relies on one of its premises on the saying of the infallible one of 'rhetorical' (khatabi) and 'dialectical' (jadali), because they consider those propositions to be from 'accepted premises' (musallamat) and 'received premises' (maqbulat), not from demonstrative certainties (burhaniyat yaqiniyat).
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a polemical critique against the logicians (especially the Peripatetic tradition) who define demonstration (burhan) as a syllogism composed of universal premises that are known without reliance on an infallible source (i.e., purely rational or empirical premises). According to them, if a premise is taken from revelation (the saying of the infallible, i.e., the Prophet), the resulting syllogism is not demonstrative but rather rhetorical (khatabi) or dialectical (jadali), because they classify such premises as 'musallamat' (accepted on authority) or 'maqbulat' (received), which are not certain. Ibn Taymiyya objects to this classification, arguing that it arbitrarily excludes divine revelation from the realm of certainty. For the Muslim, revelation provides the highest certainty. Thus, the logicians' hierarchy is flawed from an Islamic perspective. This passage is part of Ibn Taymiyya's larger project to refute the epistemological assumptions of Greek logic.

Key Terms
البرهان
الخطابي
الجدلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because revealed premises are not universal.
B) Because they consider such premises to be accepted on authority (musallamat), not certain.
C) Because the Prophet's sayings are not considered logical.
D) Because they require empirical verification.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' epistemic hierarchy that places rational certainty above revealed certainty, defending the legitimacy of revelation in demonstrative reasoning.

?
2. Find a contemporary claim from a Muslim intellectual who argues that empirical science alone yields certainty, and identify how Ibn Taymiyya would critique it.

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of the Exclusivity of Demonstrative Syllogism
Author refutes logicians' claim that only demonstrative syllogism yields certain knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique against the claim that certain knowledge is only obtained through demonstrative syllogism, and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
Prophetic certainty bypasses syllogism; the logicians' exclusive claim is the falsehood.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya attacks a core claim of the logicians: that certain knowledge (al-‘ilm al-yaqīnī) can only be acquired through demonstrative syllogism (al-qiyās al-burhānī). He offers a powerful counterexample from divine revelation: knowledge derived from an infallible source (al-ma‘sūm) yields certainty without any syllogistic reasoning. This lesson will guide you through his argument and its implications for the epistemology of the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Locate in the passage the statement that Ibn Taymiyya attributes to the logicians: that acquired sciences ('ulūm kasbiyya) are only obtained through their demonstrative syllogism. This is the target of his refutation.
The phrase 'قولهم العلوم الكسبة لا تحصل إلا بقياسهم البرهاني' is the claim at issue.
2
Understand the definition of a certain proposition
Ibn Taymiyya defines a certain proposition (al-qadiyya al-yaqiniyya) as what is known as true with certain knowledge. Note that he does not tie this definition to any specific method; it is simply known with certainty.
This definition is broad enough to include both syllogistic and non-syllogistic sources.
3
Examine the counterexample
The counterexample is this: if we know by a decisive proof that the infallible (the Prophet) only speaks truth, and we know by necessity that he declared a general prophetic proposition (e.g., 'Allah is the creator of everything'), then we have certain knowledge of that proposition. This knowledge does not come from a syllogism.
The examples given are: 'إِنَّ اللَّهَ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ عَلِيمٌ', 'اللَّهُ خَالِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ', 'لَا نَبِيَّ بَعْدِي'.
4
Analyze why Ibn Taymiyya does not press this argument here
He notes that this way is based on transmitted premises (muqaddimāt sam‘iyya) which he has not established in this work. So he does not argue against the logicians using it; instead he argues using premises they accept, and then by pure reason shows their claim is false.
This is a strategic move: he avoids reliance on revelation when debating those who may not accept it, using only rational arguments they acknowledge.
5
Conclude the refutation
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that their statement is not only false but 'among the falsest of falsehoods' (أبطل الأباطيل). He has shown by pure reason that there are ways to certainty other than their syllogism.
The refutation is complete: the logicians' claim is self-refuting because the counterexample demonstrates a certain knowledge without syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا أيضاً من ضلالهم. فإن القضية اليقينية : ما عُلم أنها حق علماً يقينياً. فإذا علم بدليل قطعي أن المعصوم لا يقول إلا حقا، وعُلم بالضرورة أنه قضى بهذه القضية النبوية غير الكلية، كما قضى بـ أن الله بكل شيء عليم ، و أن الله خالق كل شيء»، و «أنه لا نبي بعده، ونحو ذلك من القضايا الخبرية التي عُلم بالضرورة أن المعصوم أخبر بها عامة كلية، كان هذا من أحسن الطرق في حصول اليقين. وهذا الطريق لا يدخل في قياسهم البرهاني، ولكن هذا لما كان مبنيا على مقدمات سمعية لم نقررها في هذا الموضع لم نحتج به عليهم بل احتججنا عليهم بما يسلمونه . وما بيناه بمجرد العقل من أن قولهم العلوم الكسبة لا تحصل إلا بقياسهم البرهاني قول باطل، بل هو من أبطل الأباطيل .
This is also from their error. For the certain proposition is what is known as truth with certain knowledge. If it is known by a decisive proof that the infallible does not say except truth, and it is known by necessity that he ruled with this general prophetic proposition, as he ruled with 'Allah is knowledgeable of all things', and 'Allah is the creator of everything', and 'There is no prophet after him', and similar informative propositions that are known by necessity that the infallible informed of them as universal generalities, this is among the best ways of obtaining certainty. And this way does not enter into their demonstrative syllogism, but since this is based on transmitted premises that we have not established in this place, we have not argued against them with it; rather we have argued against them with what they accept. And what we have shown by pure reason that their statement that the acquired sciences are not obtained except through their demonstrative syllogism is a false statement, rather it is among the falsest of falsehoods.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a critique. Ibn Taymiyya attacks the logicians' claim that certain knowledge only comes through demonstrative syllogism. He defines a certain proposition as one known with certainty. Then he provides a counterexample: if we know by decisive evidence that an infallible speaker (the Prophet) only speaks truth, and we know by necessity that he pronounced a general proposition (like 'Allah is the creator of everything'), then this yields certainty without any syllogism. Such knowledge is based on transmitted premises (sam'iyyat) which he does not elaborate here; instead he argues against them using premises they accept. He concludes that their claim is utterly false.

Key Terms
القضية اليقينية
المعصوم
قياس برهاني
مقدمات سمعية
علوم كسبة

A) Using analogical reasoning from a legal precedent
B) Knowing a general prophetic statement from the infallible speaker
C) Combining two universal premises in the first figure
D) Applying the principle of non-contradiction

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' exclusive claim by showing that certain knowledge can be obtained through prophetic transmission, which does not depend on their demonstrative syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct your own counterexample to the logicians' claim using an Islamic source: take a Qur'anic verse known to be from the Prophet (e.g., 'Say, He is Allah, the One') and show how certainty is derived from the infallibility of the Prophet without a syllogism.
?
Evaluate: Could a logician respond that the knowledge of the Prophet's infallibility itself comes from a syllogism? If so, how would Ibn Taymiyya's argument still stand?

Implication as the Essence of All Proof
Author argues that implication is the essential criterion in all proof, regardless of form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the element of implication (luzum) in any argument from Islamic theology, regardless of its formal structure.

Core Idea
Implication, not syllogistic form, is the heart of every proof.

Ibn Taymiyya shifts the focus from the outer structure of arguments to the inner necessary connection. This lesson explores how recognizing implication (luzum) is sufficient for proof, using the argument from contingency as a case study.

Guided Path
1
Define luzum
Luzum is the necessary relationship between two things, where one entails the other. It is the core of implication.
Ibn Taymiyya uses the term to refer to any such necessary connection, not just formal logical implication.
2
Read the passage and identify the central claim
The author states that the real criterion in every demonstration and proof is implication (luzum). This is his main thesis.
3
Analyze the first example: existence implies the Maker
Ibn Taymiyya says that everything in existence is a sign of God, dependent on Him, so its existence implies the existence of the Creator. This is an implication relationship.
This is the classic argument from contingency and design.
4
Analyze the second example: the originated implies an originator
The author cites the Qur'anic verse from Surat at-Tur and the hadith of Jubayr ibn Mut'im. The verse challenges: 'Were they created from nothing or are they the creators?' The implication is that an originated thing must have an originator.
Jubayr's reaction shows that this implication can be felt intuitively, without formal reasoning.
5
Understand that implication can be known intuitively
Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that one does not need to know the technical term 'luzum' to grasp the implication. Even a Bedouin understands that if something exists, it must have a cause.
This undermines the logicians' claim that demonstration requires specific forms and terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود هنا أن الحقيقة المعتبرة في كل برهان ودليل في العالم هو «اللزوم». فمن عرف أن هذا لازم لهذا استدل بالملزوم على اللازم، وإن لم يذكر لفظ «اللزوم» ولا تصور معنى هذا اللفظ. بل من عرف أن كذا لا بد له من كذا، أو أنه إذا كان كذا كان كذا، وأمثال هذا، فقد علم اللزوم. كما يعرف أن كل ما في الوجود فهو آية الله، فإنه مفتقر إليه محتاج إليه، لا بد له منه فيلزم من وجوده وجود الصانع. وكما يعلم أن المحدث لا بد له من محدث، كما قال تعالى: {أم خلقوا من غير شيء أم هم الخالقون} [سورة الطور: 35]. وفي الصحيحين عن جبير بن مطعم أنه لما قدم في فداء الأسرى عام بدر سمع النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يقرأ في المغرب بـ «الطور». قال: «فلما سمعت قوله: {أم خلقوا من غير شيء أم هم الخالقون} أحسست بفؤادي قد انصدع.
And the intended point here is that the real criterion in every demonstration and proof in the world is 'implication'. So whoever knows that this is implied by that, he infers from the implicans to the implicatum, even if he does not mention the word 'implication' nor conceive the meaning of this word. Rather, whoever knows that such-and-such necessarily requires such-and-such, or that if it is such it is such, and the like, he has known implication. Just as he knows that everything in existence is a sign of God, for it is dependent on Him, in need of Him, and must have Him, so from its existence the existence of the Maker follows. And just as he knows that the originated thing must have an originator, as God said: {Were they created from nothing or are they the creators} [Surah at-Tur: 35]. And in the two Sahihs from Jubayr ibn Mut'im that when he came for the ransom of captives in the year of Badr, he heard the Prophet, peace be upon him, reciting in Maghrib 'at-Tur'. He said: 'When I heard His saying: {Were they created from nothing or are they the creators}, I felt my heart almost split.'
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage establishes that the essence of all proof is implication (luzum), not the external structure of a syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya argues that anyone who recognizes a necessary connection between two things has grasped implication, even without formal terms. He gives two examples: First, everything in existence is a sign of God because it is dependent on Him, implying the existence of the Creator. Second, the well-known argument from contingency: anything originated (hadith) necessarily requires an originator (muhdith). He cites the Qur'anic verse and the hadith of Jubayr ibn Mut'im, who was deeply moved by the verse and understood the implication intuitively. Thus, true reasoning is about recognizing necessary connections, not adhering to a prescribed form.

برهان

Key Takeaway
Implication (luzum) is the core of every proof; recognizing necessary connections is more fundamental than adhering to formal syllogistic structures.

The Fourth Status: No Event Without a Creator
Author states the fourth status that every event has a cause and provides Quranic proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a disjunctive syllogism in a Quranic argument and explain why Ibn Taymiyya considers it a fitri proof.

Core Idea
The Quranic proof for a creator is a self-evident disjunctive syllogism, not a technical kalam argument.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya presents the fourth status of his refutation: that every event must have a creator. He cites the Quranic verse from Surah at-Tur, which poses a stark choice: either humans were created without a creator, or they created themselves. Both options are impossible, so the only remaining conclusion is that there is a creator. Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that this argument is not a complex philosophical demonstration but an innate (fitri) and self-evident (badihi) truth that every sound mind accepts naturally. This directly challenges the logicians' reliance on abstract concepts like contingency and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exhaustive division (taqsim hasir)
Ibn Taymiyya introduces the argument as a 'taqsim hasir' (exhaustive division). This means the two possibilities cover all logical possibilities with no overlap. In this case, the two possibilities are: (i) humans were created without a creator, or (ii) they created themselves.
An exhaustive division is a key tool in disjunctive syllogisms.
2
Extract the two disjuncts from the Quranic verse
The verse asks: 'Were they created without a creator? Or did they create themselves?' These are the two disjuncts. The first disjunct is the claim that they came into existence without any agent; the second is that they caused their own existence.
The first disjunct is rejected as 'impossible' (mumtani'), the second as 'even more impossible' (ashadd imtina'an).
3
Assess the impossibility of each disjunct
Ibn Taymiyya says the first is impossible because it violates the self-evident principle that every event requires a cause (hadith requires muhdith). The second is even more impossible because self-creation is logically absurd (something cannot bring itself into existence as it would have to exist before itself).
This assessment relies on fitri (innate) knowledge, not on technical metaphysical arguments.
4
Conclude by elimination
Since both disjuncts are impossible, the only remaining conclusion (by disjunctive syllogism) is that they have a creator (khaliq) who created them. This conclusion is necessary and certain.
The logical form is: p or q; not p; not q; therefore r (where r is 'they have a creator'). But strictly, the conclusion is the negation of both disjuncts, leading to the affirmation of a third option.
5
Understand why Ibn Taymiyya calls it fitri/badihi
He argues that this proof does not require syllogistic reasoning or technical terminology. It is immediately recognized by sound human nature (fitra) as self-evident. The Quran's use of the interrogative of denial reinforces that the truth is obvious to anyone with a functioning intellect.
This is a critique of the logicians' method which introduces complex categories like 'contingent' (mumkin) and 'necessary' (wajib).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع ـ لا يوجد حادث بدون محدث أحدثه فإن هذا تقسيم حاصر . يقول : أخُلِقوا من غير خالقِ خَلَقهم؟ فهذا ممتنع في بداية وجه الدليل في آية الطور العقول . أم هم خَلَقوا أنفسهم؟ فهذا أشدّ امتناعاً. فعلم أن لهم خالقاً خَلقهم. وهو سبحانه ذكر الدليل بصيغة استفهام الإنكار ليبيّن أن هذه القضية التي استدل بها فطرية، بديهية، مستقرة في النفوس، لا يمكن أحداً إنكارها . فلا يمكن صحيح الفطرة أن يدعي وجود حادث بدون محدث أحدثه، ولا يمكنه أن يقول هو أحدث نفسه .
The fourth status: There is no event without a cause that brought it about. For this is an exhaustive division. He (Allah) says: 'Were they created without a creator who created them?' This is impossible in the beginning of the indication of the minds in the verse of at-Tur. 'Or did they create themselves?' This is even more impossible. So it is known that they have a creator who created them. And He, exalted, mentioned the proof in the interrogative form of denial to show that this proposition used as evidence is innate, self-evident, settled in souls, and cannot be denied by anyone. So a person of sound innate nature cannot claim the existence of an event without a cause that brought it about, nor can he say that it brought itself into existence.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth status in Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the logicians. It is a critical passage because the author rejects the logicians' reliance on abstract metaphysical categories (like contingency) and instead grounds the proof for a creator in a self-evident, innate argument derived from the Qur'an. The argument is a disjunctive syllogism: the verse from Surah at-Tur (52:35) poses two alternatives — either human beings were created without a creator, or they created themselves. Both are impossible. The first is impossible because every event must have a cause (a foundational principle of reason); the second is even more impossible because it implies self-creation, which is absurd. Therefore, the only remaining conclusion is that there is a creator who created them. The author emphasizes that this argument is not a technical philosophical proof but a fitri (innate) and badihi (self-evident) truth that every sound human mind accepts naturally. By presenting it in the form of an interrogative of denial (istifham al-inkar), the Qur'an highlights its obviousness. This directly critiques the logicians' method of proving the existence of God through complex concepts like imkan (contingency) and istihala (impossibility), which Ibn Taymiyya considers artificial and unnecessary. The passage thus serves as a foundational moment in his broader attack on Greek logic as a valid tool for Islamic theology.

Key Terms
حادث
محدث
تقسيم حاصر
فطرية
بديهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) A conditional syllogism (modus ponens)
C) A disjunctive syllogism with an exhaustive division
D) An inductive argument from multiple examples

Key Takeaway
The Quranic proof for a creator is a self-evident disjunctive syllogism, grounded in fitra, and does not require the technical apparatus of Greek logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ibn Taymiyya's Conditional Refutation of Eclipse Superstition
Ibn Taymiyya refutes eclipse-death superstition using hadith and logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's modus tollens argument against the belief that eclipses are caused by deaths, and identify the post hoc fallacy.

Core Idea
Eclipses are divine warnings, not effects of human deaths.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya confronts a superstitious interpretation of a natural event: the belief that eclipses are caused by the death of a great person. Using the Prophet's hadith, he constructs a logical refutation that exposes the error. You will see how conditional reasoning (modus tollens) is applied to theological matters, and how the Prophet's statements provide both the premises and the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the superstitious claim
The belief that the solar eclipse was caused by the death of Ibrahim, the Prophet's son. This is the false causal claim that Ibn Taymiyya is refuting.
2
Extract the Prophet's negation of the causal link
The Prophet said: 'The sun and moon do not eclipse for the death or life of anyone.' This statement explicitly denies any causal connection between human deaths and eclipses.
This serves as a major premise in the logical argument.
3
Reconstruct the conditional argument (modus tollens)
Ibn Taymiyya's implicit argument: If eclipses were caused by deaths, they would not be signs of warning. But they are signs of warning (as per Qur'an and hadith). Therefore, they are not caused by deaths. This is a valid modus tollens.
Formally: If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P.
4
Verify the premises from the source
The premise that eclipses are warnings is supported by the Qur'anic verse 'And We do not send the signs except as a warning' and the Prophet's command to seek refuge and charity during eclipses.
The fact that the Prophet prescribed actions to avert evil confirms eclipses are not neutral.
5
Identify the fallacy in the superstitious belief
The superstitious belief commits the post hoc ergo propter hoc fallacy: because the eclipse occurred after the death, they assumed causation. Ibn Taymiyya's argument demonstrates that correlation does not imply causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يتسبب الكسوف وقوله : «لا تنكسفان لموت أحد ولا لحياته رد لما كان قد توهمه بعض الناس من عن موت أحد أن كسوف الشمس كان لأجل موت إبراهيم ابن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، وكان قد مات وكسفت الشمس، فتوهم بعضُ الجهال من المسلمين أن الكسوف كان لأجل هذا . فبين لهم النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أن الكسوف لا يكون سببه موتُ أحد من أهل الأرض، ونفى بذلك أن يكون الكسوف معلولاً عن ذلك. وظنوا أن هذا من جنس اهتزاز العرش لموت سعد بن معاذ، كما ثبت ذلك في الصحيح » (۱) . فنفى النبيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم ذلك، وبين أن ذلك من آيات الله التي يخوف بها عباده . والتخويف إنما يكون بما يكون سبباً للشر. قال تعالى: ﴿ وَمَا نُرْسِلُ بِالْآيَاتِ إِلَّا تخويفا ﴾ [سورة الإسراء : ٥٩]. فلو كان الكسوف وجوده كعدمه بالنسبة إلى الحوادث لم يكن سبباً لشر، وهو خلاف نص الرسول . وأيضاً في السنن (۲) أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نظر إلى القمر وقال لعائشة : يا عائشة! تعوّذي بالله من شر هذا، فإن هذا هو الغاسِقُ إذا وَقَبَ (۳)) . والاستعاذة إنما تكون مما يحدث عنه شرّ . وأمر صلى الله عليه وسلم عند انعقاد أسباب الشر بما يدفع مُوجِبَها بمشيئة الله تعالى وقدرته من الصلاة والدعاء والذكر والاستغفار والتوبة والإحسان بالصدقة والعتاقة. فإن هذه الأعمال الصالحة تعارض الشرَّ الذي انعقد سببه، كما في الحديث : «إن الدعاء والبلاء ليلتقيان بين السماء والأرض، فيَعْتَلِجان» (٤).
The eclipse is not caused. And His saying: 'They do not eclipse for the death or life of anyone' is a refutation of what some people had imagined from the death of someone, that the solar eclipse was due to the death of Ibrahim, the son of the Prophet, peace be upon him. He had died and the sun eclipsed, so some ignorant Muslims imagined that the eclipse was due to this. So the Prophet, peace be upon him, clarified to them that the eclipse is not caused by the death of anyone on earth, and thereby negated that the eclipse is an effect of that. And they thought that this was similar to the shaking of the Throne at the death of Sa'd ibn Mu'adh, as confirmed in the authentic hadith. So the Prophet, peace be upon him, negated that, and clarified that it is among the signs of Allah with which He frightens His servants. And frightening is only with that which is a cause of evil. He said: 'And We do not send the signs except as a warning.' So if the eclipse were, its existence like its non-existence with respect to events, it would not be a cause of evil, and that contradicts the text of the Messenger. And also in the Sunan, the Prophet, peace be upon him, looked at the moon and said to Aisha: 'O Aisha! Seek refuge with Allah from the evil of this, for this is the moon when it eclipses.' And seeking refuge is only from that which causes evil. And he commanded, upon the occurrence of causes of evil, with what repels their necessitators by the will and power of Allah, from prayer and supplication, remembrance, seeking forgiveness, repentance, and benevolence with charity and freeing slaves. For these righteous deeds counteract the evil whose cause has occurred, as in the hadith: 'Supplication and calamity meet between heaven and earth and wrestle.'
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the superstitious belief that eclipses are caused by human deaths. The author begins by quoting the hadith that the sun and moon do not eclipse for anyone's death or life. He recounts the historical incident when the Prophet's son Ibrahim died and an eclipse occurred, leading some to mistakenly link the two. The Prophet corrected them, explaining that eclipses are signs from Allah to frighten His servants. The author emphasizes that frightening is only with something that can cause evil, so eclipses must be potential causes of evil; otherwise they would be neutral, contradicting the Prophet's statement. He then cites another hadith where the Prophet told Aisha to seek refuge from the evil of the moon during an eclipse, confirming that it is a source of evil. Finally, he explains the prescribed response: prayer, charity, etc., which counteract the impending evil. The argument follows a syllogistic structure: if eclipses were neutral, they would not be warnings; but they are warnings; therefore they are not neutral.

Key Terms
كسوف
خسوف
آيات الله
تخويف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They would not be signs of warning.
B) They would be caused by deaths.
C) They would be purely natural.
D) They would be beneficial.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya uses the Prophet's hadith to construct a modus tollens argument that eclipses are divine warnings, not effects of human events, demonstrating the use of conditional logic in refuting superstitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the same modus tollens structure, construct an argument from the hadith 'The fever is from the heat of Hell' to refute the belief that fever is caused by natural factors alone.
?
Identify the post hoc ergo propter hoc fallacy in the belief that a bad harvest is caused by a ruler's sin, and then refute it using a conditional argument from Islamic principles.

Refuting Emanation and God's Knowledge of Particulars
Ibn Taymiyya critiques emanationism and its denial of divine omniscience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the philosophical claim that God does not know particulars and refute it using Qur'anic evidence.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya rejects philosophical emanationism because it denies God's knowledge of particulars, central to Islamic theology.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya attacks the philosophers' emanationist cosmology, which leads to the blasphemous conclusion that God does not know specific individuals or events. You will learn how he uses Qur'anic verses to expose the contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophical claim about the first emanation
Find the statement that the first emanation is a self-subsistent substance and lord of the world. Note its implications for divine lordship.
2
Extract the consequence regarding God's knowledge
The philosophers claim God does not know the essences of prophets or particular events. Locate this in the Arabic.
3
Contrast with the Qur'anic description of God's knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya implies that the Qur'an explicitly mentions God's knowledge of battles and prophets. What verses indicate this?
4
Formulate the refutation
Using the principle that the Qur'an is truthful, show that the philosophical claim contradicts revelation, so it must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهم يزعمون أن أول ما صدر عن رب العالمين جوهر قائم بنفسه، وأنه رب جميع العالم، وأن العقل العاشر هو رب كل ما تحت فلك القمر، ومنه تنزلت الكتب على الأنبياء. وعندهم، إن الله لا يعرف عين موسى ولا عيسى ولا محمد، ولا غير ذلك من قولهم إنه جزئيات هذا العالم، فضلاً عن أحد، جرى يوم بدر، ويوم الأحزاب، وغير ذلك الأحوال التي يذكرها الله في القرآن ويخبر بما كان ويكون من الأمور المعينة الجزئية. ولهذا كان قولهم في النبوة إنها مكتسبة، وإنها فيض يفيض على روح النبي إذا استعدت نفسه لذلك، فمن راض نفسه حتى استعدت فاض ذلك عليه. وأن الملائكة هي ما يتخيل في نفسه من الخيالات النورانية؛ وكلام الله هو ما يسمعه في نفسه من الأصوات بمنزلة ما يراه النائم في منامه. ومن عرف ما أخبر الله به عن ملائكة جبريل وغيره في غير موضع علم أن هذا الذي قالوه أشد مخالفة لما جاءت به الرسل من أقوال الكفار المبدلين من اليهود والنصارى. فإن الله أخبر عن الملائكة لما جاءوا إلى إبراهيم في صورة البشر أضيافاً، ثم ذهبوا إلى لوط، في غير موضع وأخبر عن جبريل حين ذهب إلى مريم، وتمثل لها بشراً سوياً ونفخ فيها. وأتاه مرة في صورة أعرابي. وفي الصحيحين عن عائشة رضي الله عنها، أن الحارث بن هشام قال: يا رسول الله! كيف يأتيك الوحي؟ قال: «يأتيني أحيانا في مثل صلصلة الجرس، وهو أشد علي، فيفصم عني وقد وعيت ما قال. وأحيانا يتمثل الملك رجلاً، فيكلمني فأعي ما يقول». قالت عائشة: «لقد رأيته ينزل عليه في اليوم الشديد البرد، فيفصم عنه وإن جبينه ليتفصد عرقا». وقد قال تعالى: {إنه لقول رسول كريم ذي قوة عند ذي العرش مكين مطاع ثم أمين} [التكوير:19-21] وزعم بعض المتفلسفة أن هذا هو «العقل الفعال» لأنه دائم الفيض. فيقال: قد قال: {إنه لقول رسول كريم} والأمين هو جبريل، والعقل الفعال لو قدر وجوده فلا تأثير له فيما ثم، وإنما تأثيره عندكم فيما تحت فلك القمر. فكيف ولا حقيقة له؟ بل ما يدعونه من المجردات والمفارقات غير النفس الناطقة كالعقول والنفوس إنما وجودها في الأذهان لا في الأعيان.
And they claim that the first thing that emanated from the Lord of the worlds is a self-subsistent substance, and that it is the lord of all the world, and that the tenth intellect is the lord of everything under the sphere of the moon, and from it the books were revealed to the prophets. And according to them, God does not know the specific essence of Moses nor Jesus nor Muhammad, nor other than that from their statement that He knows the particulars of this world, let alone anyone (who fought) at the battle of Badr, and His knowledge of the details of what happened at the battle of the Confederates, and other such events that God mentions in the Qur'an and informs about what happened and what will happen of specific particular matters. And for this reason was their statement concerning prophethood: that it is acquired, and that it is an emanation that flows upon the soul of the prophet when his soul becomes prepared for that, so whoever disciplines his soul until it becomes prepared, that flows upon him. And that the angels are what he imagines in his soul of luminous imaginations; and the speech of God is what he hears in his soul of sounds, like what the sleeper sees in his dream. And whoever knows what God informed about angels – Gabriel and others – in many places, knows that this which they said is more severely contradicting what the messengers brought than the statements of the disbelieving alterers among the Jews and Christians. For God informed about the angels when they came to Abraham in the form of human guests, then they went to Lot, in many places, and informed about Gabriel when he went to Mary, and he appeared to her as a perfect human being, and he breathed into her. And he came to him once in the form of a Bedouin. And in the two Sahihs from Aisha, may God be pleased with her, that al-Harith ibn Hisham said: O Messenger of God, how does revelation come to you? He said: 'Sometimes it comes to me like the ringing of a bell, and it is most severe upon me, then it ceases from me and I have retained what was said. And sometimes the angel appears to me as a man, and he speaks to me and I retain what he says.' Aisha said: 'I saw him receiving revelation on a very cold day, and it ceased from him while his forehead was dripping with sweat.' And God Almighty said: {Indeed, it is the word of a noble messenger, possessed of power, with the Lord of the Throne, secure, obeyed, and trustworthy} [Surah at-Takwir 81:19-21]. And some of the philosophers have claimed that this is the 'active intellect' because it is constantly emanating. So it is said: He said {Indeed, it is the word of a noble messenger} and the trustworthy is Gabriel, and the active intellect, if its existence were assumed, has no effect in the hereafter, but its effect according to you is only in what is under the sphere of the moon. How then, when it has no reality? Rather, what they claim of abstract entities and separate substances other than the rational soul – like the 'intellects' and 'souls' – their existence is only in minds, not in external reality.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the philosophers' metaphysical doctrines by Ibn Taymiyya. He begins by attacking their emanationist cosmology: they claim that the first emanation is a self-subsistent substance (the first intellect), that it is the lord of the world, that the tenth intellect is the lord of the sublunar sphere and the source of revelation to prophets. He then points out the blasphemous consequence: according to them, God does not know particulars – not even Moses, Jesus, Muhammad, or the events of battles like Badr and al-Ahzab, which the Qur'an explicitly details. This is a direct contradiction of the Islamic belief in God's omniscience. Next, he critiques their theory of prophethood: they say prophecy is an acquired emanation that flows when the soul is prepared, reducing revelation to a psychological phenomenon. They also reduce angels to 'luminous imaginations' in the mind and God's speech to internal sounds akin to dreams. Ibn Taymiyya counters by citing explicit Qur'anic and hadith evidence: angels came to Abraham and Lot in human form; Gabriel appeared to Mary as a perfect man; the Prophet received revelation in different forms, such as the ringing of a bell or an angel in human form (e.g., as Dihya al-Kalbi or an Bedouin). He quotes the hadith from Aisha and the verse from Surah at-Takwir, which describes the messenger (Gabriel) as noble, powerful, and trustworthy. The philosophers claim this verse refers to the 'active intellect', so Ibn Taymiyya responds: the verse clearly says 'messenger' and 'trustworthy', which is Gabriel, not an intellect. Moreover, even if the active intellect existed, it has no effect in the hereafter, only in the sublunar world according to them – so how can it be the source of revelation about the afterlife? Finally, he dismisses their abstract entities (intellects, souls) as mental constructs, not real existents. He notes that their correct mathematical and physical ideas do not validate their metaphysical errors.

Key Terms
العقل العاشر
الفَيْض
العلم الإلهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the philosophers claim about God's knowledge of the Prophet Muhammad?
A) God knows him as a universal, not as a specific individual.
B) God knows him completely.
C) God does not know him at all.
D) God only knows his soul.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' emanationism entails a denial of God's knowledge of particulars, which contradicts the Qur'an's explicit affirmation of divine omniscience.

Refuting Acquired Prophethood and the Philosophers' Concept of Angels
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the philosophical view of prophethood and angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the philosophical notions of acquired prophethood and angels as mental imaginations using scriptural evidence from the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya shows that the philosophers' reduction of prophethood and angels contradicts the lived, physical reality of revelation recorded in the Qur'an and hadith.

In this section, Ibn Taymiyya attacks the philosophers' claim that prophethood is an acquired emanation and that angels are mental images. He counters with explicit accounts from the Qur'an and the Prophet's own description of revelation. You will learn how to use these primary sources to invalidate the philosophical reinterpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophical definition of prophethood
Find the claim that prophecy is an acquired emanation resulting from self-discipline. Note the active role of the human soul.
2
Identify the philosophical definition of angels
They say angels are luminous imaginations in the mind. Locate this in the Arabic.
3
Find the Qur'anic evidence for the physical reality of angels
Ibn Taymiyya cites the stories of angels visiting Abraham and Mary. Summarize each instance.
4
Find the hadith evidence about revelation
The Prophet describes revelation as a bell ringing and an angel appearing as a man. Why does this refute the philosophers?
5
Evaluate the claim that the 'active intellect' is Gabriel
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the verse says 'messenger' (Gabriel), not 'active intellect.' Consider his logic.

وهم يزعمون أن أول ما صدر عن رب العالمين جوهر قائم بنفسه، وأنه رب جميع العالم، وأن العقل العاشر هو رب كل ما تحت فلك القمر، ومنه تنزلت الكتب على الأنبياء. وعندهم، إن الله لا يعرف عين موسى ولا عيسى ولا محمد، ولا غير ذلك من قولهم إنه جزئيات هذا العالم، فضلاً عن أحد، جرى يوم بدر، ويوم الأحزاب، وغير ذلك الأحوال التي يذكرها الله في القرآن ويخبر بما كان ويكون من الأمور المعينة الجزئية. ولهذا كان قولهم في النبوة إنها مكتسبة، وإنها فيض يفيض على روح النبي إذا استعدت نفسه لذلك، فمن راض نفسه حتى استعدت فاض ذلك عليه. وأن الملائكة هي ما يتخيل في نفسه من الخيالات النورانية؛ وكلام الله هو ما يسمعه في نفسه من الأصوات بمنزلة ما يراه النائم في منامه. ومن عرف ما أخبر الله به عن ملائكة جبريل وغيره في غير موضع علم أن هذا الذي قالوه أشد مخالفة لما جاءت به الرسل من أقوال الكفار المبدلين من اليهود والنصارى. فإن الله أخبر عن الملائكة لما جاءوا إلى إبراهيم في صورة البشر أضيافاً، ثم ذهبوا إلى لوط، في غير موضع وأخبر عن جبريل حين ذهب إلى مريم، وتمثل لها بشراً سوياً ونفخ فيها. وأتاه مرة في صورة أعرابي. وفي الصحيحين عن عائشة رضي الله عنها، أن الحارث بن هشام قال: يا رسول الله! كيف يأتيك الوحي؟ قال: «يأتيني أحيانا في مثل صلصلة الجرس، وهو أشد علي، فيفصم عني وقد وعيت ما قال. وأحيانا يتمثل الملك رجلاً، فيكلمني فأعي ما يقول». قالت عائشة: «لقد رأيته ينزل عليه في اليوم الشديد البرد، فيفصم عنه وإن جبينه ليتفصد عرقا». وقد قال تعالى: {إنه لقول رسول كريم ذي قوة عند ذي العرش مكين مطاع ثم أمين} [التكوير:19-21] وزعم بعض المتفلسفة أن هذا هو «العقل الفعال» لأنه دائم الفيض. فيقال: قد قال: {إنه لقول رسول كريم} والأمين هو جبريل، والعقل الفعال لو قدر وجوده فلا تأثير له فيما ثم، وإنما تأثيره عندكم فيما تحت فلك القمر. فكيف ولا حقيقة له؟ بل ما يدعونه من المجردات والمفارقات غير النفس الناطقة كالعقول والنفوس إنما وجودها في الأذهان لا في الأعيان.
And they claim that the first thing that emanated from the Lord of the worlds is a self-subsistent substance, and that it is the lord of all the world, and that the tenth intellect is the lord of everything under the sphere of the moon, and from it the books were revealed to the prophets. How then, when it has no reality? Rather, what they claim of abstract entities and separate substances other than the rational soul – like the 'intellects' and 'souls' – their existence is only in minds, not in external reality.

Refutation of the Active Intellect Claim
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the identification of the Angel with the philosophers' active intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the identification of the angel with the active intellect and identify his epistemological grounds.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya denies both the relevance and the reality of the philosophers' active intellect.

Philosophers often attempt to map Greek metaphysical concepts onto revealed texts, claiming that the angel Jibril mentioned in the Qur'an is the 'Active Intellect' of the Peripatetics. In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya dismantles this claim using both scriptural evidence and philosophical reasoning. He shows that even if the Active Intellect existed, it could not be the angel described in the Qur'an. More fundamentally, he challenges the very existence of such abstract entities. This lesson will guide you through his critical method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' claim
Locate the exact claim: some philosophers assert that the 'noble messenger' in Surah al-Takwir is the Active Intellect, because it is perpetually emanating.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's scriptural evidence
Note the Qur'anic verses he cites: the messenger is described as having power, a high station with the Owner of the Throne, obeyed and trustworthy. These attributes situate the angel in the divine realm.
The variant readings of 'ḍanīn' provide additional nuance but are not central to the argument.
3
Analyze his philosophical refutation
He argues that even if the Active Intellect existed, it only affects the sublunar world (under the moon), not the divine realm (ثَمَّ). Therefore, it cannot be the angel who is in the presence of the Throne.
This is a concessio ad absurdum: assuming the philosophers' own framework, their claim fails.
4
Recognize the deeper epistemological critique
Ibn Taymiyya goes further: the Active Intellect and similar abstract entities have no reality outside the mind. They are mere mental constructs (وجودها في الأذهان لا في الأعيان).
This aligns with his broader rejection of Platonic forms and separate intellects.
5
Appreciate his cross-referencing
He refers to his own work 'al-Ṣafadiyya' for a fuller treatment, showing that this is part of a sustained critique.
This indicates the systematic nature of his refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قال تعالى : إِنَّهُ لَقَوْلُ رَسُولٍ كَرِيمٍ ذِي قُوَّةٍ عِنْدَ ذِي الْعَرْشِ مَكِينٍ مُطَاعٍ ثَمَّ أَمِينٍ . وأكثر القراء يقرءون بِضَنِينٍ يعني: بمتهم، وقد قرىء بِضَنِينٍ أي: ببخيل. وزعم بعضُ المتفلسفة أن هذا هو «العقل الفعال»، لأنه دائم الفيض. فيقال : قد قال : إِنَّهُ لَقَوْلُ رَسُولٍ كَرِيمٍ ... العقل الفعال لو قُدّر وجوده فلا تأثير له فيما ثَمَّ، وإنما تأثيره عندكم فيما تحت فلك القمر . فكيف ولا حقيقة له؟ بل ما يدعونه من المجردات و المفارقات غير النفس الناطقة كـ «العقول» و «النفوس» إنما وجودها في الأذهان لا في الأعيان كما بسط الكلام عليها في «الصَّفَدية» وغيرها
And He, exalted, said: 'Indeed, it is the word of a noble messenger, possessed of power, with the Owner of the Throne, secure, obeyed, and trustworthy.' Most reciters read 'bi-ḍanīn' meaning 'accused,' and it was read 'bi-ḍanīn' meaning 'miserly.' And some philosophers claimed that this is the 'Active Intellect,' because it is perpetually emanating. So it is said: He said, 'Indeed, it is the word of a noble messenger...' The Active Intellect, if its existence were assumed, has no effect in that realm, rather its effect, according to you, is in what is beneath the sphere of the moon. So how can it be? And it has no reality? Rather, what they claim of abstract entities and separate substances other than the rational soul, like the 'intellects' and 'souls,' their existence is only in minds, not in external entities, as has been elaborated in 'al-Ṣafadiyya' and elsewhere.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the philosophical claim that the angel Jibril mentioned in the Qur'an (Surah al-Takwir) is the 'Active Intellect' of the Peripatetic tradition. The author first cites the Qur'anic verses describing the angel as powerful, trusted, etc. He notes two readings of 'ḍanīn' (accused vs. miserly). Then he reports the philosophers' claim that this refers to the Active Intellect because it is always emanating. The refutation proceeds: even if the Active Intellect existed, it only affects the sublunar world (under the moon), not the divine realm (ثَمَّ). Therefore, it cannot be the angel who is in the presence of the Throne. Moreover, Ibn Taymiyya denies the external existence of such abstract entities entirely, asserting they are merely mental constructs. He refers to his work 'al-Ṣafadiyya' for a fuller discussion. The argument is epistemological: the philosophers' entities have no reality beyond the mind. This is a characteristic critique of Ibn Taymiyya against the metaphysical claims of the falāsifa.

Key Terms
العقل الفعال (al-‘aql al-fa‘‘āl): For Ibn Taymiyya, the 'active intellect' is a baseless philosophical concept; even if assumed, it only affects the sublunar realm and has no reality in the divine realm. [Note: In the Peripatetic tradition (e.g., al-Farabi, Ibn Sina), it is a separate celestial intellect emanating forms; Ibn Taymiyya rejects its existence outright.]
المجردات (al-mujarradāt): Abstract entities that philosophers claim exist separately from matter; Ibn Taymiyya says they exist only in minds, not in external reality. [Note: Stable term for 'abstract entities' across traditions, but Ibn Taymiyya denies their external existence.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the status of the philosophers' 'active intellect'?
A) It is a real separate substance that emanates forms.
B) It is identical to the angel Jibril as described in the Qur'an.
C) It has no reality and exists only as a mental construct.
D) It is a celestial intellect that governs the sublunar world.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's refutation shows that philosophical concepts like the active intellect have no external reality and should not be read into revelation.

Write a short critique following Ibn Taymiyya's method: check if the claim is supported by revelation and if the concept has real external existence.

How Universal Premises Depend on Analogical Reasoning
Ibn Taymiyya argues that universal premises depend on tamthil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that universal syllogisms depend on analogical reasoning for their certainty.

Core Idea
Universal syllogisms need analogy to know that their premises are truly universal.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' claim that formal syllogisms are self-sufficient for certain knowledge. In this passage, he argues that the very universality of a premise—its application to all individuals of a kind—cannot be known without analogical reasoning. This reverses the hierarchy: logical syllogism depends on analogy, not the other way around.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Logicians assert that universal categorical syllogisms (al-qiyas al-shumuli) produce certain knowledge when their premises are certain. Ibn Taymiyya does not deny that syllogisms can yield knowledge, but he questions the origin of certainty about the premises.
The logicians' claim is implicit; Ibn Taymiyya states it to refute it.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's objection
He argues that to know a universal proposition (qadiyya kulliyya) is truly universal, one must be certain that all its individuals share a common attribute (qadr mushtarak). This certainty is not derived from the syllogism itself but from an analogical recognition of similarity across instances.
The key phrase: 'لا يمكن الجزم به إلا مع الجزم بتماثل أفراده'.
3
Analyze his alternative: analogical reasoning (tamthil)
The recognition of similarity among individuals is precisely what qiyas al-tamthil (analogical reasoning) provides. Ibn Taymiyya claims that the universal syllogism cannot function without this analogical step, turning the tables on logicians who dismiss tamthil as weak.
He uses 'قياس التمثيل' to mean the juristic analogy familiar in fiqh.
4
Evaluate the dependency
Thus, the universal syllogism depends on analogy for its premises; without tamthil, the universality of the premise is not justified. Ibn Taymiyya concludes that no knowledge is exclusively dependent on formal logical syllogism.
This is an epistemological critique, not a rejection of all reasoning.
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
In fiqh, the universal premise 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (al-muskir haram) is known through analogical comparison of various intoxicating substances (khamr, nabidh, etc.) that share the 'illa of intoxication. This illustrates Ibn Taymiyya's point.
The universal is built from analogical extension, not deduced from a higher universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أن حصول العلم لا يتوقف على «القياس المنطقي»
وإذا كان كذلك فنقول : لا ننكر أن القياس يحصل به علم إذا كانت مواده يقينية. لكن نحن نبين أن العلم الحاصل به لا يحتاج فيه إلى «القياس المنطقي»، بل يحصل بدون ذلك. فلا يكون شيء من العلم متوقفاً على هذا «القياس» . / ونبين أن المواد اليقينية التي ذكروها لا يحصل بها علم بالأمور الموجودة، فلا يحصل بها مقصود تزكو به النفوس .
بل ولا علم بالحقائق الموجودة في الخارج على ما لا يعلم كون الكلية كلية يحصل بـ قياس التمثيل. فلا يمكن قط أن يحصل بـ «القياس الشمولي المنطقي إلا بالتمثيل الذي يسمونه البرهاني) علم إلا وذلك يحصل بـ «قياس التمثيل» الذي يستضعفونه . فإن ذلك القياس لا بد فيه من قضية كلية والعلم بكون الكلية كلية لا يمكن الجزم به إلا مع الجزم بتماثل أفراده في القدر المشترك. وهذا يحصل بـ قياس التمثيل .
وكلا القياسين ينتفع به إذا تلقت بعض مقدماته الكلية عن خبر المعصوم إذا استعملت في الإلهيات بطريق الأولى»، كما جاء به القرآن وأما بدون هذين فلا ينفع في الإلهيات، ولا ينفع أيضاً في الطبيعيات منفعة علمية برهانية، وإنما يفيد قضايا عادية قد تنحرف فتكون من باب الأغلب.
وأما الرياضي المجرَّد عن المادة كالحساب والهندسة فهذا حق في نفسه، لكن ليس له معلوم في الخارج، وإنما هو تقدير عددٍ ومقدار في النفس، لكن ذلك يطابق أي معدود ومقدر واقعة في الخارج؟ و البرهان لا يقوم إلا على ما في النفس، لا يقوم على ما في الخارج. وأكثر ما تعبوا في الهندسة ليتوصلوا بذلك إلى علم الهيئة، كصفة الأفلاك والكواكب، ومقادير ذلك وحركاته. وهذا بعضه معلوم بـ «البرهان»، وأكثره غير معلوم بـ «البرهان»، وبينهم فيه من الاختلاف ما يطول وصفه. فصار المعلوم ببراهينهم من الرياضي وغيره أمر لا تزكو به النفوس، ولا يعلم به الأمور الموجودة إلا كما يُعلم بـ «قياس التمثيل».
Statement that the attainment of knowledge does not depend on logical syllogism. If that is the case, we say: We do not deny that a syllogism produces knowledge if its materials are certain. But we show that the knowledge obtained by it does not need logical syllogism, rather it is obtained without it. So no knowledge depends on this syllogism. And we show that the certain materials they mentioned do not produce knowledge of existing things, so they do not produce the intended purpose by which souls are purified. Rather, there is no knowledge of the realities existing externally such that the universality of a universal is known except by analogical syllogism. So it is absolutely impossible that knowledge is obtained by universal logical syllogism except by analogy, which they call demonstrative, and that is obtained by analogical syllogism which they consider weak. For that syllogism necessarily requires a universal proposition, and knowledge that a universal is universal cannot be asserted except with the assertion of the similarity of its individuals in the common attribute. And this is obtained by analogical syllogism. Both syllogisms are beneficial if some of their universal premises are received from an infallible report when used in theology by the 'first priority' method, as brought by the Quran. But without these two, it is not beneficial in theology, nor is it beneficial in physics with a certain scientific benefit; rather it gives customary propositions that may deviate so they belong to the realm of the probable. As for mathematics abstracted from matter, like arithmetic and geometry, this is true in itself, but it has no known object in external existence; rather it is an estimation of number and quantity in the soul. But does that correspond to any counted or measured thing occurring externally? Demonstration is only based on what is in the soul, not based on what is in the external. And most of their labor in geometry is to attain knowledge of astronomy, like the description of the celestial spheres and planets, their magnitudes and movements. Some of this is known by demonstration, but most of it is not known by demonstration, and among them there is disagreement too lengthy to describe. So what is known by their demonstrations, from mathematics and otherwise, is a matter by which souls are not purified, nor are existing things known except as they are known by analogical syllogism.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument from Ibn Taymiyya against the claim that logical syllogism (al-qiyas al-mantiqi) is necessary for certain knowledge. He begins by conceding that a syllogism can yield knowledge if its premises are certain, but he insists that this knowledge does not depend on the formal rules of logic—it can be attained without them. His main target is the idea that universal premises, which are essential for syllogisms, can be known with certainty only through analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil). He argues that to know a universal proposition is universal, one must be certain that all its instances share a common attribute (al-qadr al-mushtarak). This certainty, he claims, is not achieved by the syllogism itself but by an analogical inference that recognizes the similarity among individuals. He then distinguishes between two types of syllogism: the universal syllogism (al-qiyas al-shumuli) and the analogical syllogism (qiyas al-tamthil). The logicians exalt the former while disparaging the latter, but Ibn Taymiyya turns the tables: the universal syllogism cannot function without the analogical one. He adds that both can be useful in theology if their universal premises are received from an infallible source (khabar ma'sum) and used via the method of 'first priority' (awla), as exemplified in the Quran. Without such divine guidance, they are useless in theology and physics, yielding only probabilistic common sense. Turning to mathematics (arithmetic and geometry), he admits it is true in the abstract but has no external referent; demonstration in mathematics operates on mental constructs, not external realities. He criticizes the use of geometry to establish astronomy, noting that astronomical claims are largely not proven by demonstration and are disputed. Thus, the knowledge produced by logicians' demonstrations does not purify souls nor give certainty about external things—it is no better than analogical reasoning.

Key Terms
القياس المنطقي
قياس التمثيل
قضية كلية
القياس الشمولي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why can a universal premise (qadiyya kulliyya) not be known with certainty through a universal syllogism alone?
A) Because universal premises are innate and do not require proof.
B) Because the certainty of a universal premise depends on knowing the similarity of its instances, which requires analogical reasoning.
C) Because universal premises are derived from sense perception, which is always particular.
D) Because universal premises are only valid in mathematics, not in theology or physics.

Key Takeaway
Universal premises in syllogisms cannot be known with certainty without analogical reasoning, which proves that formal logic depends on analogy, not the other way around.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the universal proposition 'Every contract with gharar is invalid' and explain, following Ibn Taymiyya's argument, how its universality is established through analogical reasoning across specific instances of gharar (e.g., selling a bird in the air, selling fish in the sea).
?
Consider a syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Identify the universal premise and explain how its certainty depends on analogical reasoning.
