Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate
8 min
Why Sense Perception Cannot Provide Universal Premises
Ibn Taymiyya critiques sense perception as source of universals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that sense perception cannot yield universal premises for demonstration, and identify the epistemological status he assigns to empirical generalizations.

Core Idea
Sense perception yields only particulars; universal premises require a different foundation.

Ibn Taymiyya begins his critique of the logicians' certain premises by examining the first category: sense perception (al-hissiyyat). The logicians claim that our senses provide certain knowledge, such as that fire burns. Ibn Taymiyya challenges this, arguing that sense perception only gives particular observations, never a universal statement. In this lesson, we will unpack his reasoning and understand why he reduces empirical generalizations to probability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
The logicians count sense perceptions (hissiyyat) among the certain premises (yaqiniyyat) that can serve as premises in a demonstration. Find where Ibn Taymiyya states this and note the example they give (fire burning).
The logicians' position is that hissiyyat are a class of necessary knowledge (daruriyyat).
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's objection
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that sense perception does not perceive any universal at all. Identify the key sentence: 'Mалум أن الحس لا يدرك أمرا كليا عاما أصلا' (It is known that sense perception does not perceive any universal in general at all).
This is the core epistemological claim.
3
Examine the 'fire burns' example
Ibn Taymiyya deconstructs the example: the universal claim 'every fire burns' is not known by sense perception but by repeated experience (tajriba) and custom (ada). These, he says, are equivalent to analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil).
He equates custom and experience with analogical syllogism, not with certain demonstration.
4
Follow the further objection about the 'burning power'
If logicians appeal to an inherent burning power (quwwa muhriqa) in fire, Ibn Taymiyya counters that the universal claim 'every fire has this power' is itself a universal judgment needing proof. Even if granted, it does not yield certainty that every such power burns every object.
He shows that any attempt to salvage the universality leads to infinite regress or reliance on probability.
5
Summarize the epistemic equivalence
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that universal syllogism (qiyas shumuli), analogical syllogism (qiyas tamthil), custom (ada), and incomplete induction (istiqra' naqis) all share the same epistemological limitation: they yield only predominant (ghalib) rather than certain knowledge.
This undermines the logicians' claim that demonstration (burhan) can be built on empirical premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الأول - بيان أصناف اليقينيات عندهم التي ليس فيها قضية كلية
فإذا كان لا بد في كل قياس من قضية كلية فنقول : المواد اليقينيات قد حصروها في الأصناف المعروفة عندهم . أحدها الحسّيات. ومعلوم أن الحس لا يدرك أمراً كليا عاماً أصلاً . فليس في الأول: الحسيات المجرَّدة قضية كلية عامة تصلح أن تكون مقدمةً في «البرهان اليقيني». وإذا مثلوا ذلك بـ أن النار تُحرق ونحو ذلك لم يكن لهم علم بعموم هذه القضية، وإنما التجربة والعادة التي هي من جنس قياس التمثيل لما يعلمونه من الحكم الكلي، لا فرق بينه وبين قياس الشمول» و «قياس التمثيل». وإن عُلم ذلك بواسطة اشتمال النار على قوة مُحْرِقة، فالعلم بأن كل نار لا بد فيها من هذه القوة هو أيضاً حكم كلي .
معهم
وإن قيل : إن الصورة النارية لا بد أن تشتمل على هذه القوة، وأن ما لا قوة فيه ليس بنار، فهذا الكلام إذا قيل : إنه صحيح، قيل : إنه لا يفيد الجزم بأن كل ما فيه هذه القوة يحرقُ كلَّ ما لاقاه، وإن كان هو الغالب. فهذا يشترك فيه قياس التمثيل، والشمول، والعادة، والاستقراء الناقص. ومعلوم أن كل من قال «إن كل نار تَحْرِقُ كلَّ
The first aspect: Statement of the categories of certainties according to them in which there is no universal proposition. Since every syllogism requires a universal proposition, we say: The certain materials they have enumerated in the known categories. The first of them: sensibles. It is known that sense perception does not perceive any universal in general at all. So in the first: bare sensibles, there is no universal proposition suitable to be a premise in certain demonstration. And if they give the example of fire burning and the like, they have no knowledge of the generality of this proposition; rather, it is experience and custom, which are of the kind of analogical syllogism, for what they know of the universal judgment—there is no difference between it and universal syllogism and analogical syllogism. And if that is known through the fact that fire contains a burning power, then the knowledge that every fire necessarily contains this power is also a universal judgment. If it is said that the fiery form must contain this power, and that what has no power is not fire, this statement, if said to be correct, it is said that it does not provide certainty that everything possessing this power burns everything it meets, even if it is predominant. So in this, analogical syllogism, universal syllogism, custom, and incomplete induction share. And it is known that whoever says 'every fire burns every...'
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' first category of certain premises: sense perception (al-hissiyyat). He points out that sense perception only apprehends particulars, never universals. Therefore, bare sense experiences cannot provide a universal proposition that can serve as a premise in a certain demonstration (burhan yaqini). He addresses the common example given by logicians: 'fire burns'. He argues that one cannot know the universality of this judgment through sense perception alone; it is based on repeated experience (tajriba) and custom (ada), which are essentially analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil). According to him, there is no epistemic difference between the universal syllogism (qiyas shumuli) and analogical syllogism in this regard—both rely on the same kind of generalization. If one tries to explain the burning by appealing to an inherent burning power (quwwa muhriqa), then the claim that every fire necessarily possesses this power is itself a universal judgment that requires proof. Even if it is granted that the form of fire always contains the burning power, this does not entail certainty that everything with that power burns everything it contacts; it is only a predominant occurrence (ghalib). Thus, the same epistemological limitations apply to analogical reasoning, universal syllogism, custom, and incomplete induction—they all fail to provide demonstrative certainty. Ibn Taymiyya undercuts the logicians' claim that sense perception yields certain universal premises, showing that empirical generalizations are probabilistic, not apodictic.

البرهان اليقيني
التجربة والعادة
الاستقراء الناقص

Checkpoint

A) Because fire does not always burn in every circumstance.
B) Because sense perception only gives particular instances, and the universality is inferred through experience and custom, which are not certain.
C) Because the proposition 'fire burns' is tautological and trivial.
D) Because the logicians themselves admit that fire does not burn in paradise.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that empirical generalizations, including sense perception, cannot provide universal premises for certain demonstration; at best, they yield probable knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Using Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning, critique the premise 'Every intoxicating drink is forbidden (haram)' as a certain universal premise. Show why the evidence from sense perception (tasting intoxication) is insufficient for certainty.
?
Task 2: Compare the epistemic status of the statement 'Every day follows night' according to the categories of hissiyyat, tajriba, and 'ada. Explain why, on Ibn Taymiyya's view, this is not a certain universal proposition.

Demonstration -- Burhan

Universal premises are mental constructs
Author argues that universal premises are mental not external.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's argument that universal demonstrative premises are only mental constructs and not applicable to external particulars.

Core Idea
Universal premises exist only in the mind, not in external reality.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya delivers a direct challenge to the logicians' claim that demonstrative syllogisms (burhan) yield certain knowledge of the external world. He argues that the universal premises they rely on are merely mental estimations (muqaddarat dhihniyya) and lack extra-mental reality. Understanding this critique is essential for evaluating the scope and limits of logical demonstration in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Find the logicians' claim that universal premises used in burhan give certain knowledge of specific external things. The author is attacking this.
2
Locate the examples given
Note the specific premises mentioned: 'one is half of two' and 'things equal to the same thing are equal'.
These are awwaliyat (primary axioms) according to the author.
3
Analyze the author's classification
The author classifies these premises as muqaddarat dhihniyya (mental estimations), not universal in external existence.
4
Understand the key distinction
The author distinguishes between mental existence and external existence. Universality exists only in the mind; external particulars are not universal.
5
Draw the conclusion
Because the premises are only mental, they cannot be used to know specific existing things. Therefore, burhan cannot give knowledge of particulars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا تستعمل القضايا الكلية في شيء من الموجودات لم يبق معهم إلا الأوليات التي هي البديهيات العقلية و الأوليات الكلية قضايا مطلقة في الأعداد والمقادير ونحوها مثل قولهم الواحد نصف الاثنين، و الأشياء المساوية لشيء واحد متساوية، ونحو ذلك . وهذه مقدرات في الذهن، ليست في الخارج كلية . فقد تبين أن القضايا الكلية البرهانية التي يجب القطعُ بكليتها التي يستعملونها في قياسهم لا تُستعمل في شيء من الأمور الموجودة المعينة، وإنما تستعمل في مقدّرات ذهنية . فإذاً لا يمكنهم معرفة الأمور الموجودة المعينة بـ «القياس البرهاني». وهذا هو المطلوب.
Universal propositions are not used in anything of the existents. Nothing remains with them except the primary premises (awwaliyat), which are intellectual axioms, and the universal primary premises are absolute propositions in numbers and quantities and the like, such as their saying 'one is half of two', and 'things equal to the same thing are equal', and so on. And these are mental estimations (muqaddarat), not universal in external existence. So it has become clear that the universal demonstrative propositions whose universality must be determined, which they use in their syllogism, are not used in anything of the specific existing things, but rather are used in mental estimations. Therefore, they cannot know the specific existing things through 'demonstrative syllogism'. And that is what is required.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
In this critical passage, the author makes a powerful epistemological claim against the logicians: the universal propositions that form the basis of demonstrative syllogisms (al-qiyas al-burhani) cannot be applied to any real, specific existents. He first notes that the only universal premises left to the logicians (after his previous criticisms) are the 'awwaliyat' or primary axioms. These include mathematical truths like 'one is half of two' and 'things equal to the same thing are equal'. However, these are purely mental constructs (muqaddarat dhihniyya); they have no universal instantiation in the external world. The universality of these propositions exists only in the mind, not in extra-mental reality. Consequently, when logicians claim to use demonstrative syllogisms to gain knowledge of particular existing things (like specific bodies, substances, etc.), they are mistaken. The demonstrative method only works with mental estimations, not external particulars. Therefore, the logicians' claim to certain knowledge of the external world through syllogism is false—which is the very point the author seeks to establish.

Key Terms
القضايا الكلية
الأوليات
مقدرات ذهنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because logicians do not use the correct figures.
D) Because demonstration requires induction.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that universal demonstrative premises are mental constructs and cannot be used to know specific existing things.

Epistemological limitation of demonstrative syllogism
Author argues that universal premises are mental not external.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the epistemological consequence Ibn Taymiyya draws from his critique of universal premises.

Core Idea
Demonstrative syllogism cannot give certain knowledge of particular external existents.

Having established that universal premises are merely mental, Ibn Taymiyya now draws the devastating conclusion that the logicians' burhan cannot deliver knowledge of the specific things that exist in the world. This lesson examines that conclusion and its implications for the claims of the philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Restate the author's conclusion
At the end of the passage, the author states: 'Therefore, they cannot know the specific existing things through demonstrative syllogism.' This is his central conclusion.
2
Examine the warrant
The warrant is that the premises used in burhan are only mental estimations, not universal in external reality.
3
Consider the scope
The author specifies 'the universal demonstrative propositions whose universality must be determined' – not all universal propositions, but those used in demonstrations aimed at certainty.
4
Evaluate the implication
If true, this means that logicians cannot claim certain knowledge of any particular external thing through burhan. Their science is limited to mental constructs.
5
Apply to a concrete scenario
Think of a theologian who uses a syllogism to prove that this specific object is created. According to the author, such a proof cannot yield certain knowledge because the universal premise is only mental.

Refuting the Identification of the Intellectual World with the Unseen World
Ibn Taymiyya refutes identifying the intellectual world with the prophetic unseen world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the philosophers' identification of the intellectual world with the unseen world and apply his distinction between mental abstractions and real unseen realities.

Core Idea
The philosophers' 'intellectual world' is a mental construct, not the real unseen world of revelation.

This passage is a direct refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of a dangerous conflation: equating the philosophers' abstract intellectual realm with the unseen world (al-ghayb) that prophets spoke of. Ibn Taymiyya argues that the so-called 'intellectuals' (al-'aqliyyat) exist only in the mind, not externally, whereas the unseen world of God, angels, and Paradise is truly real. We will examine his argument step by step and see how it protects the integrity of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' claim
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states that philosophers like Ibn Sina, al-Shahrastani, and al-Razi claim that the intellectual world is the unseen world of the messengers.
Look for the phrase 'يدعون أن العالم العقلي الذي يثبتونه هو أخبرت به الرسل من الغيب'.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's core objection
Notice his repeated assertion that intellectual entities exist only in the mind ('لا وجود لها إلا في العقل'). This is the key distinction.
He compares them to universals (al-kulliyat) which are mental concepts.
3
Trace the historical error
Ibn Taymiyya outlines how philosophers from Pythagoras to Plato to Aristotle mistakenly projected mental abstractions onto the external world.
Pythagoras: abstract numbers; Plato: universal essences; Aristotle: universals accompanying individuals.
4
Separate what is true from false in their doctrine
Ibn Taymiyya affirms the rational soul (al-nafs al-natiqa) as a self-subsistent entity that survives death, but denies that abstract universals exist externally.
He cites the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus for the soul's reality, showing he does not reject all immaterial realities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فدعواهم أن «الجوهر جنس تحته أربعة وهي العقل، والنفس، والمادة، والصورة ؛ والخامس هو الجسم. إذا حقق الأمر عليهم كان ما يثبتونه من «العقليات» إنما هو موجود في الذهن و العقل» بمنزلة الكليات لا وجود لها في الخارج. وقد اعترف بهذا مَنْ ينصرهم ويعظمهم، كابن حزم وغيره . ومن زعم أن عالم الغيب الذي أخبرَتْ به الرسلُ هو العالم العقلي الذي يثبته هؤلاء فهو من أضل الناس فإن ابن سينا ومن سلك سبيله في هذا، كالشهرستاني والرازي وغيرهما يقولون : إن الإلهيين يثبتون العالم العقلي، ويردُّون على الطبيعيين الذين لا يثبتون إلا العالم الحسي. ويدعون أن العالم العقلي الذي يثبتونه هو أخبرت به الرسل من الغيب الذي أمروا بالإيمان به مثل وجود الرب، والملائكة، والجنة . وليس الأمر كذلك. فإن ما يثبتونه من «العقليات» إذا حقق الأمر لم يكن لها وجود إلا في العقل. وسُمّيت «مجردات» و «مفارقات»، لأن العقل يجرد الأمور الكلية عن المعينات . وأما تسميتها مفارقات فكان أصله أن النفس الناطقة تفارق البدن، وتصير حينئذ «عقلاً». وكانوا يسمون ما جامَعَ المادة بالتدبير لها كالنفس قبل الموت «نفس»، وما فارقها بالكلية فلم يتعلق بها لا تعلق تدبير ولا غيره «عقلاً». ولا ريب أن «النفس الناطقة قائمة بنفسها باقية بعد الموت منعَّمة أو معذَّبة، كما دلّ على ذلك نصوص الكتاب والسنة وإجماع سلف الأمة وأئمتها ؛ ثم تُعاد إلى الأبدان. ومن قال من أهل الكلام: إن النفس عَرَض من أعراض البدن أو جزء من أجزائه» فقوله بدعة، ولم يقل ذلك أحد من سلف الأمة . لكن ما يدعون ثبوته في الخارج من المجردات العقلية» لا يثبت ـ على السبر العقلي - له تحقق إلا في الذهن . وهذا كان مبدأ فلسفة هؤلاء فإنهم نظروا في الأجسام الطبيعية، فعلموا القدر المشترك الكلي، فصاروا يظنون ثبوته في الخارج. فكان أولهم فيثاغورس وشيعته أثبتوا أعداداً مجرَّدة في الخارج. ثم رَدَّ ذلك عليهم أفلاطن وشيعته، وأثبتوا ماهيات كلية مجردة، مثل: إنسان كلي و فرس كلي - أزلي أبدى خارج الذهن. وأثبتوا أيضاً زماناً مجرّداً سموه الدهر»، وأثبتوا مكاناً مجرّداً سموه «الخَلاء»، وأثبتوا مادة مجرّدة عن الصُّوَر ، وهي المادة الأولى» و «الهيولى الأولى» عندهم. فجاء أرسطو وشيعته، فردُّوا ذلك كله عليهم، ولكن أثبتوا هذه «المجردات» في الخارج مقارنةً للأعيان وفرَّقوا بين الشيء الموجود في الخارج، وبين ماهيته الكلية المقارنة لأفرادها في الخارج، كما ذكر ذلك ابن سينا وأمثاله. وغلط هؤلاء في هذا. وكذلك أثبتوا العقول العشرة»، وظنوا وجودها في الخارج وهم غالطون في ذلك،
And also, their claim that 'the genus of substance has under it four: intellect, soul, matter, and form; and the fifth is body. When the matter is examined, what they affirm as 'intellectuals' exist only in the mind and reason, like universals, having no existence in the external world. This has been admitted by those who support and venerate them, such as Ibn Hazm and others. And whoever claims that the unseen world which the messengers informed about is the intellectual world which these people affirm is among the most misguided of people. Indeed, Ibn Sina and those who follow his way in this, such as al-Shahrastani and al-Razi and others, say: The divine philosophers affirm the intellectual world and refute the naturalists who affirm only the sensible world. And they claim that the intellectual world which they affirm is what the messengers informed about of the unseen which they commanded to believe in, such as the existence of the Lord, the angels, and Paradise. But the matter is not so. For what they affirm as 'intellectuals', when the matter is examined, have no existence except in the mind. They are called 'abstracts' and 'separates' because the mind abstracts universal matters from particularized things. As for the name 'separates', its origin is that the rational soul separates from the body and then becomes 'intellect'. They used to call what is attached to matter by managing it, like the soul before death, 'soul'; and what is completely separated from it, so that no attachment of management or other attaches to it, 'intellect'. And there is no doubt that the rational soul is self-subsistent, remaining after death, blissful or punished, as indicated by the texts of the Book and the Sunnah and the consensus of the early community and its imams; then it will be returned to bodies. Whoever among the theologians says that the soul is an accident of the body or a part of its parts, his statement is an innovation, and none of the early community said that. But what they claim to be externally existent of the 'intellectual abstracts' cannot be proven—by rational investigation—to have reality except in the mind. This was the starting point of the philosophy of these people: they looked at natural bodies and knew the common universal share, and then they began to think it exists externally. The first among them was Pythagoras and his followers; they affirmed abstract numbers externally. Then Plato and his followers refuted them and affirmed abstract universal essences, such as universal man and universal horse—eternal and perpetual outside the mind. They also affirmed abstract time which they called 'eternity', and abstract place which they called 'void', and abstract matter devoid of forms, which is 'prime matter' and 'first hyle' in their terminology. Then came Aristotle and his followers, and they refuted all of that, but they affirmed these 'abstracts' in the external world as accompanying individuals, and they distinguished between a thing existing externally and its universal essence accompanying its individuals externally, as mentioned by Ibn Sina and his likes. And these people erred in this. Likewise, they affirmed the 'ten intellects' and thought they existed externally, and they are erroneous in that.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a sustained critique by Ibn Taymiyya of the philosophers' claim that the 'intellectual world' (al-'aql al-kulli) is the same as the unseen world (al-ghayb) mentioned in revelation. He begins with their classification of five substances (jawhar): intellect, soul, matter, form, and body. He argues that when examined, these 'intellectuals' exist only in the mind, like universals, and have no external reality—a point admitted even by their supporters like Ibn Hazm. Ibn Taymiyya then strongly condemns anyone who equates this intellectual world with the prophetic unseen world, as Ibn Sina and his followers do. They claim that what they affirm (like the Lord, angels, Paradise) is what the prophets spoke of, but Ibn Taymiyya refutes this by repeating that these 'intellectuals' are merely mental abstractions. He explains the terminology: they are called 'abstracts' (mujarradat) because the mind abstracts universals, and 'separates' (mufariqat) because the rational soul separates from the body and becomes 'intellect'. He affirms the Islamic doctrine of the soul's survival after death, and rejects the notion that the soul is an accident. He then traces the historical development of philosophical errors: Pythagoras affirmed abstract numbers, Plato affirmed universal essences, and Aristotle affirmed abstract universals as accompanying individuals in the external world—all of which Ibn Taymiyya considers errors. Finally, he mentions the 'ten intellects' (al-'uqul al-'ashara) as another erroneous external reality. The overall argument is that what the philosophers call the 'intellectual world' is merely a mental construct, not the true unseen world of revelation.

المجردات

النفس الناطقة

What is Ibn Taymiyya's main argument against the philosophers' claim that the 'intellectual world' is the unseen world (al-ghayb) of revelation?
A) The philosophers affirm that the unseen world is imaginary.
B) The philosophers' 'intellectuals' exist only in the mind, not in reality, so they cannot be the unseen world of revelation which is real.
C) The philosophers deny the existence of the unseen world altogether.
D) The philosophers claim that the unseen world is the same as the material world.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' intellectual world is a mental construct, not the real unseen world of revelation; Ibn Taymiyya protects the integrity of revealed realities by distinguishing mental abstractions from external existents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate
10 min
Four Quranic Arguments for Bodily Resurrection
Ibn Taymiyya enumerates four Quranic proofs for the possibility of bodily resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four Quranic analogical arguments for bodily resurrection as presented by Ibn Taymiyya and analyze their logical structure.

Core Idea
The Quran uses four analogies to prove bodily resurrection is possible.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya systematically presents four types of Quranic arguments for al-ma'ād (bodily resurrection). He shows that the Quran provides rational, analogical proofs, not mere rhetorical persuasion. Understanding these arguments helps us see how Islamic theology grounds belief in resurrection in observable phenomena and divine power.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four argument types
List the four categories Ibn Taymiyya uses: historical revivals (e.g., Companions of the Cave), the first creation (al-nash'a al-ūlā), the creation of heavens and earth, and the revival of dead land through plants.
Notice each is supported by multiple Quranic verses.
2
Extract the analogical structure for each
For each argument, identify the source case (asl) and the target case (far'). For example, in the plant argument: asl = dead land revived by rain, far' = dead bodies revived by God. The common factor is God's power to give life to what was lifeless.
The analogical reasoning assumes that if God can do the easier (or greater) case, He can do the harder (or lesser).
3
Analyze the underlying principle
Note the principle used: either from greater to lesser (creation of heavens and earth > creation of humans) or from observed to unobserved (first creation known, re-creation similar). This is a form of qiyās in kalam.
Ibn Taymiyya is showing that the Quran's arguments are logically sound.
4
Assess the strength of each argument
Consider which argument might be most compelling to the original audience. The historical example of the Companions of the Cave is a direct observation; the first creation relies on the principle that re-creation is easier; the heavens and earth argument uses scale; the plant argument uses seasonal cycle.
Ibn Taymiyya does not rank them but presents them as equally valid.
5
Connect to broader theological debates
Understand that these arguments refute philosophers and theologians who denied bodily resurrection. The Quran's method is simple and certain, contrasting with speculative arguments.
This passage is from Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians.

Source Text

المقام الرابع - الوجه الثاني : طريقة القرآن في بيان إمكان المعاد
إِلَيْكَ ثُمَّ اجْعَلْ عَلَى كُلِّ جَبَلٍ مِنْهُنَّ جُزْءًا ثُمَّ ادْعُهُنَّ يَأْتِينَكَ سَعْيًا وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ اللَّهَ عَزِيزٌ حَكِيمٌ ) [سورة البقرة : ٢٦٠]. وكما أخبر عن المسيح عليه السلام أنه كان يُحيي الموتى بإذن الله
قصة أصحاب الكهف وكما أخبر عن أصحاب الكهف أنهم : لَبِثُوا - نياما - و فِي كَهْفِهِمْ ثَلَاثَ مِائَةٍ سنين وَازْدَادُوا تِسْعًا ﴾ [سورة الكهف : ٢٥] وقال تعالى: ﴿وَكَذَلِكَ أَعْثَرْنَا عَلَيْهِمْ لِيَعْلَمُوا أَنَّ وَعْدَ اللَّهِ حَقٌّ وَأَنَّ السَّاعَةَ لَا رَيْبَ فِيهَا إِذْ يَتَنَزَعُونَ بَيْنَهُمْ أَمْرَهُمْ ﴾ [سورة الكهف : ٢١]. وقد ذَكَر غيرُ واحدٍ من العلماء أن الناس كانوا قد تنازعوا في زمانهم هل يبعث الله الأرواح فقط ، أو يبعث الأرواح والأجساد؟ فأعثر الله هؤلاء على أهل الكهف، وعلموا أنهم بقوا نياماً لا يأكلون ولا يشربون ثلاث مئة سنة شمسية، وهي ثلاث مئة وتسع هلالية فأعلمهم الله بذلك إمكانَ إعادة الأبدان / فهذه إحدى الطرق التي يبين الله بها إمكان المعاد . وتارة يستدل على ذلك بالنشأة الأولى، وأن الإعادة أهون من الابتداء، كما في الثانية : قوله : [ يَتَأَيُّهَا النَّاسُ] إن كُنتُمْ فِي رَيْبٍ مِنَ الْبَعْثِ فَإِنَّا خَلَقْنَاكُم مِّن تُرَابٍ ثُمَّ مِن نُّطْفَةٍ ثُمَّ مِنْ عَلَقَةٍ ثُمَّ مِن مُضْغَةٍ تُخَلَّقَةٍ وَغَيْرِ مُخَلَّقَةٍ لِنُبَيِّنَ لَكُمْ ﴾ [سورة الحج: ٥]. وكما في قوله: وَضَرَبَ لَنَا مَثَلًا وَنَسِيَ خَلْقَةُ قَالَ مَن يُحْيِ الْعِظَمَ وَهِيَ رَمِيمٌ ) قُلْ يُحْيِيهَا الَّذِي أَنشَأَهَا أَوَّلَ مَرَّةٍ وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ خَلْقٍ عَلِيمٌ ﴾ [سورة يس : ۷۸ - ۷۹]، وكما في قوله : [ وَقَالُوا] أَعِذَا كُنَّا عِظَمًا وَرُفَتَنَّا أَي نَا لَمَبْعُوثُونَ خَلْقًا جَدِيدًا الله قُل كُونُوا حِجَارَةً أَوْ حَدِيدًا له أَوْ خَلْقًا مِمَّا يَكْبُرُ فِي صُدُورِكُمْ فَسَيَقُولُونَ من يُعِيدُنَا قُلِ الَّذِى فَطَرَكُمْ أَوَّلَ مَرَّةً ﴾ [سورة الإسراء : ٤٩ - ٥١]، وكما في قوله : وَهُوَ الَّذِى يَبْدَوُا الْخَلْقَ ثُمَّ يُعِيدُهُ وَهُوَ أَهْوَنُ عَلَيْهِ ﴾ [سورة الروم : ۲۷] .
وتارةً يَستدل على إمكان ذلك بخلق السموات والأرض، فإن خلقها أعظم من إعادة الإنسان، كما في قوله : ﴿ وَقَالُواْ أَعِذَا كُنَّا عِظَمًا وَرُفَتًا أَعِنَّا لَمَبْعُوثُونَ خَلْقًا جَدِيدًا أَوَلَمْ يَرَوْا أَنَّ اللَّهَ الَّذِي خَلَقَ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ قَادِرُ عَلَى أَن يَخلُقَ مِثْلَهُمْ وَجَعَلَ لَهُمْ أَجَلًا لَّا رَيْبَ فِيهٍ ﴾ [سورة الإسراء : ۹۸ - ۹۹]، وكما في قوله تعالى: ﴿ أَوَلَيْسَ الَّذِي خَلَقَ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ بِقَدِرٍ عَلَى أَن يَخلُقَ مِثْلَهُمْ بَلَى وَهُوَ الْخَلَّقُ الْعَلِيمُ )) [سورة يس : ۸۱]، وقوله : أَوَلَمْ يَرَوْا أَنَّ اللهَ الَّذِى خَلَقَ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ وَلَمْ يَعْيَ بِخَلْفِهِنَّ بِقَدِرٍ عَلَى أَن يُحْتَى الْمَوْنَ بَلَى إِنَّهُ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرٌ [سورة الأحقاف : ٣٣] .
الرابعة : وتارة يستدل على إمكانه بخلق النبات، كما في قوله: ﴿ وَهُوَ الَّذِي يُرْسِلُ الرِّيحَ بشرا بين يَدَى رَحْمَنِهِ حَتَّى إِذَا أَقَلَتْ سَحَابًا ثِقَالًا سُقْتَهُ لِبَلَو مَيْت فَأَنزَلْنَا بِهِ الْمَاءَ فَأَخْرَجْنَا بِهِ مِن كُلِّ الثَّمَرَاتِ كَذَلِكَ يُخْرِجُ الْمَوْتَى لَعَلَّكُمْ تَذَكَرُونَ ﴾ [سورة الأعراف : ٥٧]، وكما في قوله : ( وَاللَّهُ الَّذِي أَرْسَلَ الرِّيحَ فَتُثِيرُ مَحَابًا فَسُفْتَهُ إِلَى بَلَدٍ مَيْتِ فَأَحْيَيْنَا بِهِ الْأَرْضَ بَعْدَ مَوْتِهَا كَذَلِكَ النُّشُورُ [سورة فاطر: ۹] .
The Fourth Station - The Second Aspect: The Quranic Method of Demonstrating the Possibility of Resurrection. ...to you, then place on each mountain a part of them, then call them; they will come to you rushing. And know that Allah is Mighty, Wise (Q 2:260). And as He informed about the Messiah, peace be upon him, that he used to revive the dead by Allah's permission. And as He informed about the Companions of the Cave: that they stayed in their cave for three hundred years and added nine (Q 18:25). And He said: And thus We caused them to be found that they might know that the promise of Allah is true and that the Hour, there is no doubt about it (Q 18:21). And more than one scholar mentioned that the people in their time disputed whether Allah resurrects only the souls, or both souls and bodies. So Allah made them find the Companions of the Cave, and they knew that they remained asleep without eating or drinking for three hundred solar years, which is three hundred and nine lunar years. So Allah informed them thereby of the possibility of the restoration of bodies. So this is one of the ways by which Allah demonstrates the possibility of the resurrection. And sometimes He argues for that by the first creation, and that the repetition is easier than the beginning, as in the second: His statement: O mankind, if you are in doubt about the resurrection, then indeed We created you from dust, then from a sperm-drop, then from a clinging clot, then from a lump of flesh, formed and unformed, that We may make clear to you (Q 22:5). And as in His statement: And he strikes for Us an example and forgets his own creation. He says, 'Who will give life to bones while they are decayed?' Say, 'He will give them life who produced them the first time; and He is, of every creation, Knowing' (Q 36:78-79). And as in His statement: And they say, 'When we are bones and crumbled dust, will we indeed be resurrected as a new creation?' Say, 'Be stones or iron or [any] creation that is greater in your breasts.' Then they will say, 'Who will return us?' Say, 'He who created you the first time' (Q 17:49-51). And as in His statement: And He it is who begins creation; then He repeats it, and it is easier for Him (Q 30:27). And sometimes He argues for the possibility of that by the creation of the heavens and the earth, for their creation is greater than the re-creation of man, as in His statement: And they say, 'When we are bones and crumbled dust, will we indeed be resurrected as a new creation?' Do they not see that Allah, who created the heavens and the earth, is able to create the likes of them? And He has appointed for them a term, about which there is no doubt (Q 17:98-99). And as in His statement: Is not He who created the heavens and the earth able to create the likes of them? Yes, and He is the Knowing Creator (Q 36:81). And His statement: Do they not see that Allah, who created the heavens and the earth and did not tire in their creation, is able to give life to the dead? Yes, indeed, He is over all things competent (Q 46:33). The fourth: And sometimes He argues for its possibility by the creation of plants, as in His statement: And He it is who sends the winds as good tidings before His mercy. Until when they have carried heavy clouds, We drive them to a dead land and send down rain therein and bring forth thereby [some] of all the fruits. Thus will We bring forth the dead; perhaps you may be reminded (Q 7:57). And as in His statement: And Allah is He who sends the winds, and they stir the clouds, and We drive them to a dead land and give life thereby to the earth after its lifelessness. Thus is the resurrection (Q 35:9).
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a classification and exposition of four Quranic methods for proving the possibility of bodily resurrection. Ibn Taymiyya presents them as part of a broader argument that the Quran provides sufficient rational proofs. The first method uses historical examples of God reviving the dead, such as Abraham's birds and Jesus raising the dead, and especially the Companions of the Cave, who remained asleep for 300 years and were revived, demonstrating that bodies can be preserved and restored. The second method argues from the first creation: since God created humans from nothing initially, re-creation is easier. The third method argues from the greater creation of the heavens and earth: if God can create the immense cosmos, recreating a human body is trivial. The fourth method uses the analogy of reviving dead land through rain and plant growth: just as dead land comes to life, so will dead bodies. Each argument is supported by Quranic verses, and Ibn Taymiyya presents them as clear, certain proofs that are superior to the convoluted arguments of philosophers and theologians.

Key Terms
المعاد
إمكان
النشأة الأولى
خلق السموات والأرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the four Quranic arguments for resurrection mentioned by Ibn Taymiyya?
A) The argument from the first creation
B) The argument from the creation of the heavens and earth
C) The argument from the creation of the soul
D) The argument from the revival of dead land through plants

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that the Quran provides four distinct analogical proofs for bodily resurrection, each rooted in observable phenomena, thus rebutting the philosophers' claim that resurrection is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the analogical structure in the argument from the first creation (al-nash'a al-ūlā) using the Quranic verse Q 36:78-79. State the asl, far', and illa.
?
Compare the strength of the argument from heavens and earth with the argument from plant growth. Based on the passage, which one does Ibn Taymiyya seem to emphasize more and why?

Critique of Philosophers' Negative Attributes
Critique of philosophical negative attributes about Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's argument against philosophical negative attributes and explain why they are considered corrupt and epistemologically harmful.

Core Idea
Negative attributes about Allah that don't imply perfection can imply non-existence.

Ibn Taymiyya launches a sharp critique against philosophers who describe Allah using negative propositions like 'He is neither separate from creation nor adjacent.' He argues that such descriptions are not only imperfect but could also apply to non-existent or impossible things. This lesson dissects the logical and theological flaws in that approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' claim
Locate the specific negative attributes Ibn Taymiyya attributes to them: 'He is neither separate (bā'in) nor adjacent (mujānib).'
2
Analyze the critique
Ibn Taymiyya argues these attributes are 'qadāyā salbiyya' that do not convey perfection and can be shared by non-existent and impossible things. Understand why he labels them corrupt.
Note the logical problem: they could imply combining opposites (jam' bayn al-naqīdayn).
3
Examine the epistemological harm
The philosophers aim to use these false propositions as 'uṣūl 'aqliyya' (rational principles) to oppose revelation and innate knowledge (fiṭrah). This shows the broader danger.
4
Contrast with the Quranic method
Ibn Taymiyya hints that the Quran's method is superior. Reflect on how the Quran describes Allah with positive attributes of perfection.
He asks: 'Where are their ways from the method of the Quran?'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأين طرق هؤلاء في إثبات الإمكان الخارجي من طريقة القرآن؟

ثم إنهم بمثل هذه الطرق الفاسدة يريدون خروج الناس عما فُطروا عليه من المعارف اليقينية والبراهين العقلية وما جاءت به الرسل من الأخبار الإلهية واليوم الآخر.

ويثبتوا لرب العالمين من الوجود ما يستلزم الجمع بين النقيضين، فيكذبوا بصريح المعقول وصحيح المنقول، كقولهم لا هو مبائن للمخلوقات، ولا مجانب لها يُشار إليه، ونحو ذلك من القضايا السلبية التي يَصِفُون بها ربَّ العالمين مما لا يتضمن وصفه بصفة كمال بل يُشاركه فيها الممتنعات والمعدومات، وتستلزم كون الموصوف بها معدوماً، بل ممتنعاً. ويريدون أن يجعلوا مثل هذه القضايا الكاذبة والخَيالات الفاسدة أصولاً عقلية يُعارض بها ما أرسل الله به رُسُله وأنزل به كتبه من الآيات، وما فَطَر الله عليه عباده، وما تقوم عليه الأدلة العقلية التي لا شُبهة فيها.

ولهذا كان أساطينُ الفلاسفة القدماء وكثير من المتأخرين منهم على خلاف قول هؤلاء التفاة، وكانوا أقرب إلى موافقة الأنبياء وأتباع الأنبياء من هؤلاء النفاة من المتفلسفة والجهمية والمتكلمة، كما قد بسطت أقوالهم في غير هذا الموضع.

والمقصود هنا التنبيه على أصول سَلَّموها، أفسدوا بها العلوم العقلية والسمعية. فإن مبنى العقل على صحة الفطرة وسلامتها ومبنى السمع على تصديق الأنبياء -

المقام الرابع - محاولتهم معارضة الفطر وتعاليم الرسل

كون تعليم الأنبياء جامعاً للأدلة العقلية والسمعية جميعاً

ثم الأنبياء صلوات الله عليهم كَمَّلوا للناس الأمرين، فدلوهم على الأدلة العقلية التي بها تُعلم المطالب الإلهية التي يمكنهم علمهم بها بالنظر والاستدلال، وأخبروهم مع ذلك من تفاصيل الغيب بما يَعْجزون عن معرفته بمجرد نظرهم واستدلالهم. وليس تعليم الأنبياء صلوات الله عليهم مقصوراً على مجرد الخبر، كما يظنه كثير من النظار. بل هم بينوا من البراهين العقلية التي بها تعلم العلوم الإلهية ما لا يوجد عند هؤلاء ألبتة. فتعليمهم صلوات الله عليهم جامع للأدلة العقلية والسمعية جميعاً بخلاف الذين خالفوهم فإن تعليمهم غير مفيد للأدلة العقلية والسمعية مع ما في نفوسهم من الكبر الذي ما هم ببالغيه، كما قال تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ يُجَادِلُونَ فِي آيَاتِ اللَّهِ بِغَيْرِ سُلْطَانٍ أَتَاهُمْ إِنْ فِي صُدُورِهِمْ إِلَّا كِبَرٌ مَا هُمْ بِبَالِغِيهِ فَاسْتَعِذْ بِاللَّهِ إِنَّهُ هُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ ﴾ [سورة غافر: ٥٦]، وقال: ﴿ الَّذِينَ يُجَادِلُونَ فِي آيَاتِ اللَّهِ بِغَيْرِ سُلْطَانٍ أَتَاهُمْ كَبُرَ مَقْتًا عِنْدَ اللَّهِ وَعِنْدَ الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا كَذَلِكَ يَطْبَعُ اللَّهُ عَلَى كُلِّ قَلْبٍ مُتَكَبِّرٍ جَبَّارٍ ﴾ [سورة غافر: ٣٥]، وقال تعالى: ﴿ فَلَمَّا جَاءَتْهُمْ رُسُلُهُمْ بِالْبَيِّنَاتِ فَرِحُوا بِمَا عِنْدَهُمْ مِنَ الْعِلْمِ وَحَاقَ بِهِمْ مَا كَانُوا بِهِ يَسْتَهْزِئُونَ ﴾ [سورة غافر: ٣]. ومثل هذا كثير في القرآن وقد بسطنا القول فيه في بيان دَرْءِ تعارض الشرع والعقل
Where are the ways of these people in proving external possibility from the method of the Quran? Then they, with such corrupt methods, want to turn people away from what they were created upon of certain knowledges and rational proofs and what the messengers brought of divine reports and the Last Day. And they establish for the Lord of the worlds an existence that necessitates the combination of opposites, thus they deny clear reason and sound transmitted report, like their saying: He is neither separate from created things nor adjacent to them that He can be pointed to, and similar negative propositions with which they describe the Lord of the worlds, which do not include describing Him with a perfection attribute, but rather the impossible and non-existent things share in it, and they necessitate that the one described with them is non-existent, rather impossible. And they want to make such false propositions and corrupt fantasies into rational principles to oppose what Allah sent His messengers with and revealed His books of signs, and what Allah created His servants upon, and what the rational proofs that have no ambiguity are based on. For this reason, the leading ancient philosophers and many of the later ones were contrary to the statement of these negators, and they were closer to agreement with the prophets and followers of the prophets than these negators among the philosophers, Jahmiyya, and speculative theologians, as I have expanded their statements elsewhere. And the purpose here is to alert to principles they have conceded, by which they corrupted the rational and revealed sciences. For the basis of reason is the soundness and integrity of the innate disposition, and the basis of revelation is belief in the prophets. The fourth station - their attempt to oppose the innate dispositions and teachings of the messengers. The teaching of the prophets is comprehensive of both rational and revealed proofs. Then the prophets, peace be upon them, perfected both matters for people: they guided them to the rational proofs by which the divine objectives that they can know by reflection and inference are known, and they informed them along with that of details of the unseen that they are incapable of knowing by their own reflection and inference alone. And the teaching of the prophets, peace be upon them, is not limited to mere report, as many thinkers suppose. Rather, they elucidated from the rational proofs by which the divine sciences are learned what is not found with these people at all. So their teaching, peace be upon them, is comprehensive of both rational and revealed proofs, unlike those who opposed them, for their teaching is not beneficial for rational and revealed proofs, along with what is in their souls of pride that they will not attain, as Allah said: 'Indeed, those who argue concerning the signs of Allah without any authority having come to them - there is not in their breasts except pride that they will never attain. So seek refuge in Allah. Indeed, He is the Hearing, the Seeing.' [Surah Ghafir: 56], and He said: 'Those who argue concerning the signs of Allah without any authority having come to them - it is greatly hateful to Allah and to those who believe. Thus does Allah seal upon every arrogant tyrant heart.' [Surah Ghafir: 35], and He said: 'And when their messengers came to them with clear proofs, they rejoiced in what they had of knowledge, and they were enveloped by that which they used to ridicule.' [Surah Ghafir: 3]. And much like this is in the Quran, and I have expanded the discussion on it in the book 'Dar' Ta'arud al-Shar' wa al-'Aql'.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the philosophers' method and an affirmation of the comprehensive nature of prophetic teaching. Ibn Taymiyya begins by contrasting the Quranic method with that of the philosophers in proving external possibility (al-imkan al-khariji), implying the Quran's method is superior. He then accuses the philosophers of using corrupt methods to turn people away from innate certain knowledge (ma'arif yaqiniyya) and rational proofs (barahin aqliyya) brought by the prophets. Specifically, they attribute to God a kind of existence that implies combining opposites (jam' bayn al-naqidayn), such as saying He is neither separate from creation nor adjacent, which are negative attributes (qadaya salbiyya) that do not convey perfection and could even apply to non-existent or impossible things. The philosophers aim to elevate these false propositions to rational principles (usul 'aqliyya) to oppose revelation (ma arsala Allah bihi rusulahu) and innate disposition (fitra). Ibn Taymiyya notes that the leading ancient philosophers and many later ones disagreed with these negators (nafat), being closer to agreement with the prophets. The goal of this discussion is to expose the corrupt principles that have ruined both rational and revealed sciences, emphasizing that reason is founded on sound fitra and revelation on belief in the prophets. The prophet's teaching is comprehensive: they provided rational proofs for divine matters (matailib ilahiyya) accessible through reflection, and also revealed details of the unseen (tafasil al-ghayb) beyond human reasoning. Contrary to the claim that prophets only conveyed reports (khabar), they actually elucidated rational proofs (barahin aqliyya) for divine sciences not found with the philosophers. Thus, prophetic teaching unites rational and revealed proofs, unlike those who opposed them, whose teaching is futile and stems from pride that will never achieve its goal. Ibn Taymiyya supports this with Quranic verses condemning those who argue without authority, illustrating that such pride is condemned.

Key Terms
الإمكان الخارجي
الفطرة
القضايا السلبية
الجمع بين النقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the problem with describing Allah as 'neither separate from creation nor adjacent'?
A) It is a correct negation that upholds tawhid
B) It is a negative proposition that does not convey perfection and could apply to non-existent things
C) It is a positive attribute that shows Allah's transcendence
D) It is a statement that only philosophers understand

Key Takeaway
Negative descriptions of Allah that do not affirm a perfection are not true attributes and can imply non-existence; only positive perfections based on revelation and fitra are valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a contemporary Muslim philosophical argument that describes Allah using only negative terms (e.g., 'He cannot be constrained by time or space'). Using Ibn Taymiyya's criteria, determine whether this argument is valid or falls into the same error.
?
Compare the Quranic description of Allah as 'al-Ẓāhir wal-Bāṭin' (the Manifest and the Hidden) with the philosophers' 'not separate, not adjacent'. Explain why the Quranic example is a positive description of perfection.

Prophetic Teaching: Uniting Rational and Revealed Proofs
Assertion that prophetic teaching unites rational and revealed proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe how prophetic teaching integrates rational and revealed proofs and contrast it with the philosophical method.

Core Idea
Prophets taught both rational proofs for divine matters and revealed details of the unseen.

Ibn Taymiyya asserts that the prophets' teaching is not limited to mere reports; they provided rational proofs (barahin aqliyya) for divine sciences and also revealed unseen details beyond human reach. This lesson explores that comprehensive method and its superiority over philosophical speculation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states that prophetic teaching is comprehensive of both rational and revealed proofs. Note the exact phrase: 'جامع للأدلة العقلية والسمعية جميعاً'.
2
Analyze the two components
Separate what prophets provided: (1) rational proofs for divine objectives accessible through reflection, and (2) details of the unseen (tafasil al-ghayb) beyond human reason.
This refutes the idea that prophets only gave reports without rational arguments.
3
Contrast with philosophers
Ibn Taymiyya says philosophers' teaching is not beneficial for either rational or revealed proofs, and it stems from pride. Note the Quranic verses he quotes.
The verses condemn those who argue without authority, highlighting pride.
4
Assess the epistemological foundation
Understand that the basis of reason is sound fitra and the basis of revelation is belief in prophets. Prophetic teaching harmonizes both.

Demonstration -- Burhan
advanced
10 min
Reasoning from Universals to Particulars: Hidden to Manifest
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the epistemic priority of universal demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that syllogistic demonstration is epistemically inferior to analogical reasoning and identify his distinction between certain and empirical premises.

Core Idea
Demonstration from universals is not the clearest proof—it is reasoning from the hidden to the manifest.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' hierarchy of knowledge. He argues that the supposed universal premises of demonstration are actually less clear than the particular conclusions they are meant to prove. In this passage, he exposes the epistemic inversion at the heart of the syllogistic method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central thesis
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that reasoning from universals to particulars is reasoning from the hidden (al-khafi) to the manifest (al-jali). Note how he reverses the expected order of clarity.
2
Examine his grounding claim
He argues that universal premises are themselves known through analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil), not through the universal syllogism. The individuals are known without the syllogism.
3
Analyze the distinction between true and empirical demonstration
True demonstration uses certain, necessary premises; ordinary empirical premises (natural, medical, grammatical) are subject to exceptions, making them dialectical or rhetorical, not demonstrative.
4
Assess his response to the objection about speaker's intent
When asked about certainty of the Prophet's intent, Ibn Taymiyya replies that it is particular, not universal, knowledge and thus not part of demonstration.
This shows that certainty about particular cases does not require universal syllogistic proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكليات الاستدلال بالكليات على أفرادها استدلال بالخفي على الجلي والمقصود أن الذي يدَّعونه من الكليات هو إذا كان علماً فهو مما يُعرف بـ «قياس التمثيل»، لا يقف على القياس المنطقي الشمولي أصلاً. بل ما يدعون توقفه على هذا القياس تُعلَم أفراده التي يُستدل عليها بدون هذا القياس; ذلك أيسر وأسهل. فيكون الاستدلال عليها بالقياس الذي يسمونه «البرهاني» استدلالاً على الأجلى بالأخفى .
وإذا قيل : فالبرهان يفيد قضية كلية قيل : أما البرهان الذي يستحق اسم البرهان عندهم، وهو ما كان مؤلفاً من المقدمات اليقينية المحضة الواجب قبولها التي يمتنع نقيضها، فإنها بهذه المنزلة . وأما ما لا يكون كذلك، بل يكون مؤلفاً من القضايا التجربية العادية كالقضايا الطبيعية والطبية والنحوية ونحو ذلك، فهذه كثيراً ما تكون منتقضة، ولا يجزم العقلُ بامتناع انتقاضها إلا بشروط. فإن العاديات يجوز انتقاضها، والقضية الكلية إذا جاز انتقاضها لم يكن عندهم مادّة للبرهان، بل للجدل أو الخطابة .
فإن قيل : وأنتم تجزمون بمراد المتكلم بكلامه في غالب المواضع، كما تجزمون بمراد الرسول ومراد علماء الشرع والطب والنحو ; والعلم باللغات مبناه على القضايا العادية، قيل : الجزم بمراد الشخص المعين ليس هو قضية كلية، بل هو علم بمراد شخص معين . وهذا وإن كان علمنا بلغته وعادته هو مما يُعين على العلم بمراده فلا بد مع ذلك من علم يَختص به يُعرّف أنه إنما تكلمه بتلك العادة ليُفْهِمَنا مُرادَه . وحينئذ فليس هذا مما نحن فيه . ولهذا لا نستدل على هذا بمجرد ما ذكروه من برهانهم .
وهم يعيبون في صناعة الحد أن يعرَّف الجلي بالخفي، وهذا في صناعة البرهان أشدُّ عيباً. فإن البرهان لا يُراد به إلا بيانُ المدلول عليه وتعريفه وكشفه وإيضاحه. فإذا كان هو أوضح وأظهر كان هذا بياناً للجلي بالخفي. وأما «الحدّ» فالصواب أن المراد به التمييز بينه وبين المحدود لا تعريفُ الماهية. وإذا كان مطلوبه التمييز فقد يكون المميز أخفى، وقد يكون أجلى .
ومع هذا فلما كان الجلاء والخفاء من الأمور النسبية فقد ينتفع بالدليل الخفي بعض الناس وفي المناظرة
The universals: reasoning from universals to their individuals is reasoning from the hidden to the manifest. And the intent is that what they claim of universals, if it is known, then it is known through analogical syllogism, not dependent on the universal logical syllogism at all. Rather, what they claim depends on this syllogism: its individuals are known without this syllogism; that is easier and simpler. So reasoning upon them by the syllogism they call 'demonstrative' is reasoning from the more manifest to the more hidden.
If it is said: The demonstration yields a universal proposition, it is said: As for the demonstration that deserves the name demonstration according to them, which is composed of certain premises that must be accepted whose contradictory is impossible, then it is in this rank. But as for what is not like that, being composed of ordinary empirical propositions like natural, medical, grammatical cases, these are often contradictory, and the mind does not judge the impossibility of their contradiction except with conditions. For the customary are subject to contradiction, and the universal proposition, if its contradiction is possible, is not for them matter of demonstration, but of dialectic or rhetoric.
If it is said: Yet you are certain of the intent of the speaker in most cases, as you are certain of the intent of the Messenger and the intent of scholars of law, medicine, and grammar; and knowledge of languages is based on customary propositions, it is said: Certainty of the intent of a specific person is not a universal proposition, but it is knowledge of the intent of a specific person. And even though our knowledge of his language and habit helps in knowing his intent, there must also be specific knowledge that informs that he only spoke with that habit to make us understand his intent. Therefore, this is not our subject. And for this reason we do not rely on this merely by what they mentioned of their demonstration.
And they criticize in the art of definition that the manifest be defined by the hidden, and this in the art of demonstration is a more severe defect. For demonstration is only intended to explain the indicated, define it, uncover it, and clarify it. So if it is clearer and more manifest, this would be a clarification of the manifest by the hidden. As for definition, the correct view is that its purpose is distinction between it and the defined, not definition of the essence. And if its goal is distinction, then the distinguisher may be more hidden, or more manifest.
And with this, since clarity and hiddenness are relative matters, the hidden proof may benefit some people and in debate.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against the logicians' claim that syllogistic demonstration from universals to particulars is the highest form of knowledge. Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating that reasoning from universals to their individuals is actually reasoning from the hidden to the manifest. He argues that the supposed universal premises are themselves known through analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil), not through the universal syllogism. Moreover, the individuals one seeks to prove are already known without that syllogism, making the syllogistic inference unnecessary and actually less clear. He then addresses an expected objection: that demonstration yields universal propositions. He distinguishes between true demonstration (composed of certain, necessary premises) and ordinary empirical premises (such as those in natural sciences, medicine, grammar) which are often subject to exceptions and thus only yield dialectical or rhetorical arguments. Next, he anticipates an objection: that Muslims are certain of the intent of the Messenger and scholars based on customary propositions (e.g., language). He replies that certainty about a specific person's intent is not a universal proposition but a particular knowledge, supplemented by contextual indications, so it is not part of the universal syllogistic demonstration. Finally, he points out an inconsistency: logicians criticize defining the manifest by the hidden in the art of definition (hadd), but their own demonstration does the same when it infers particulars from universals. He argues that definition should aim at distinction, not essential definition, so it may permissibly use hidden terms. He concludes that clarity is relative, so hidden proofs can still be useful in certain contexts.

Key Terms
برهان
قياس التمثيل
مقدمات يقينية
قضية كلية
جدل
خطابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the actual basis for the universal premises claimed by logicians?
A) Intuitive axioms
B) Analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil)
C) Empirical observation
D) Divine revelation

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya inverts the logicians' hierarchy: universal syllogistic demonstration is not the most manifest proof; particular knowledge through analogical reasoning is clearer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: In a debate with a logician, you are asked to prove that the world is temporally originated. The logician says: use a universal syllogism with the premise 'everything temporally originated has a cause.' Ibn Taymiyya would argue that this universal premise itself is known from observing particular temporal events. Write a brief response (2-3 sentences) explaining why Ibn Taymiyya would say the syllogistic proof is not the clearest route, but analogical reasoning is.
?
Task: Identify whether the following statement is a universal proposition or particular knowledge according to Ibn Taymiyya: 'The Prophet intended to abrogate the previous law.' Justify your answer using the passage.

The Purpose of Definition: Distinction, Not Essence
Ibn Taymiyya redefines the purpose of definition and exposes the logicians' inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's revision of the purpose of definition (hadd) and identify the logical inconsistency he finds in the logicians' stance on clarity.

Core Idea
Definition is for distinguishing, not revealing essences—and the logicians' own demonstration violates their rule about clarity.

Ibn Taymiyya turns his critical eye to the art of definition. He argues that the logicians' criticism of defining the clear by the hidden is hypocritical, since their demonstration does the same. He then offers an alternative: definition is about discrimination, not essential definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' rule on definition
They criticize defining the manifest (jali) by the hidden (khafi). Understand what this rule means: the definiens should be clearer than the definiendum.
2
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's critique of demonstration
He says that demonstration is even more blameworthy because it aims to clarify but often uses hidden premises to prove manifest conclusions. This is an inconsistency.
3
Learn Ibn Taymiyya's alternative conception of definition
Definition (hadd) should serve to distinguish (tamyiz) the defined from others, not to reveal its essence. Therefore, using hidden terms in definition is permissible if it aids distinction.
4
Grapple with the relativity of clarity
Clarity and hiddenness are relative; a hidden proof may benefit some people in a debate.

Source Text

Demonstration -- Burhan
advanced
5 min
Ibn Taymiyya’s Critique: Reasoning from Hidden to Manifest
Ibn Taymiyya argues that using universals to know particulars is reasoning from hidden to manifest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya’s objection to the demonstrative syllogism for particulars and evaluate its epistemological implications.

Core Idea
Using universals to prove particulars is reasoning from hidden to manifest.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges a core assumption of Aristotelian logic: that demonstrative syllogisms provide certain knowledge. He argues that when you use a universal premise to deduce a particular, you are actually moving from what is more hidden (the universal) to what is more manifest (the particular). This inverses the intended order of demonstration. In this lesson, we will unpack his argument and test its validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states that reasoning from universals to particulars is from hidden to manifest.
The phrase 'استدلال بالخفي على الجلي' is key.
2
Understand the epistemological hierarchy
According to Ibn Taymiyya, the particular is more manifest (jali) and the universal is more hidden (khafi). Why? The particular is known directly through sense or text, while the universal requires abstraction.
3
Apply to a real example
Consider the universal 'every intoxicant is forbidden'. The particular 'nabidh is forbidden' – how do we know the universal? Is it more hidden than the particular?
Ibn Taymiyya would say the universal is derived from particular texts, making it less direct.
4
Evaluate the critique
Does Ibn Taymiyya’s argument hold? It does if we accept that particulars are known first. But logicians might argue universals are not always hidden; they can be axioms.
This leads to a debate about the source of universal premises.
5
Connect to Islamic sciences
How does this critique affect proofs in kalam, like the contingency argument? The universal 'everything contingent has a cause' – is it more hidden than the conclusion 'the world has a cause'?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع - الاستدلال بالكليات على أفرادها استدلال بالخفي على الجلي على هذا القياس تُعلَم أفراده التي يُستدل عليها بدون هذا القياس؛ وذلك أيسر وأسهل. فيكون الاستدلال عليها بالقياس الذي يسمونه «البرهاني» استدلالاً على الأجلى بالأخفى.
The fourth station - Reasoning from universals to their particulars is reasoning from hidden to manifest. By this reasoning, its particulars which are known without this reasoning are known; and that is easier and simpler. Thus, reasoning upon them by the syllogism they call 'demonstrative' is reasoning about the more manifest by the more hidden.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument. Ibn Taymiyya starts by labeling the station: using universals to deduce particulars is reasoning from the hidden (the universal) to the manifest (the particular). He notes that the particular can be known without the syllogism, directly and more easily. Therefore, employing the so-called demonstrative syllogism to reach the particular is actually reasoning about what is more manifest (the particular) by means of what is more hidden (the universal), which contradicts the purpose of demonstration to clarify.

Key Terms
كليات
أفراد
برهاني
خفي
جلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, when you deduce that nabidh is forbidden from the universal 'every intoxicant is forbidden', what is the epistemological status of the premise relative to the conclusion?
A) The premise (universal) is more manifest than the conclusion (particular).
B) The premise is more hidden than the conclusion.
C) Both are equally manifest.
D) The premise is certain while the conclusion is probable.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that using universal premises to deduce particular conclusions reverses the order of knowledge, making the manifest dependent on the hidden.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the example from Usul: every intoxicant is forbidden. Ibn Taymiyya would say the particular rulings for each intoxicant are known directly. Identify a contemporary fiqh issue where a universal premise is used to deduce a ruling for a new case. Evaluate whether Ibn Taymiyya's critique applies.
?
In theology, the argument for God's existence from contingency often uses a universal premise 'every contingent being has a cause'. According to Ibn Taymiyya, is this universal premise more manifest or more hidden than the conclusion that the world has a cause? Explain.

What Makes a Demonstration True?
Definition of true demonstration by its premise conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions that a syllogism must satisfy to qualify as a true demonstration (burhan) according to the logicians.

Core Idea
Demonstration requires premises whose contradiction is impossible.

The logicians distinguish true demonstration from other forms of argument by the certainty of its premises. In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya reports the logicians' strict definition: a demonstration worthy of the name must be composed of purely certain premises that are impossible to negate. Let's break down what this means and why it matters.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find where Ibn Taymiyya says 'the demonstration that deserves the name of demonstration with them'. Identify the key condition: it is composed of certain premises.
The phrase 'المقدمات اليقينية المحضة' is the core.
2
Identify the type of premises
What are 'purely certain premises'? They are propositions that are necessarily true, obligatory to accept, and whose contradiction is impossible.
Such premises are foundational, like first principles.
3
Understand the scope
Why is impossibility of contradiction crucial? Because if a premise could be false, the conclusion is not certain. Demonstration must yield absolute certainty.
4
Contrast with empirical premises
Note that Ibn Taymiyya immediately contrasts these with empirical premises. This highlights the condition: empirical premises are not impossible to contradict, so they fall outside demonstration.
This sets up the classification of arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قيل : فالبرهان يفيد قضية كلية قيل : أما البرهان الذي يستحق اسم البرهان عندهم، وهو ما كان مؤلفاً من المقدمات اليقينية المحضة الواجب قبولها التي يمتنع نقيضها، فإنها بهذه المنزلة . وأما ما لا يكون كذلك، بل يكون مؤلفاً من القضايا التجربية العادية كالقضايا الطبيعية والطبية والنحوية ونحو ذلك، فهذه كثيراً ما تكون منتقضة، ولا يجزم العقلُ بامتناع انتقاضها إلا بشروط. فإن العاديات يجوز انتقاضها، والقضية الكلية إذا جاز انتقاضها لم يكن عندهم مادّة للبرهان، بل للجدل أو الخطابة .
And if it is said: The demonstration yields a universal proposition, it is said: As for the demonstration that deserves the name of demonstration with them, it is that which is composed of certain premises that are purely certain, obligatory to accept, whose contradiction is impossible; these are of that rank. As for what is not like that, but rather is composed of ordinary empirical propositions like those of natural science, medicine, grammar, and the like, these are often contradictory, and the intellect does not affirm the impossibility of their contradiction except under conditions. For ordinary matters may be contradictory, and if a universal proposition may be contradictory, it is not with them material for demonstration, but for dialectic or rhetoric.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage defines the logicians' concept of true demonstration (burhan). Ibn Taymiyya states that the demonstration worthy of the name is composed of certain, necessary premises whose opposite is impossible. In contrast, premises derived from empirical regularities—such as those in physics, medicine, grammar—are not certain; they can be contradicted. Therefore, such premises do not belong to the art of demonstration but rather to dialectic (jadal) or rhetoric (khataba). The key distinction is that demonstration requires absolute certainty, while empirical universals are only probable.

قضايا تجريبية عادية

The Line Between Demonstration, Dialectic, and Rhetoric
Classification of arguments by premise certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify arguments into demonstration, dialectic, or rhetoric based on the certainty of their premises, as defined by the logicians.

Core Idea
The certainty of premises determines whether an argument is demonstration, dialectic, or rhetoric.

The logicians divide arguments into three categories based on their premises: certain premises yield demonstration, generally accepted premises yield dialectic, and persuasive premises yield rhetoric. Ibn Taymiyya highlights that ordinary empirical regularities – like those in natural science, medicine, or grammar – are not certain enough for demonstration. Let's explore this classification and apply it to real examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three arts
From the passage, find the three reasoning arts mentioned: burhan (demonstration), jadal (dialectic), and khataba (rhetoric).
The passage explicitly contrasts demonstration with jadal and khataba.
2
Find the condition for each
Demonstration uses purely certain premises. Dialectic and rhetoric use premises that are not certain, like empirical generalizations. The passage says empirical premises 'are material for dialectic or rhetoric.'
The logicians have other types (e.g., sophistry) but only these are mentioned here.
3
Understand why empirical premises fail
The passage states: 'the intellect does not affirm the impossibility of their contradiction except under conditions.' So empirical premises can always be contradicted in principle, making them unfit for demonstration.
This is a key epistemological point: empirical generalizations are probable, not certain.
4
Apply the principle
Think of an example from natural science: 'All fires are hot.' Could it be contradicted? In a vacuum or under certain conditions, it might not hold. Therefore, it belongs to dialectic or rhetoric, not demonstration.
This shows the practical implication of the classification.

Ibn Taymiyya's Defense: Particular vs. Universal Knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya responds to the objection that knowledge of a speaker's intent relies on empirical universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's response to the objection that certainty about a speaker's intent depends on empirical universals.

Core Idea
Certainty about a specific speaker's intent is particular, not universal, so it bypasses the need for empirical demonstration.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya faces a counterargument: if he denies that empirical universals can ground certain knowledge, how can he claim certainty about what the Prophet or a scholar meant? His answer reveals a key epistemological distinction between universal and particular knowledge, and it shows why his critique of Greek logic does not undermine practical certainty in everyday or scholarly interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's challenge
The objector claims that certainty about a speaker's intent (like the Prophet's) relies on empirical universals (knowledge of language and custom). This challenges Ibn Taymiyya's rejection of empirical premises for demonstration.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's key distinction
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes between universal propositions (qadiyya kulliyya) and particular knowledge of a specific person. Certainty about a specific person's intent is not a universal judgment.
3
Understand the role of specific knowledge
He adds that besides language, there is knowledge specific to that person (his custom and intention) that clarifies his intent. This specific knowledge is particular, not universal.
The specific knowledge (علم يختص به) is what informs us that the speaker intends to communicate by his custom.
4
Evaluate the relevance to demonstration
He concludes that this case is not part of the subject of demonstration (burhan), which deals with universal conclusions. Therefore, the objection does not affect his critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : وأنتم تجزمون بمراد المتكلم بكلامه في غالب المواضع، كما تجزمون بمراد الرسول ومراد علماء الشرع والطب والنحو ؛ والعلم باللغات مبناه على القضايا العادية، قيل : الجزم بمراد الشخص المعين ليس هو قضية كلية، بل هو علم بمراد شخص معين . وهذا وإن كان علمنا بلغته وعادته هو مما يُعين على العلم بمراده فلا بد مع ذلك من علم يَختص به يُعرّف أنه إنما تكلمه بتلك العادة ليُفْهِمَنا مُرادَه . وحينئذ، فليس هذا مما نحن فيه . ولهذا لا نستدل على هذا بمجرد ما ذكروه من برهانهم .
For if it is said: And you are certain of the intended meaning of a speaker by his speech in most cases, just as you are certain of the intended meaning of the Messenger and the intended meaning of the scholars of law, medicine, and grammar; and knowledge of languages is based on ordinary propositions. It is said: Certainty about the intended meaning of a specific person is not a universal proposition; rather, it is knowledge of the intended meaning of a specific person. And this, even though our knowledge of his language and custom helps in knowing his intent, yet there must also be knowledge specific to him that informs us that he speaks according to that custom in order to make us understand his intent. And then, this is not part of our subject. Hence, we do not reason about this by mere mention of their demonstration.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This is a dialectical exchange. An objector says: 'You are certain about a speaker's intent (e.g., the Prophet's intent from his speech), and this certainty is based on empirical universals like customary usage, which contradicts your denial of empirical premises for demonstration.' Ibn Taymiyya answers: The certainty about a specific person's intent is not a universal judgment; it is particular knowledge of that individual. While knowledge of language and custom helps, there is also a specific understanding that the speaker intends to convey. Therefore, this case is not part of the discussion about demonstration, which deals with universal conclusions. Thus, they do not use the logicians' demonstration in such matters.

Ibn Taymiyya saves his critique by showing that certainty about a specific speaker's intent is particular and does not rely on universal empirical premises.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique: Defining Manifest by Hidden in Demonstration
Ibn Taymiyya argues that using hidden to explain manifest is worse in demonstration than in definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's objection to the logicians' rule about defining the manifest by the hidden, and explain his alternative view on the purpose of definition.

Core Idea
Definition distinguishes, demonstration clarifies; the hidden-to-manifest fault hurts demonstration more.

Ibn Taymiyya here sharpens a criticism against the logicians. They themselves criticize defining the manifest by the hidden, but Ibn Taymiyya turns the table: this fault is even graver in demonstration, not in definition. Moreover, he redefines the very purpose of definition, stripping it of its metaphysical burden of revealing essences. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' rule
The logicians criticize defining the manifest (jali) by the hidden (khafi). This is a standard principle: the definiens should be clearer than the definiendum. Recognize this as the target of Ibn Taymiyya's critique.
2
Compare the fault in definition vs. demonstration
Ibn Taymiyya says the fault is worse in demonstration because demonstration aims to clarify and reveal its conclusion. If the proof (burhan) is itself clearer than the conclusion, then using hidden premises to explain a manifest conclusion is a graver error. In contrast, definition's purpose is merely distinction, so it can tolerate a hidden distinguisher.
The key is the differing purposes: clarification vs. distinction.
3
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's redefinition of hadd
He states that the correct purpose of hadd is tamyez (distinction) between the defined and others, not definition of the essence (mahiyya). This is a critical shift from the Aristotelian/logician view that definition reveals the quiddity.
This redefinition has profound implications for how definitions are used in Islamic sciences.
4
Evaluate the implications
If definition is only about distinction, then the definiens need not be clearer; it can be more hidden as long as it distinguishes. This opens the door for using technical or less obvious terms in definitions, provided they differentiate the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهم يعيبون في صناعة الحد أن يعرَّف الجلي بالخفي، وهذا في صناعة البرهان أشدُّ عيباً. فإن البرهان لا يُراد به إلا بيانُ المدلول عليه وتعريفه وكشفه وإيضاحه. فإذا كان هو أوضح وأظهر كان هذا بياناً للجلي بالخفي. وأما «الحدّ» فالصواب أن المراد به التمييز بينه وبين المحدود لا تعريفُ الماهية. وإذا كان مطلوبه التمييز فقد يكون المميز أخفى، وقد يكون أجلى .
And they criticize in the art of definition that the manifest be defined by the hidden, and this in the art of demonstration is a worse fault. For demonstration is only intended to clarify the object of proof, define it, disclose it, and make it clear. So if it (the proof) is clearer and more evident, then this would be clarifying the manifest by the hidden. As for definition (hadd), the correct view is that its purpose is distinction between the defined and others, not definition of the essence. And if its aim is distinction, then the distinguisher may be more hidden or more manifest.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
Ibn Taymiyya criticizes the logicians' own rule that a definition should not define the manifest by the hidden. He says this fault is even worse in demonstration. Why? Because demonstration aims to clarify and make evident its conclusion. If the premises (the proof) are clearer than the conclusion, then using hidden premises to explain manifest conclusion would be a graver error. In contrast, the correct purpose of definition (hadd) is merely to distinguish the defined from other things, not to reveal the essence. Therefore, in definition, it is acceptable for the distinguisher to be either more hidden or more manifest, as long as it distinguishes.

Key Terms
حد
برهان
تمييز
ماهية

C) Because definition already accepts hidden terms.
D) Because logicians ignore demonstration.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya reorients the purpose of definition from essence-disclosure to distinction, and shifts the critique from definition to demonstration, using the logicians' own principle against them.
