The Impossibility of Two Self-Evident Premises for Disputed Matters
Author argues logicians cannot provide two self-evident premises for any disputed matter, citing al-Nawbakhti's testimony.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's logical and empirical refutation of the claim that two self-evident premises are required for demonstrating any disputed matter.

Core Idea
If two premises were self-evident, one would suffice—so the claim of two is empty.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges a core tenet of Aristotelian demonstration: that every theoretical conclusion requires two self-evident premises. He dismantles this by analyzing the scenario: if both were self-evident, one is redundant; if one is theoretical, it needs proof; if both, both need proof. He then supports his argument with the empirical testimony of al-Nawbakhti, who asked logicians for such premises and was never shown any. This lesson will guide you through Ibn Taymiyya's critique and its implications for how we assess logical proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
The claim under attack is that every disputed theoretical conclusion requires two self-evident premises (badihiyyat) for demonstration. Understand that this claim is foundational to Aristotelian syllogistic.
Self-evident means needing no proof.
2
Analyze the three cases
Ibn Taymiyya examines what happens if we assume the premises are self-evident: if both are self-evident, one would be enough; if one is theoretical, it needs proof; if both are theoretical, both need proof. This shows the claim is internally inconsistent.
This is a logical exhaustion of possibilities.
3
Assess the empirical evidence
The author cites al-Nawbakhti's inquiry: he asked logicians' leaders to produce two self-evident premises for a disputed matter, but they could not. This empirical failure reinforces the logical critique.
Al-Nawbakhti was a prominent Imami scholar.
4
Conclude the invalidity
Since logicians cannot provide such premises, the claim that they are required is baseless. Ibn Taymiyya declares that Aristotle's mentioned premises are 'non-existent and unknown.'
This refutes the necessity of two self-evident premises in demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومقصوده أنهم لا يجدون مقدمتين أوليتين بديهيتين يستدل بهما على شيء من موارد النزاع التي تحتاج إلى البرهان، بل لا بد أن يكون إحداهما أو كلاهما غير بديهية. ومتى قدر أنهما بديهيتان فإحداهما تكفي كما ذكره من المثال. وإن قدرت إحداهما نظرية فهي التي يحتاج إلى بيانها. وإذا كانتا جميعاً نظريتين احتيج إلى بيانهما جميعاً، كما لو كانت ثلاث مقدمات. وما يحتاج إلى بيانه يُستدلّ عليه، ثم يستدل به. وإنما يستدل ابتداءً بما هو بين بنفسه كالبديهيات. قال: ولا يجدون في المطالب العلمية أن المطلوب يقف على مقدمتين بينتين بأنفسهما. بل إذا كان الأمر كذلك كانت إحداهما كافية. قال: ونقول لهم: أرونا مقدمتين أوليتين لا تحتاجان إلى برهان يتقدمهما يُستدل بهما على شيء مختلف فيه وتكون المقدمتان في العقول أولى بالقبول من النتيجة. فإذ كنتم لا تجدون ذلك بطل ما ادعيتموه. قال أبو محمد الحسن بن موسى النوبختي: وقد سألت غير واحد من رؤسائهم أن يُوجِدَنِيه، فما أوجدنيه. قال: فما ذكره أرسطو طاليس غير موجود ولا معروف
And his point is that they do not find two primary self-evident premises by which to infer anything from the matters of dispute that require demonstration; rather, one or both of them must be non-self-evident. And if it is assumed that they are self-evident, then one of them suffices, as he mentioned in the example. And if one of them is assumed to be theoretical, then it is the one that needs explanation. And if both are theoretical, then both need explanation, as if there were three premises. And what needs explanation is inferred from (something else), then used to infer. And inference is initially made from what is self-evident like the self-evident truths. He said: And they do not find in the scientific inquiries that the required (conclusion) depends on two premises that are self-evident. Rather, if it were so, one of them would be sufficient. He said: And we say to them: Show us two primary premises that do not need a preceding proof, by which to infer something disputed, and the two premises are more acceptable in minds than the conclusion. Since you do not find that, your claim is invalid. Abu Muhammad al-Hasan ibn Musa al-Nawbakhti said: I asked more than one of their leaders to show it to me, but they did not show me. He said: What Aristotle mentioned is non-existent and unknown.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that two self-evident premises are required for any theoretical conclusion. The author begins by stating the logicians' position: they cannot find two self-evident premises for matters of dispute that require demonstration. He then analyzes the scenario: if both premises were self-evident, one would suffice; if one is theoretical, it needs proof; if both are theoretical, both need proof. Thus, the claim that two premises are always needed is flawed. The author then quotes an argument: logicians cannot show two premises that are more acceptable than the conclusion for any disputed matter. He supports this with the testimony of al-Nawbakhti, who asked their leaders but found none. Finally, he declares that what Aristotle mentioned is non-existent. This shows that the foundational claim of syllogistic demonstration is baseless.

Key Terms
مقدمتان بديهيتان
موارد النزاع
البرهان
أرسطو طاليس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are both needed.
B) One of them suffices.
C) The conclusion is not certain.
D) The premises must be from Aristotle.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the necessity of two self-evident premises by showing that if both were self-evident, one would be redundant, and such pairs are never found for disputed matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Necessity of Conceiving Implication for Knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya on the necessity of conceiving implication for knowledge from evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between clear and hidden implications (luzum) and explain why mere evidence without implication does not yield knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge requires both evidence and the recognition of its implication.

Ibn Taymiyya clarifies a fundamental epistemological principle: evidence (dalil) leads to knowledge only when the implication (luzum) to the conclusion is also conceived. He uses the example of a mule to illustrate that knowing the evidence alone is insufficient. This lesson unpacks that principle and distinguishes between clear and hidden implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components needed for knowledge from evidence.
From the text, knowledge requires both the evidence itself (dalil) and the conception of the implication (luzum) that connects the evidence to the conclusion (madlul). Write these down.
The text says: 'As for mere evidence without conceiving the implication of the conclusion, it does not yield knowledge.'
2
Apply the mule example.
The example: knowing an animal is a mule (evidence) and knowing that mules do not give birth (implication) yields knowledge that this particular mule is not pregnant (conclusion). If someone knows it is a mule but does not know the implication, they do not gain that knowledge.
The implication here is that 'being a mule' necessarily implies 'not being pregnant'.
3
Distinguish clear from hidden implications.
Clear implications are known immediately upon knowing the evidence. Hidden implications require an additional proof (wasaṭ) to establish the implication. The text notes that minds vary: some need the extra proof, others do not.
Ibn Taymiyya uses the terms 'clear' (bayyin) and 'hidden' (khafiyya) implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالمدلول لازم للدليل . فمتى تصور الإنسان الدليل ولزوم المدلول له كون المدلول تصور المدلول . فإذا تصور أنها بغلة وتصور لازم ذلك، وهو نفي الحمل عن البغلة، تصور قطعاً نفيه عن هذه. فأما مجرد الدليل بدون تصور لزوم المدلول له فلا يحصل به العلم. واللوازم البين لزومها للدليل تُعلم بمجرد العلم به وبلزومها له ؛ واللوازم الخفية التي يفتقر العلم بلزومها إلى وسط وهو دليل ثانٍ على اللزوم، يقف على ذلك. والأذهان في هذا متفاوتة. فقد يحتاج هذا الذهن في معرفة اللزوم إلى وسط الدليل، والآخر لا يحتاج إليه . وقد تنازع النظار في العلم الحاصل بالدليل: هل هو لزومه عن الدليل لزوماً عادياً كما يقولونه في الشَّبَع مع الأكل، أو لزوماً عقلياً يسمى «التضمن» بحيث لا يمكن الانفكاك عنه كما يمتنع وجود العلم والإرادة بدون الحياة. والأول قول قدماء النظار كالأشعري وغيره. ولهذا جعله المعتزلة من باب العلم بالدليل سبب للعلم التولد»، وهذا كالرؤية مع التحديق وكالسمع مع الإصغاء. وإلا فحصول العلم بالدليل دون المدلول عليه ليس ممتنعاً لذاته، بل الأول سبب للثاني، ومقتض له، وموجب له، بحكم سُنَّة الله تعالى في عباده بخلاف الحياة مع العلم، فإن الأول شرط للثاني. ولهذا كان العلم يوجد مع الحياة، ليست الحياة متقدمة عليه كما يتقدم العلم بالمدلول عليه .
So the conclusion (madlul) is necessary to the evidence (dalil). Whenever a person conceives the evidence and the implication of the conclusion from it, he conceives the conclusion. So if he conceives that it is a mule and conceives the necessary consequence of that, which is the negation of pregnancy for the mule, he necessarily conceives the negation of pregnancy for this particular. As for mere evidence without conceiving the implication of the conclusion, it does not yield knowledge. Those implications whose implication from the evidence is clear are known merely by knowing the evidence and its implication; but hidden implications, whose knowledge of implication requires an intermediate (wasaṭ) which is a second proof of the implication, depend on that. And minds vary in this. So one mind may need an intermediate to know the implication, while another does not. And the theorists have disputed concerning the knowledge obtained from the evidence: is it necessary from the evidence by customary necessity, as they say about satiety from eating, or by rational necessity called 'tadamun' (inclusion) such that it cannot be separated, like the impossibility of knowledge and will without life? The first is the view of the earlier theorists like al-Ash'ari and others. For this reason, the Mu'tazila make it from the category of knowledge from evidence as a 'generated' cause for knowledge, like sight with focusing and hearing with listening. Otherwise, the occurrence of knowledge from evidence without the conclusion is not impossible in itself; rather, the first is a cause for the second, a requirement and an obligator, by the custom (sunnah) of Allah Almighty in His servants, unlike life with knowledge, for the first is a condition for the second. Thus knowledge exists with life; life is not prior to it as knowledge is prior to the conclusion.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage explores the epistemological relationship between evidence (dalil) and conclusion (madlul). Ibn Taymiyya states that the conclusion is necessarily implied by the evidence. To gain knowledge, one must conceive both the evidence and its implication. Using the mule example, if one knows a particular animal is a mule and also knows the necessary consequence (that mules do not give birth), one knows that animal is not pregnant. However, merely having the evidence without understanding the implication yields no knowledge. He distinguishes between 'clear implications' (lawāzim bayyin), where knowledge of the implication is immediate upon knowing the evidence, and 'hidden implications' (lawāzim khafiyya), which require a second proof to establish the implication. This varies among individuals. He then recounts a debate among theorists: whether the knowledge from evidence is a customary (ʿādi) necessity (like fullness from eating) or a rational necessity involving inclusion (taḍammun) such that it is inseparable (e.g., knowledge and will require life). The earlier theorists (e.g., al-Ashʿarī) held the customary view; the Muʿtazila considered it a 'generated' (mutawallid) causation, like sight by focusing. Ibn Taymiyya concludes that knowledge from evidence does not happen by impossibility but by Allah's custom—the evidence is a cause and requirement for the conclusion, unlike life for knowledge, where life is a condition (sharṭ) not a cause.

Key Terms

مدلول
لزوم
تضمن

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is required for knowledge to result from a piece of evidence (dalil)?
A) Just the evidence
B) The evidence and the conclusion
C) The evidence and the assumption that the conclusion is true
D) The evidence and conceiving its implication to the conclusion

Key Takeaway
Knowledge from evidence requires both the evidence and the conception of its implication; the implication may be clear or hidden, and individuals vary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the hadith: 'The prayer of one who has had a drink is not accepted.' Identify the evidence, the clear implication, and a possible hidden implication that would require another evidence.
?
In the mule example, state the evidence, the conclusion, and the necessary implication. Then explain what would happen if someone knows the evidence but not the implication.

Customary vs Rational Necessity in Evidence-Conclusion Link
Ibn Taymiyya on the nature of implication: customary vs rational necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between customary necessity (such as satiety from eating) and rational necessity (such as life for knowledge) in the context of evidence-conclusion relationships.

Core Idea
The link between evidence and conclusion can be customary or rational, but it is never impossible.

Ibn Taymiyya reports a debate among theologians: is the knowledge from evidence a customary necessity (like satiety from eating) or a rational necessity of inclusion (like life for knowledge)? This lesson examines these two models and Ibn Taymiyya's own conclusion that it is a causal relationship by Allah's custom, not an inherent impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions on the nature of necessity.
The text presents: (1) customary necessity ('ādiya) – similar to satiety following eating, according to al-Ash'ari and earlier theorists; (2) rational necessity (taḍammun) – where the conclusion is 'included' so that separation is impossible, like knowledge requiring life.
The dispute is about whether the evidence 'causes' knowledge by custom or by rational inclusion.
2
Compare the two models using the examples given.
Customary: like satiety from eating – the link is observed, not rationally necessary. Rational: like knowledge requiring life – it is impossible to have knowledge without life. The Mu'tazila treat evidence as a 'generated' cause (tawallud), like sight by focusing.
Ibn Taymiyya notes that the earlier theorists (Ash'ari) lean customary; Mu'tazila lean generated causation.
3
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's resolution.
He states that knowledge from evidence without the conclusion is not impossible in itself; rather, the evidence is a cause by Allah's custom (sunnah). This differs from life and knowledge, where life is a condition (sharṭ), not a cause.
The key distinction: a condition is necessary for existence but does not bring about the effect; a cause (by custom) does.

Ibn Taymiyya: Syllogism Cannot Prove the Specific Creator
Ibn Taymiyya argues universals are mental, so syllogism fails for specific existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that syllogism cannot prove specific existents and evaluate its implications for theological demonstrations.

Core Idea
Universals are mental abstractions; syllogisms yield only universals, so they cannot prove the specific Creator.

Ibn Taymiyya presents a third argument against the epistemological value of syllogism: universals exist only in the mind, while external reality consists of unique particulars. Since syllogisms reason with universals, they cannot capture the specific essence of any existent, including the Creator. This critique directly targets the famous kalam arguments for God's existence—the argument from origination (huduth) and contingency (imkan). In this lesson, you will dissect Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning and assess its force.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Premise about Universals
Ibn Taymiyya states that universal general propositions (al-qadaya al-kulliyya) do not exist externally as universals; they exist only in the mind (al-adhhan), not in external objects (al-a'yan).
This is a metaphysical claim about the locus of universals.
2
Distinguish Mental and External Existence
Externally, every existent is a particular (juz'i) with a unique essence that prevents conceptual sharing. Internally, we form universal concepts that abstract away from individual differences.
The uniqueness of each existent is key: no two beings share exactly the same essence.
3
Trace the Conclusion about Syllogism
Since syllogisms reason with universals, they cannot indicate a specific particular existent. Logicians themselves admit this, saying syllogism does not indicate a particular (juz'i) but only a universal (kulli).
Ibn Taymiyya turns the logicians' own admission against them.
4
Apply to the Proofs for the Creator
Take the huduth argument: 'This is originated; every originated thing has an originator; therefore, there is an originator.' Ibn Taymiyya says this only yields an absolute originator (muhdith mutlaq), not the specific Creator of Islam. Similarly for the imkan argument.
Adding attributes like 'eternal' or 'all-knowing' does not overcome the limitation, as the syllogism still operates at a universal level.
5
Evaluate the Critique
Consider whether Ibn Taymiyya's argument is sound: do the kalam proofs actually intend to prove a specific Being, or only a generic necessary existent? Assess whether Ibn Taymiyya's critique is a refutation or a refinement.
This step requires engaging with both the logical and theological dimensions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث: أن القضايا الكلية العامة لا توجد في الخارج كلية عامة، وإنما كون القياس تكون كلية في الأذهان لا في الأعيان وأما الموجودات في الخارج فهي أمور معينة، كل موجود له حقيقة تخصه يتميز بها عما سواه لا يشاركه فيها غيره. فحينئذ لا حقائق يمكن الاستدلال بـ «القياس على خصوص وجود معين وهم معترفون بذلك وقائلون أن «القياس» لا يدل على أمر معين. وقد يعبرون عن ذلك بأنه لا يدل على جزئي، وإنما يدل على كلي. والمراد بالجزئي ما يمنع تصوره من وقوع الشركة فيه. وكل موجود له حقيقة تخصه يمنع تصورها من وقوع الشركة فيها . فإذن القياس» لا يفيد معرفة أمر موجود بعينه، وكل موجود فإنما هو موجود بعينه فلا يفيد معرفة شيء من حقائق الموجودات، وإنما يفيد أموراً كلية مطلقة مقدَّرة في الأذهان لا محققة في الأعيان . وقد بسطنا الكلام على هذا وغيره في غير هذا الموضع، وبين أن ما يذكره النظار القياسية من الأدلة القياسية التي يسمونها براهين» على إثبات الصانع سبحانه وتعالى لا يدل شيء منها على عينه، وإنما يدل على أمر مطلق كلّي لا يمنع تصوره من وقوع الشركة فيه . فإنا إذا قلنا هذا محدَّث، وكل محدث فلا بد له من محدث»، أو «ممكن، والممكن لا بد له من واجب إنما يدل هذا على محدث مطلق أو واجب مطلق. ولو عين بأنه قديم أزلي عالم بكل شيء، وغير ذلك، فكل هذا إنما يدل فيه «القياس»
The third aspect: that universal general propositions do not exist externally as universals; rather, the syllogism occurs as universal in minds, not in external objects. As for existents in the external world, they are particular matters; every existent has an essence specific to it by which it is distinguished from others, and no other shares in that essence. Therefore, there are no essences that can be used as evidence via syllogism for a specific particular existence, and they themselves admit this, saying that syllogism does not indicate a particular matter; rather, it indicates a universal. And by 'particular' they mean that whose conception prevents the occurrence of sharing [in that concept]. And every existent has an essence specific to it which prevents its conception from the occurrence of sharing in it. Hence, the syllogism does not yield knowledge of a specific existent; rather, every existent is precisely a specific existent, so it yields no knowledge of any of the essences of existents. Rather, it yields absolute universal matters that are estimated in minds, not actualized in external objects. And we have expanded the discussion on this and other matters elsewhere, and made clear that what the speculative theologians mention of syllogistic proofs, which they call 'demonstrations' for proving the Creator, may He be exalted, none of them indicates His specific essence. Rather, it indicates an absolute universal matter whose conception does not prevent the occurrence of sharing in it. For if we say 'This is originated, and every originated thing must have an originator,' or 'possible, and the possible must have a necessary,' this only indicates an absolute originator or absolute necessary. And if one specifies that He is eternal, pre-eternal, knowing everything, and so on, all of this only indicates via syllogism.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's third argument against the value of logical syllogism. He makes a metaphysical distinction: universal propositions exist only in the mind, while external reality consists of particular individuals each with a unique essence. Since syllogism deals in universals, it cannot capture the specific essence of any particular existent. Ibn Taymiyya notes that logicians themselves admit that syllogism does not indicate a particular (juz'i) but only a universal (kulli). He then applies this critique to the famous arguments for the existence of God—the argument from origination (huduth) and from contingency (imkan). These arguments, he claims, only prove a universal concept of an originator or necessary being, not the specific Creator of Islam. Adding attributes like 'eternal' or 'all-knowing' does not overcome this limitation, because the syllogism still operates at a universal level. Thus, Ibn Taymiyya concludes that syllogistic demonstration is epistemologically insufficient for knowing the specific existent, especially the Creator.

Key Terms
القضايا الكلية
القياس
الجزئي
الصانع
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the syllogism is too complex for ordinary people.
B) Because the syllogism only yields a universal concept, not a specific essence.
C) Because originated things do not actually require an originator.
D) Because the syllogism is based on sense experience, which is unreliable.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that since syllogisms operate with universals, they cannot grasp the unique essence of any particular existent, including the Creator; they only yield a generic concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique: Syllogism Cannot Prove the Specific Creator
Ibn Taymiyya argues that demonstrative syllogism cannot prove the specific existence of the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that demonstrative syllogism yields only a universal concept of God, not knowledge of His specific essence, and contrast this with Quranic signs as particular indicators.

Core Idea
Syllogisms give universal concepts; signs give the specific Creator.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the claim that demonstrative syllogism can prove the existence of God in His particular essence. He argues that syllogisms operate with universals, while every existent is particular. Therefore, syllogistic proofs only yield abstract notions, not the concrete Creator. In contrast, Quranic signs directly indicate the specific divine reality. In this lesson, you will trace his reasoning and see how it reshapes the epistemology of theological proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
What exactly does Ibn Taymiyya criticize? Find the assertion that demonstrative syllogism can prove the Creator's existence.
Look for the phrase 'إثبات الصانع' – proving the Creator.
2
Understand the nature of universals
How does Ibn Taymiyya characterize universal propositions? He says they exist only in the mind (الأذهان), not in external reality (الأعيان).
This is key: universals are mental abstractions.
3
Trace the syllogistic limitation
Why can't a syllogism indicate a particular existent? Because its premises are universal, and the conclusion cannot be more specific than the premises. Every existent is particular (جزئي), so the conclusion remains universal.
Ibn Taymiyya references the logicians' own admission that syllogism does not indicate particulars.
4
Contrast with Quranic signs
How do signs (آيات) differ? They are particular, perceptible phenomena (like the sun) that directly point to the Creator's essence, not a common genus.
Example: the sun is a specific sign of the day, not just a universal 'light source'.
5
Draw the epistemological conclusion
What is Ibn Taymiyya's alternative? True knowledge of God's specific essence comes through direct signs and revelation, not syllogistic reasoning.
The term dalil (evidence) is broader than qiyas and includes signs.

الوجه الثالث: عدم دلالة «القياس البرهاني» على إثبات الصانع الوجه الثالث: أن القضايا الكلية العامة لا توجد في الخارج كلية عامة، وإنما كون القياس تكون كلية في الأذهان لا في الأعيان وأما الموجودات في الخارج فهي أمور معينة، كل موجود له حقيقة تخصه يتميز بها عما سواه لا يشاركه فيها غيره. فحينئذ لا حقائق يمكن الاستدلال بـ «القياس على خصوص وجود معين وهم معترفون بذلك وقائلون أن «القياس» لا يدل على أمر معين. وقد يعبرون عن ذلك بأنه لا يدل على جزئي، وإنما يدل على كلي. والمراد بالجزئي ما يمنع تصوره من وقوع الشركة فيه. وكل موجود له حقيقة تخصه يمنع تصورها من وقوع الشركة فيها . فإذن القياس» لا يفيد معرفة أمر موجود بعينه، وكل موجود فإنما هو موجود بعينه فلا يفيد معرفة شيء من حقائق الموجودات، وإنما يفيد أموراً كلية مطلقة مقدرة في الأذهان لا محققة في الأعيان . وقد بسطنا الكلام على هذا وغيره في غير هذا الموضع، وبين أن ما يذكره النظار القياسية لا من الأدلة القياسية التي يسمونها براهين» على إثبات الصانع سبحانه وتعالى لا يدل شيء تدل على الصانع بعينه منها على عينه، وإنما يدل على أمر مطلق كلّي لا يمنع تصوره من وقوع الشركة فيه . فإنا إذا قلنا هذا محدث، وكل محدث فلا بد له من محدث»، أو «ممكن، والممكن لا بد له من واجب إنما يدل هذا على محدث مطلق أو واجب مطلق. ولو عين بأنه قديم أزلي عالم بكل شيء، وغير ذلك، فكل هذا إنما يدل فيه «القياس» على أمر مطلق كلي لا يمنع تصوره من وقوع الشركة فيه. وإنما يُعلم عينه بعلم آخر يجعله الله في القلوب. وهم معترفون بهذا لأن النتيجة لا تكون أبلغ من المقدمات، والمقدمات فيها قضية كلية لا بد من ذلك والكلي لا يدل على معيَّن . وهذا بخلاف ما يذكره الله في كتابه من «الآيات»، كقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ فِي خَلْقِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ وَاخْتِلَافِ الَّيْلِ وَالنَّهَارِ - إلى قوله - لِقَوْمٍ يَعْقِلُونَ ﴾، وقوله : إن في ذلك لآيات لقوم يعقلون، لقوم يتفكرون، وغير ذلك. فإنه يدل على المعيَّن، كالشمس التي هي آية النهار . وقال تعالى : ﴿وَجَعَلْنَا الَّيْلَ وَالنَّهَارَ ءَايَنَيْنِ فَمَحَوْنَا ءَايَةَ الَّيلِ وَجَعَلْنَا ءَايَةَ النَّهَارِ مُبْصِرَةً ﴾ . و الدليل» أعم من «القياس». فإن الدليل قد يكون بمعين على معين، كما يُستدل بالنجم وغيره من الكواكب على الكعبة . ف «الآيات» تدل على نفس الخالق سبحانه لا على قدر مشترك بينه وبين غيره. فإن كل ما سواه مفتقر إليه نفسه، فيلزم من وجوده وجود عين الخالق نفسه .
The third aspect: The failure of demonstrative syllogism to prove the existence of the Creator. The third aspect: That universal general propositions do not exist in the external world as universal and general; rather, the syllogism's premises are universal in the minds, not in external entities. As for existents in the external world, they are specific things; every existent has a reality specific to it by which it is distinguished from others, no other shares it therein. Thus, there are no realities by which one can infer through syllogism to the specific existence of a particular thing, and they themselves acknowledge this, saying that the syllogism does not indicate a specific thing, but only indicates a universal. By 'particular' they mean what prevents its conception from being shared (i.e., being applicable to multiple individuals). Every existent has a reality specific to it that prevents its conception from being shared. Therefore, the syllogism does not yield knowledge of a specific existent thing, and every existent is only a specific existent; thus it does not yield knowledge of any of the realities of existents, but only yields absolute universal matters that are assumed in the minds, not realized in the external world. We have elaborated on this and other matters elsewhere, and have shown that what the proponents of syllogistic arguments mention, from the syllogistic proofs that they call 'demonstrations' (barahin) for proving the Creator—glorified and exalted—none of them indicate the Creator Himself by His very essence; rather, they indicate an absolute universal matter whose conception does not prevent being shared. For if we say 'This is temporally originated, and every temporally originated thing must have an originator,' or 'possible,' and 'every possible thing must have a necessary being,' this only indicates an absolute originator or absolute necessary being. And even if we specify that He is eternal, pre-eternal, knowing everything, etc., all that the syllogism indicates is an absolute universal matter whose conception does not prevent being shared. His specific essence is only known through another knowledge that God places in hearts. And they themselves acknowledge this because the conclusion cannot be more extensive than the premises; the premises include a universal proposition, and that is necessary, and the universal does not indicate a particular. And this is contrary to what God mentions in His Book of 'signs' (ayat), like His saying: 'Indeed, in the creation of the heavens and the earth and the alternation of the night and day...' to 'for a people who use reason,' and His saying: 'Indeed in that are signs for a people who use reason, for a people who reflect,' etc. For they indicate the specific thing, like the sun which is the sign of the day. And He said: 'And We made the night and day two signs, and We erased the sign of the night and made the sign of the day visible.' And the 'evidence' (dalil) is more general than 'syllogism' (qiyas). For evidence may be by a specific thing for a specific thing, as one uses the star and other celestial bodies to point to the Kaaba. So the 'signs' indicate the Creator Himself—glorified—not a common factor between Him and others. For everything other than Him is in need of Him Himself, so from His existence necessarily follows the existence of the Creator Himself.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the claim that demonstrative syllogism (al-qiyas al-burhani) can prove the existence of the Creator (al-Sani') in His specific essence. He begins by asserting that universal propositions do not exist as universal in the external world; they exist only in the mind. External existents are particular, each having a unique reality. Therefore, a syllogism—which deals with universals—cannot lead to knowledge of a specific existent. The logicians themselves admit that syllogism does not indicate a particular (juz'i) but only a universal (kulli). Hence, syllogistic proofs for God's existence only yield a universal concept (e.g., 'a necessary existent' or 'an originator'), not knowledge of the specific, unique God. Even if additional attributes are added, the conclusion remains universal. He then contrasts this with the Quranic 'signs' (ayat), which are specific, perceptible phenomena that point directly to the Creator Himself, not to a common genus. For example, the sun is a specific sign of the day, and the stars point to the Kaaba. Thus, these signs indicate the very essence of the Creator, because everything other than Him is in need of Him, so the existence of the signs necessarily indicates the existence of the Creator Himself. This passage highlights Ibn Taymiyya's epistemological critique: that rational syllogisms are insufficient for theological knowledge, and revelation provides direct, particular indication.

القضية الكلية
الجزئي
الآيات

C) The syllogism is invalid

Conditions for the Middle Term in Logical Proof
Ibn Taymiyya analyzes the logical conditions for the evidence (dalil) in a syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify whether a proposed middle term meets the two necessary conditions of coverage and entailment for a valid syllogistic proof in Islamic legal reasoning.

Core Idea
The middle term must be general enough to cover all subjects but specific enough to entail the judgment.

Ibn Taymiyya uses the concrete example of proving that nabidh (a fermented drink) is forbidden to illustrate a critical refinement of the logicians' conditions for the middle term in a syllogism. He shows that the middle term (here, 'khamr' or wine) must satisfy two precise relationships with the subject and the predicate to serve as valid evidence. This lesson walks through his analysis so you can apply these conditions to any legal or theological argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms in the example
In the argument that nabidh is forbidden because it is intoxicating and every intoxicant is wine, locate the subject (nabidh, the minor term), the predicate (forbidden, the major term), and the middle term (wine, which connects them).
These correspond to al-hadd al-asghar, al-hadd al-akbar, and al-hadd al-awsat.
2
Determine the generality relations
Compare the extension of each term: wine is more general than nabidh (since all nabidh is wine but not vice versa) and more specific than forbidden (since wine is a subset of forbidden things).
Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes these relative generalities.
3
State the first condition: coverage of the subject
The middle term must be equal to or more general than the subject of judgment, so that it covers every instance of the subject. If it were more specific, some cases of the subject would not be included in the evidence.
Here, wine covers all nabidh because every intoxicating nabidh is wine.
4
State the second condition: entailment of the judgment
The middle term must be equal to or more specific than the judgment, so that wherever the middle term is present, the judgment necessarily follows. If it were more general, the judgment could be absent despite the presence of the middle term.
Here, wherever wine is present, the judgment 'forbidden' holds, since all wine is forbidden.
5
Apply the conditions to verify the example
Check that wine (middle term) is indeed more general than nabidh (coverage) and more specific than forbidden (entailment). Since both conditions are satisfied, the argument is valid as an example of a correct syllogistic proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاستدل على ذلك بأن النبيذ المسكر خمر بالنص، وهو قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : كل مسكر خمر - رواه مسلم وغيره . فـ الخمر أعم من «النبيذ» المتنازع فيه، وأخص من «الحرام» و «الحرام» هو الحكم، وهو الخبر، وهو الصفة، وهو المحكوم عليه . أو مساوياً له المطلوب بالدليل، وهو الذي يسمونه الحد الأكبر، ويسمونه «محمول النتيجة» و «النبيذ» هو المحكوم عليه وهو المبتدأ المخبر عنه وهو الموصوف، وهو محل الحكم، وهو الذي يسمونه «الحد الأصغر»، و «موضوع النتيجة». و «الخمر» هو أخص من الدليل، وهو «الحد الأوسط» . والمطلوب بالدليل معرفة الحكم، لا معرفة عينه فهذا الدليل يجب أن لا يكون أخص من محل الحكم، بل يكون إما مساوياً له، وإما أعم منه، لأنه لا بد أن يشمل جميع محل الحكم . فإذا كان أخص لم يشمله . ويجب أن لا يكون أعم من الحكم، بل يكون إما مساوياً له، وإما أخص منه . لأنه الحكم لازم له فإذا كان أعم منه أمكن وجوده بدون وجود الحكم، فلا يصلح أن يكون دليلاً مستلزماً له فلا بد في الدليل أن يكون مساوياً للحكم، أو أخص منه، ليكون مستلزماً له ولا بد أن يكون أعم من المحكوم عليه، أو مساوياً له، ليتناول جميع صُوَر المحكوم عليه، وإلا لم يكن دليلاً على حكمه، بل على حكم بعضه . والناس هنا قد يضطرب أذهانهم في «الدليل»، هل يجب أن لا يكون أعم من الحكم المدلول عليه، أو لا يكون أخص؟ وسبب ذلك أن المدلول عليه قد يُعنى به المحكوم عليه الحكم» نفسه، وقد یعنی به «المحكوم عليه . فإذا أقمنا الدليل على «أن النبيذ حرام» فقد يقال : المدلول عليه هو «النبيذ»، وهذا يجب أن لا يكون أعم من الدليل، بل إما مساوياً، وإما أخص. وقد يقال : المدلول عليه هو الحكم، وهو «حرمة النبيذ». وهذا يجب أن لا يكون أخص من الدليل، بل يكون إما مساوياً له، وإما أعم منه . لأن الحكم لازم للدليل والدليل لازم للمحكوم عليه فلا بد أن يكون المحكوم عليه مستلزماً للدليل، بحيث يكون حيث وجد وُجد الدليل ليشمله الدليل. ولا بد أن يكون الحكم لازماً للدليل، بحيث يكون حيث تحقق الدليل تحقق الحكم، حتى يثبت الحكم في جميع صور المحكوم عليه .
He used as evidence that intoxicating nabidh is wine by explicit text, and it is the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Every intoxicant is wine' - related by Muslim and others. So 'wine' is more general than the disputed 'nabidh', and more specific than 'forbidden', and 'forbidden' is the judgment, the predicate, the attribute, and the subject of judgment. Or equal to it, the sought [conclusion] by the evidence, which they call the major term, and they call it 'predicate of the conclusion', and 'nabidh' is the subject of judgment, the subject, the described, the locus of the judgment, which they call the minor term, and 'subject of the conclusion'. And 'wine' is more specific than the evidence, and it is the middle term. And the sought by the evidence is knowledge of the judgment, not knowledge of its essence. So this evidence must not be more specific than the locus of the judgment, but either equal to it or more general than it, because it must encompass all instances of the locus of the judgment. If it were more specific, it would not encompass it. And it must not be more general than the judgment, but either equal to it or more specific than it, because the judgment is necessary from it; if it were more general than it, it could exist without the existence of the judgment, so it would not be suitable as an evidence entailing it. So the evidence must be equal to the judgment or more specific than it, to be entailing it, and must be more general than the subject of judgment or equal to it, to cover all forms of the subject of judgment; otherwise it would not be evidence for its judgment, but only for a part of it. People here may be confused about the 'evidence': should it not be more general than the judgment signified, or not more specific? The reason is that what is signified may be meant as the judgment itself, or as the subject of judgment. So if we establish the evidence that 'nabidh is forbidden', it may be said: what is signified is 'nabidh', and this must not be more general than the evidence, but either equal or more specific. And it may be said: what is signified is the judgment, which is 'the prohibition of nabidh', and this must not be more specific than the evidence, but either equal to it or more general than it. Because the judgment is necessary from the evidence and the evidence is necessary from the subject of judgment, so the subject of judgment must be entailing the evidence, such that wherever it exists the evidence exists to encompass it. And the judgment must be necessary from the evidence, such that wherever the evidence is realized the judgment is realized, so that the judgment is established in all instances of the subject of judgment.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' conditions for the middle term in a syllogistic proof. He uses the concrete example of proving that nabidh (a fermented drink) is haram because it is intoxicating and every intoxicant is wine (khamr), and wine is haram. He identifies the logical terms: the major term (al-hadd al-akbar) is 'forbidden' (haram), the minor term (al-hadd al-asghar) is 'nabidh', and the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) is 'wine' (khamr). He explains the logical relationships: 'wine' is more general than 'nabidh' and more specific than 'forbidden'. He then lays down two necessary conditions for the middle term (the evidence or dalil): First, it must not be more specific than the subject of judgment (the minor term), but must be equal or more general, so that it covers all instances of the subject. Second, it must not be more general than the judgment (the major term), but must be equal or more specific, so that the judgment is entailed by it. He notes that people are confused because 'what is signified' (al-madlul 'alayhi) can either mean the subject of judgment or the judgment itself. If it means the subject, then the evidence must not be more general than it; if it means the judgment, then the evidence must not be more specific than it. He insists that the subject must necessitate the evidence (so that the evidence covers all cases) and the evidence must necessitate the judgment (so that the conclusion follows in every case).

Key Terms
الدليل
الحد الأكبر
الحد الأصغر
الحد الأوسط
المحكوم عليه

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, for the evidence (middle term) to be valid, which of the following must hold?
A) It must be more general than the subject and more specific than the judgment.
B) It must be equal to both the subject and the judgment.
C) It must be more specific than both the subject and the judgment.
D) It must be more general than both the subject and the judgment.

Key Takeaway
For the middle term to prove a judgment on all instances of a subject, it must be co-extensive with or more general than the subject and co-extensive with or more specific than the judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the following qiyas argument: 'Every date wine (nabidh) is intoxicating; every intoxicant is forbidden; therefore every date wine is forbidden.' Identify the middle term and check whether it meets Ibn Taymiyya's two conditions. If not, how would you adjust the argument?
?
Consider a proposed middle term 'drink' in the same argument. Is 'drink' valid as an evidence? Apply the two conditions to explain why or why not.

Ibn Taymiyya's Quranic A Fortiori Reasoning (Qiyas al-Awla)
Ibn Taymiyya illustrates Quranic a fortiori reasoning with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply qiyas al-awla (a fortiori reasoning) as a Quranic method distinct from Aristotelian syllogism, using examples from the Quran.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-awla: what creation has, Allah deserves more; what creation lacks, Allah is more free.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya presents a core method of Quranic reasoning: qiyas al-awla, or a fortiori argument. Unlike the Greek syllogism that relies on middle terms and universal premises, this reasoning is based on the principle of priority: if a limited creature possesses a perfection, the Creator is infinitely more deserving of it. Ibn Taymiyya supports this with three Quranic examples from Surahs Ar-Rum, An-Nahl, and An-Najm. This lesson will help you understand this method and see how it refutes polytheism and affirms Allah's perfection.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle
Grasp the core idea: whatever perfection exists in creation, Allah is more deserving; whatever deficiency creatures are free from, Allah is even more free from it. This is the basis of qiyas al-awla.
2
Analyze the first Quranic example (Ar-Rum 28)
Allah asks polytheists: would you accept your own slaves as equal partners in wealth? Since they would not, by a fortiori, they should not accept partners for Allah. The reasoning is: if you don't accept equals among your subordinates, then Allah, who is infinitely above, certainly does not have equals.
This example shows the negative form: negation of deficiency in creatures implies stronger negation for Allah.
3
Analyze the second example (An-Nahl 57-60)
The polytheists assign daughters to Allah while preferring sons for themselves. This is a deficiency (associating offspring, especially females, with Allah). Since they themselves despise daughters, how can they attribute them to Allah? Allah is free from such deficiency.
This uses the positive form: perfection (having sons) is preferred by them, but they assign the opposite to Allah; thus Allah is above their assignment.
4
Analyze the third example (An-Najm 19-22)
They worship female idols (Al-Lat, Al-Uzza, Manat) while desiring males for themselves. This is an unjust division. By a fortiori, if they consider males superior, they should attribute the superior to Allah, not the inferior. Yet they do the opposite, showing their injustice.
This demonstrates the principle: what is good and perfect in creation should be attributed to Allah in a higher degree.
5
Apply the method to a new case
Use qiyas al-awla to affirm a divine attribute or negate a deficiency. For example: if human mercy is good and humans are merciful, then Allah is more merciful (affirming perfection). Similarly, if humans are free from lying, then Allah is more free from lying (negating deficiency).
This method is used in Kalam and Tafsir to derive theological truths.

والقرآن يستعمل الاستدلال بـ «الآيات»، ويستعمل أيضاً في إثبات الإلهية استعمال القرآن «قياس الأولى»، وهو أن ما ثبت لموجود مخلوق من كمال لا نقص فيه فالرب أحق به، وما نُزِّه عنه مخلوق من النقائص فالرب أحق بتنزيهه عنه، كما ذكر سبحانه وتعالى هذا في محاجته للمشركين الذين جعلوا له شركاء، فقال: ﴿ ضَرَبَ لَكُم مَّثَلًا مِنْ أَنفُسِكُمْ هَل لَكُم مِّن مَّا مَلَكَتْ أَيْمَانُكُم مِّن شُرَكَاءَ فِي مَا رَزَقْنَاكُمْ فَأَنْتُمْ فِيهِ سَوَاءٌ تَخَافُونَهُمْ كَخِيفَتِكُمْ أَنْفُسَكُمْ ﴾ [سورة الروم: ۲۸] . وقال تعالى: ﴿ وَيَجْعَلُونَ لِلَّهِ الْبَنَاتِ سُبْحَانَهُ وَلَهُمْ مَا يَشْتَهُونَ وَإِذَا بُشِّرَ أَحَدُهُمْ بِالْأُنْثَى ظَلَّ وَجْهُهُ مُسْوَدًا وَهُوَ كَظِيمٌ يَتَوَارَى مِنَ الْقَوْمِ مِنْ سُوءِ مَا بُشِّرَ بِهِ ۚ أَيُمْسِكُهُ عَلَى هُونٍ أَمْ يَدُسُّهُ فِي التُّرَابِ ۗ أَلَا سَاءَ مَا يَحْكُمُونَ ۗ لِلَّذِينَ لَا يُؤْمِنُونَ بِالْآخِرَةِ مَثَلُ السَّوْءِ ۖ وَلِلَّهِ الْمَثَلُ الْأَعْلَى ۚ وَهُوَ الْعَزِيزُ الْحَكِيمُ ﴾ [سورة النحل: ٥٧-٦٠] . وقال: ﴿ أَفَرَأَيْتُمُ اللَّاتَ وَالْعُزَّىٰ وَمَنَاةَ الثَّالِثَةَ الْأُخْرَىٰ  أَلَكُمُ الذَّكَرُ وَلَهُ الْأُنْثَىٰ  تِلْكَ إِذًا قِسْمَةٌ ضِيزَىٰ ﴾ [سورة النجم: ١٩-٢٢] . وكذلك في إثبات صفاته، وإثبات النبوة والمعاد؛ كما قد بسط في موضعه.
And the Quran uses reasoning by signs, and it also uses, in establishing divinity, the Quran's use of 'a fortiori reasoning' (qiyas al-awla), which is: whatever perfection with no deficiency is established for an existing creature, the Lord is more deserving of it; and whatever deficiency a creature is free from, the Lord is more deserving of being free from it. As the Exalted mentioned this in His argument against the polytheists who made partners for Him, saying: 'He sets forth for you an example from yourselves: do you have among those whom your right hands possess any partners in what We have provided for you so that you are equal therein? Do you fear them as you fear each other?' (Surah Ar-Rum: 28). And He said: 'And they assign to Allah daughters – exalted is He – and for themselves what they desire. And when one of them is informed of a female, his face becomes dark, and he is filled with grief. He hides from the people because of the evil of what he was informed of. Should he keep it in humiliation or bury it in the ground? Unquestionably, evil is what they judge. For those who do not believe in the Hereafter is an evil description, and for Allah is the highest description. And He is the Almighty, the Wise.' (Surah An-Nahl: 57-60). And He said: 'So have you considered Al-Lat and Al-Uzza and Manat, the third, the other? Is the male for you and for Him the female? That, then, is an unjust division.' (Surah An-Najm: 19-22). And likewise in establishing His attributes, and establishing prophethood and the resurrection; as has been expanded in its place.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage explains the Quranic method of 'a fortiori reasoning' (qiyas al-awla), which Ibn Taymiyya presents as a valid form of reasoning distinct from the logicians' syllogism. The principle is: if a created being possesses a perfection without deficiency, then the Creator is even more worthy of that perfection. Conversely, if a creature is free from a deficiency, the Creator is even more worthy of being free from it. Ibn Taymiyya supports this by citing three Quranic verses where Allah argues against polytheists using this method. In Surah Ar-Rum (28), Allah asks the polytheists whether they would accept their own slaves as equal partners in their wealth; since they would not, how can they accept partners for Allah? This is a fortiori: if you don't accept equals among your slaves, then surely Allah is above having equals. In Surah An-Nahl (57-60), Allah condemns the polytheists for assigning daughters to Him while preferring sons for themselves; this is a deficiency they attribute to Allah, while He is free from it. Finally, in Surah An-Najm (19-22), Allah rebukes them for assigning female idols to Him while they desire males for themselves; such a division is unjust, and Allah is above it. Thus, the Quran uses this reasoning to affirm Allah's perfection and negate deficiencies. Ibn Taymiyya notes that this method is also used for establishing attributes, prophethood, and resurrection, as detailed elsewhere.

Key Terms
قياس الأولى
المثل الأعلى

Checkpoint

B) If a creature has a perfection, Allah is more deserving of it; if a creature is free from a deficiency, Allah is more deserving of being free from it.
C) The middle term must be known through complete conception.
D) Syllogisms with two universal premises yield certainty.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-awla is a Quranic method of reasoning based on the principle that Allah is more deserving of any perfection and more free from any deficiency than any creature.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Syllogistic Necessity
Ibn Taymiyya argues that knowledge of God and prophets is through direct signs, not syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' claim that syllogisms are necessary for certain knowledge, and identify his alternative epistemic basis.

Core Idea
Knowledge of God and prophets comes through direct signs, not syllogistic deductions.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the foundational claim of the logicians that certain knowledge, especially of theological matters, depends on categorical syllogisms. In this passage, he asserts that such knowledge is obtained through divine signs and necessary knowledge, and that comprehensive syllogisms are no more certain than analogical reasoning. Let's examine his critique step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim being attacked
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states what the logicians assert: that establishing knowledge of the Creator and prophethood is dependent on syllogisms. He denies this dependence.
The logicians' claim is implicit: they consider syllogism necessary for certain knowledge.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's alternative sources of knowledge
He mentions two alternatives: direct signs (ayat) that indicate a specific thing with no sharing, and necessary knowledge (al-'ilm al-daruri) that does not require reflection (nazar). Identify these in the Arabic.
Direct signs are concrete, not abstract syllogisms.
3
Analyze his equation of comprehensive and analogical syllogisms
Ibn Taymiyya says what is obtained by comprehensive syllogism (qiyas shumuli) is of the same status as what is obtained by analogical syllogism (qiyas tamthil). This downgrades the certainty of comprehensive syllogism. Find this comparison in the text.
In standard logic, comprehensive syllogism is certain, analogy is not; Ibn Taymiyya rejects that distinction.
4
Assess the implications for theological epistemology
Consider what this means for proving God's existence or prophethood. Ibn Taymiyya argues that syllogism cannot give knowledge specific to God or the Prophet without additional knowledge. How does this affect the standard theological proofs?
This critique challenges the method of mutakallimun who rely on syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإثبات العلم بالصانع والنبوات ليس موقوفاً على شيء من الأقيسة، بل يعلم بالآيات الدالة على شيء معين لا شركة فيه، ويحصل بالعلم الضروري الذي لا يفتقر إلى نظر. وما يحصل منها بالقياس الشمولي فهو بمنزلة ما يحصل بقياس التمثيل. فهو أمر موقوف على كلي لا يحصل به العلم بما يختص به الرب وما يختص به الرسول إلا بانضمام علم آخر إليه. قياسهم الأنبياء بغيرهم والعلم بصدق المخبر المعين وإن لم يكن نبيا يعلم بأسباب متعددة غير «القياس»، ويعلم أيضاً بقياس التمثيل كما يعلم بقياس الشمول فكيف العلم بصدق النبي الصادق صلى الله عليه وسلم؟ وقد ذكرنا طرقاً من الطرق التي يعلم بها صدق الأنبياء في غير هذا الموضع.. والناس يعلمون الأمور الموجودة، وصفاتها وأحوالها من غير قياس شمولي، فضلاً عن أن يقال: لا بد هنا من مقدمتين - صغرى وكبرى. فالصغرى هي المشتملة على الحد الأصغر، والكبرى المشتملة على الحد الأكبر، والحد الأوسط متكرر فيها ـ خبر محمول في الصغرى ومبتدأ موضوع في الكبرى، كقولك «كل خمر حرام» . فيقال: إذا علم أن كل خمر حرام فقد يعلم ابتداء مفردات الخمر، وأنها شاملة لكل مسكر، بل يظن أنها متناولة لبعض المسكر كعصير العنب الني المشتد، ثم يعلم بعد ذلك شمولها لكل مسكر وهو إذا تجدد له هذا العلم فإنما يجدد له علمه بالعموم، وعلمه بالعموم إنما يعود بتصوره التام لمسمى الخمر. فإنه كان قبل ذلك لم يتصورها تصوراً جامعاً، بل تصوراً غير جامع. فلو حصل له هذا التصور الجامع لم يحتج إلى قياس. فقد تبين أن «القياس المفيد للتصديق يغني عنه التصور التام للحد الأوسط.
And establishing knowledge of the Creator and prophethood is not dependent on any of the syllogisms, rather it is known by signs indicative of a specific thing with no sharing, and it is obtained by necessary knowledge that does not require reflection. And what is obtained by comprehensive syllogism is of the same status as what is obtained by analogical syllogism. It is something dependent on a universal by which knowledge of what is specific to the Lord and what is specific to the Messenger is not obtained except by attaching another knowledge to it. Their syllogism [comparing] prophets to others: and knowledge of the truthfulness of a specific informer, even if not a prophet, is known by multiple causes other than syllogism, and is also known by analogical syllogism as it is known by comprehensive syllogism. So how about knowledge of the truthfulness of the truthful Prophet, peace be upon him? And we have mentioned ways from among the ways by which the truthfulness of prophets is known elsewhere. And people know existing things, their attributes and states, without comprehensive syllogism, let alone it being said: there must be two premises here - minor and major. The minor is the one containing the lesser term, and the major containing the greater term, and the middle term is repeated in them — a predicate in the minor and a subject in the major, as in your saying 'Every wine is forbidden'. So it is said: if it is known that every wine is forbidden, then he may know initially the individuals of wine, and that it encompasses every intoxicant, rather he thinks that it pertains to some intoxicants like raw strong grape juice, then he knows after that its encompassment of every intoxicant. And when this knowledge is renewed for him, it only renews his knowledge of generality, and his knowledge of generality only returns by his complete conception of the meaning of wine. For before that he had not conceived it with a comprehensive conception, but rather an incomplete conception. So if he had obtained this comprehensive conception, he would not have needed a syllogism. Thus it has become clear that 'the syllogism useful for assent is dispensed with by the complete conception of the middle term.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the logicians' claim that certain knowledge, especially of God and prophethood, depends on syllogistic reasoning (qiyas shumuli). He begins by asserting that such knowledge is not dependent on syllogisms at all; rather, it is obtained through direct signs (ayat) and necessary knowledge (al-'ilm al-daruri) that does not require theoretical reflection (nazar). He then draws a comparison: what is obtained by comprehensive syllogism (qiyas shumuli) is equivalent to what is obtained by analogical syllogism (qiyas tamthil). Both are dependent on a universal, but that universal alone does not yield knowledge of the specific attributes of God and the Prophet unless combined with other knowledge. Ibn Taymiyya then questions how we know the truthfulness of prophets, arguing that even knowledge of a non-prophetic informant can be had through multiple means besides syllogism, including analogy. He mentions that he has discussed proofs of prophethood elsewhere. He then attacks the logicians' requirement of two premises (minor and major) with a middle term. He gives an example: 'Every wine is forbidden.' He argues that if one knows that all wine is forbidden, they initially may not have a comprehensive conception of 'wine' — they might think it only covers some intoxicants like strong grape juice. Only when they acquire a complete conception of the meaning of 'wine' as including all intoxicants do they realize the universality. This complete conception itself renders the syllogism unnecessary. Therefore, the syllogism that yields assent (tasdiq) is made redundant by a complete conception (tasawwur tamm) of the middle term. The argument here is that the logicians' own machinery collapses: if you already have the complete conception, you don't need the syllogism; if you don't, the syllogism cannot give you certain knowledge.

Key Terms
القياس الشمولي
قياس التمثيل
التصور التام
الحد الأوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Direct signs and necessary knowledge
C) Analogical syllogisms only
D) Philosophical reflection

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya denies that syllogisms are necessary for theological knowledge, offering direct signs and necessary knowledge as sufficient alternatives.

Complete Conception as a Substitute for Syllogism
Ibn Taymiyya argues that complete conception of the middle term makes syllogism redundant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's argument that a complete conception of the middle term makes a syllogism redundant, and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
A complete conception eliminates the need for a syllogism.

In the second part of the passage, Ibn Taymiyya uses the example of 'wine is forbidden' to illustrate his point that if one has a complete conception of the term 'wine' as covering all intoxicants, the syllogism becomes unnecessary. This challenges the very structure of syllogistic reasoning. Let's analyze this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the standard syllogistic structure using the example
The logicians require two premises: a minor (e.g., 'Wine is an intoxicant') and a major ('Every intoxicant is forbidden'), with 'intoxicant' as the middle term. The conclusion is 'Wine is forbidden'. Identify this structure in the Arabic.
Ibn Taymiyya references this structure: middle term repeated as predicate in minor and subject in major.
2
Identify the incomplete vs complete conception
Ibn Taymiyya describes a person who initially has a limited conception of 'wine' (e.g., only raw grape juice) and later acquires a complete conception covering all intoxicants. Find this distinction in the text.
The incomplete conception thinks wine is only some intoxicants; complete conception includes all.
3
Trace Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning that complete conception replaces the syllogism
He argues that when one obtains the complete conception of the middle term ('intoxicant'), the knowledge of the universal premise ('Every intoxicant is forbidden') becomes self-evident from that conception itself. Hence, the syllogism is not needed. How does he justify this?
The syllogism is useful only when the conception is incomplete; with complete conception, the universality is directly known.
4
Evaluate whether this argument is logically sound
Consider whether complete conception of a term truly entails knowledge of universal propositions. Does knowing the meaning of 'intoxicant' logically imply knowing that all intoxicants are forbidden? Or does that require an additional premise? Assess the strength of Ibn Taymiyya's claim.
This touches on the relationship between conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq).

Positive and Negative Analogy in the Quran
Ibn Taymiyya explains positive and negative analogy from Quranic stories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between positive analogy (qiyas al-tard) and negative analogy (qiyas al-aks) as derived from Quranic stories and apply them to new cases.

Core Idea
The Quran commands both positive and negative analogy to derive lessons from past nations.

Ibn Taymiyya opens this passage by stating that the Quranic command to reflect (i'tibar) includes both positive and negative analogy. He provides a concrete example from the destruction of deniers of messengers to illustrate each type. Understanding this distinction is key to correctly applying analogical reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic account used as the original case (asl).
The passage refers to Allah destroying the deniers of messengers (al-mukadhdhibin li al-rusul) as the original situation with a known ruling: they were punished.
2
Extract positive analogy (qiyas al-tard).
Positive analogy applies the same ruling to a branch that shares the same property: whoever does as the deniers did will receive the same punishment. The goal is to establish the same ruling (nazir).
3
Extract negative analogy (qiyas al-aks).
Negative analogy establishes the opposite ruling for a branch that differs: those who follow the messengers will be spared. The goal is to establish the opposite (aks).
Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that negative analogy is the main purpose of reflecting on the punished.
4
Apply to a new scenario.
Choose a contemporary situation: e.g., today, those who reject prophetic guidance and persist in oppression can be analogized to the destroyed nations (positive analogy), while those who accept guidance can be analogized to the saved followers (negative analogy).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما أمر الله به من الاعتبار في كتابه يتناول قياس الطرد وقياس العكس. فإنه لما أهلك المكذبين للرسل بتكذيبهم كان من الاعتبار أن يعلم أن من فعل ما فعلوا أصابه وبقياس ما أصابهم، فيتقي تكذيب الرسل حذرا من العقوبة، وهذا قياس الطرد. ويعلم أن من لم يكذب الرسل بل اتبعهم لا يصيبه ما أصاب هؤلاء، وهذا قياس العكس وهو المقصود من الاعتبار بالمعذبين، فإن المقصود أن يثبت في الفرع عكس حكم الأصل لا نظيره. والاعتبار يكون بهذا وبهذا قال تعالى: {لَقَدْ كَانَ فِي قَصَصِهِمْ عِبْرَةٌ لِأُولِي الْأَلْبَابِ} [يوسف: 111]. وقال: {قَدْ كَانَ لَكُمْ آيَةٌ فِي فِئَتَيْنِ الْتَقَتَا فِئَةٌ تُقَاتِلُ فِي سَبِيلِ اللَّهِ وَأُخْرَى كَافِرَةٌ يَرَوْنَهُم مِّثْلَيْهِمْ رَأْيَ الْعَيْنِ وَاللَّهُ يُؤَيِّدُ بِنَصْرِهِ مَن يَشَاءُ إِنَّ فِي ذَلِكَ لَعِبْرَةً لِأُولِي الْأَبْصَارِ} [آل عمران: 13]. الميزان المنزل من الله هو القياس الصحيح. وقد قال سبحانه وتعالى: {اللَّهُ الَّذِي أَنزَلَ الْكِتَابَ بِالْحَقِّ وَالْمِيزَانَ} [الشورى: 17]. وقال: {لَقَدْ أَرْسَلْنَا رُسُلَنَا بِالْبَيِّنَاتِ وَأَنزَلْنَا مَعَهُمُ الْكِتَابَ وَالْمِيزَانَ لِيَقُومَ النَّاسُ بِالْقِسْطِ} [الحديد: 25]. و«الميزان» يفسره السلف بالعدل، ويفسره بعضهم بما يوزن به، وهما متلازمان. وقد أخبر أنه أنزل ذلك مع رسله كما أنزل معهم الكتاب ليقوم الناس بالقسط. فما يُعرف به تماثل المتماثلات من الصفات والمقادير هو من «الميزان»، وكذلك ما يُعرف به اختلاف المختلفات. فمعرفة أن هذه الدراهم أو غيرها من الأجسام الثقيلة بقدر هذه تعرف بموازينها. وكذلك معرفة أن هذا الكيل مثل هذا يعرف بميزانه، وهو المكاييل. وكذلك معرفة أن هذا الزمان مثل هذا الزمان يعرف بموازينه التي يقدر بها الأوقات، كما يُعرف به الظلال، وكما يجري من ماء ورمل وغير ذلك. وكذلك معرفة أن هذا بطول هذا يعرف بميزانه، وهو الذراع. فلا بد بين كل متماثلين من قدر مشترك كلي يعرف به أن أحدهما مثل الآخر.
And what Allah commanded of reflection (i'tibar) in His Book encompasses both positive analogy (qiyas al-tard) and negative analogy (qiyas al-aks). For when He destroyed the deniers of the messengers due to their denial, it was part of reflection to know that whoever does what they did will be afflicted by what afflicted them, so as to avoid denial of the messengers for fear of punishment—this is positive analogy. And to know that whoever did not deny the messengers but followed them will not be afflicted by what afflicted those, this is negative analogy, and it is the purpose of reflection through the punished, for the purpose is to establish in the branch the opposite of the ruling of the original, not its counterpart. And reflection is by both, as He said: {Indeed in their stories is a lesson for those of understanding} [Yusuf 12:111]. And He said: {There has already been for you a sign in the two armies that met, one fighting in the cause of Allah and another disbelieving, whom they saw twice as many as they were, with their own eyes, and Allah supports with His victory whom He wills. Indeed in that is a lesson for those of vision} [Al-Imran 3:13]. The scale (mizan) sent down from Allah is the correct analogy (qiyas). And He, exalted, said: {It is Allah who sent down the Book with truth and the scale} [Ash-Shura 42:17]. And He said: {We have sent Our messengers with clear proofs and sent down with them the Book and the scale that people may maintain justice} [Al-Hadid 57:25]. The 'mizan' is interpreted by the Salaf as justice, and by some as that with which one weighs, and they are concomitant. And He informed that He sent that down with His messengers just as He sent down the Book with them so that people may maintain justice. So that by which the similarity of similars is known—of attributes and measures—is part of the 'mizan', and likewise that by which the difference of differents is known. For the knowledge that these dirhams or other heavy bodies are equal to this in weight is known by their scales. Likewise, the knowledge that this measure is like this is known by its scale, which is the measuring vessels. Likewise, the knowledge that this time is like this time is known by its scales by which times are measured, like by shadows, or by the flow of water, sand, and the like. Likewise, the knowledge that this is of the length of this is known by its scale, which is the cubit. So between every two similars there must be a common universal measure (qadr mushtarak kulli) by which it is known that one is like the other.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is an argumentative expansion showing that the Quran commands reflection (i'tibar) which involves both positive and negative analogy. Ibn Taymiyya first explains the Quranic basis: when Allah destroyed the deniers, the lesson is positive—whoever does the same will suffer the same fate (qiyas al-tard); and negative—those who follow the messengers will be spared (qiyas al-aks). The latter is the main purpose of reflecting on the punished. He then cites Quranic verses (Yusuf 12:111 and Al-Imran 3:13) as evidence. Next, he connects this to the concept of 'mizan' (scale) which Allah sent down with the messengers. The mizan is interpreted as justice and as the actual scales. He explains that the mizan is the correct qiyas: it is the tool by which similars are known to be similar (via a common universal measure) and differents are known to be different. He gives concrete examples: weighing dirhams, measuring volumes, measuring time, and measuring length. The conclusion is that between any two similars there is a common universal aspect (qadr mushtarak kulli) that allows us to recognize their similarity. This universal aspect is the basis of all analogical reasoning, which ultimately derives from the divine mizan.

Key Terms
قياس الطرد
قياس العكس
اعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of reflecting (i'tibar) on the punished, according to Ibn Taymiyya?
A) To establish the same judgment on the branch as on the original (nazir).
B) To establish the opposite judgment on the branch (aks) so that followers of messengers are spared.
C) To merely remember historical events.
D) To derive a new ruling without analogy.

Key Takeaway
Quranic i'tibar requires both positive analogy (same ruling for same cause) and negative analogy (opposite ruling for different cause), with the latter being the ultimate lesson.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a Quranic story (e.g., the fate of Pharaoh) and distinguish the positive analogy for those who act like him and the negative analogy for those who submit to Allah.
?
Take a contemporary issue (e.g., a community that abandons prayer) and construct both a positive and negative analogy based on the Quranic principle of punishment for disobedience.

The Divine Scale (Mizan) as Correct Analogy
Ibn Taymiyya explains the divine scale (mizan) as the basis of correct analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of 'mizan' as the divine scale for correct analogy and identify the common universal measure (qadr mushtarak kulli) that justifies analogical inference.

Core Idea
The divine scale (mizan) is the principle of correct analogy grounded in a common universal measure.

Ibn Taymiyya expands the discussion by linking the Quranic concept of mizan (scale) to correct analogical reasoning. He argues that the mizan, sent down with the messengers, is the very principle that allows us to recognize similarity and difference between things. This leads to the foundational idea that between any two similars there must be a common universal measure (qadr mushtarak kulli).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic references to mizan.
Ibn Taymiyya quotes two verses: Ash-Shura 42:17 and Al-Hadid 57:25, where Allah mentions sending down the Book and the mizan. He interprets mizan as justice and as actual measuring instruments.
2
Understand mizan as the tool for knowing similarity and difference.
The mizan is what enables us to know that two things are similar or different in attributes and measures. This includes physical scales, measuring vessels, time measurement, and length measurement.
3
Extract the universal principle: the common universal measure.
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that between any two similars there must be a 'common universal measure' (qadr mushtarak kulli) that grounds the judgment of similarity. This is the basis of all correct analogy.
This concept parallels the 'illah in usul al-fiqh but is broader.
4
Apply the principle to a fiqh example.
Consider the analogy between wine (khamr) and date wine (nabidh): both share the 'illah of intoxication (iskan), which is the common universal measure that justifies the same ruling of prohibition.
